# Keeper League

# Chapter 1

Nate Griggs heard the house before he saw it. The driveway was a quarter-mile of gravel through pines, and somewhere up ahead Tommy Renaldi was laughing — that big, open-throated laugh that carried across practice fields and parking lots and apparently through woods in rural Minnesota, because Nate could hear it over his engine and the podcast ad for a mattress company he hadn't bothered to pause.

He parked behind Cliff's Audi. The Audi was new, or new-looking — Cliff's cars were always new-looking even when they weren't. Nate grabbed his duffel, his mic bag, and the thirty-rack of Hamm's that was his annual contribution to the weekend. Martin stocked the house with Lagavulin and craft beer, but Nate brought the Hamm's because he'd brought the Hamm's since 2001, and some bits were sacred even when the beer wasn't.

The lake house was an A-frame on Elbow Lake, pine-paneled, with a deck wrapping around the lake side. Martin's parents had bought it in the eighties, and Martin got it in the divorce five years back — Linda never contested the house because she'd always hated the drive from the Cities. Twenty-five Septembers they'd been coming here. The same nail held the draft board on the wall, the same third stair creaked, the same smell of wood smoke and lake damp lived in the pine. The league was the excuse. The house was the thing.

Nate let himself in and walked into noise. Tommy was in the great room with a beer in one hand and Doug Whitfield's shoulder in the other, telling a story about a cornerback who'd transferred to his school. Paul Decker was at the kitchen table with a scotch, overnight bag squared against the wall like it was awaiting inspection. Martin was at the counter unwrapping deli paper from a stack of sandwiches he'd driven to Dent to get, because Martin always drove to Dent, because Martin could not allow a person to be hungry in his house.

"Griggsy," Martin said, pointing with a mustard-smeared knife. "Thirty-rack goes in the fridge, your room's the same, towels are on the bed."

"I didn't even get to put my bag down."

"Towels on the bed, room's the same, thirty-rack in the fridge. Different order, same information."

Nate dropped the Hamm's on the counter. Martin was already rearranging the fridge to accommodate it, moving bottles with the focus of a man who believed refrigerator organization was a moral issue. He was six-one, stocky, with a ruddy face and a receding hairline he'd stopped fighting about three years back. He wore a flannel that cost a hundred and fifty dollars and looked like it cost twelve, which was Martin's whole approach to possessions — expensive things made to seem careless, because caring visibly about what you owned was a vulnerability Martin didn't permit himself. Big hands. Gap between his front teeth when he smiled, which he did now, reaching for the Hamm's.

"Tommy," Nate said, nodding toward the great room. "What inning are we in?"

"The cornerback. The one who can't catch." Paul sipped his scotch. "I think we're approaching the father who calls the superintendent."

"Classic."

Tommy hadn't noticed Nate yet, or had noticed and didn't care. The shoulder he was gripping belonged to Doug, who was standing in the kitchen doorway with the patience of someone who'd been caught in Tommy's gravity and knew the only exit was to wait it out. Doug had lost weight. More than the usual. His face was thinner, the hoodie hung wrong on him, and the bags under his eyes looked permanent. But he was leaning into the room rather than away from it — tilted toward the noise, smiling at Tommy's story with his whole face, the posture of a guy who'd driven three hours specifically to be near people.

Nate caught his eye and raised a hand. Doug waved back.

"You need anything?" Doug asked.

"A beer and a time machine."

"I can do one of those."

"He needs both," Tommy said, finally breaking off. He clapped Nate on the back hard enough to move him. "Griggs. Holy shit, that hat. You're still wearing that hat?"

The podcast-branded cap. GRIGGS ON THE GRID in white block letters. His listeners had collectively paid for a hundred of them, which meant he'd sold twenty-three.

"Content is content."

"It's a garbage hat is what it is. You look like you're sponsoring yourself." Tommy grinned — six-two and still built like something functional, crew cut graying at the temples, hands that were always gripping something. A beer, a shoulder, the back of his own neck. "I'm kidding. Kind of. Not really. It's a bad hat."

Cliff Haywood came downstairs in pressed chinos and a collared polo, like he'd confused the lake house with a client dinner. His handshake was firm enough that Nate felt it in his elbow. He had silver hair and a permanent tan and the kind of posture that made you aware of your own.

"Nate. Good drive up?"

"Cliff. Are those new pants?"

"These are pants."

"Those are pants the way your car out there is just-a-car."

Cliff smiled — the automatic one, the sales-floor one, the one that activated before his face decided anything — and moved to the counter. "Kess, tell me you went to Lindemann's. Tell me you got the turkey club."

"Two turkey clubs. You ate one and a half last year."

"I ate one. Paul had the other half."

"To be fair," Paul said, "that's accurate."

"You had one and a half." Martin tapped his temple. "I remember everything that happens in this kitchen."

Joel Beckman was the last one Nate registered, and only because Joel wasn't doing anything registrable. He was leaning against the wall near the hallway, arms crossed, beer untouched in his hand. He looked the same — lean, wiry, sharp features, those quick eyes that had made him the best poker player in the group for a decade — but something was wrong. His jaw was set. He smiled when someone looked at him, but the smile came late by half a second, like it was being translated from a language Joel wasn't currently speaking.

Nate couldn't place it. Joel was always a little wound up — the public defender in him always running cross-examination in the background — but this was different. This was a man who'd already had a fight nobody else had been invited to.

"Beck," Nate said. "You good?"

"Fine." Joel pushed off the wall. "You want a beer?"

He was at the fridge before Nate could answer, and the question was dead.

Martin caught Nate's eye across the kitchen. A small shrug. Don't push it.

The house filled up the way it always did over the next hour. Duffel bags dropped in rooms, the third stair creaked under everyone who went up, Martin circulated making sure drinks were topped off and food stayed accessible. Nate clipped his podcast mic to his phone and propped it against the chip bowl on the kitchen counter. He wasn't recording yet — just testing levels. He'd grab audio during the draft later: the arguments, the trash talk, the ambient sound of seven guys treating a fantasy football league like it mattered more than their mortgages. His listeners expected the draft-night episode. All hundred and twelve of them.

"You bringing that thing again?" Tommy said, eyeing the mic.

"Content is content."

"Last year you put my voice on there and some shithead kid in Ohio said my zone defense was garbage."

"He wasn't wrong."

"He was twelve, Griggs."

"Wise beyond his years."

Tommy threw a chip at him, and the evening had started.

---

The draft board went up at six. Martin hung it on the same nail in the great room wall he'd been using since 2004, when the nail had replaced the thumbtack that had replaced the masking tape that had replaced the year they just wrote picks on the back of a pizza box. The board was laminated whiteboard now, with columns for each team and rows for rounds. Martin had pre-printed the player rankings on a separate sheet and taped it beside the board. He'd brought color-coded markers.

He brought color-coded markers every year since the incident of 2016, when Tommy and Cliff had both used blue and the third round became unreadable and the argument about it lasted, intermittently, until April.

"Red's mine," Martin said, uncapping a marker and writing KESSLER at the top of the first column. "Pick order's in the bowl."

He set a ceramic bowl on the table with seven folded slips of paper inside. Martin had cut the paper, written the numbers, folded them identically, because Martin folded things identically. The draft order was random, but the randomness was Martin's — he produced the system, ran it, and never submitted it to review. Nobody had ever accused him of rigging it. Nobody had ever verified it either.

Tommy drew first pick and bellowed. Cliff drew second and said nothing, which meant he was doing math. Doug drew third and said, "Sure, that works." Paul drew fourth and nodded. Nate drew fifth and made a sound that Tommy later described on the podcast clip as "a walrus being told bad news." Joel drew sixth without comment. Seventh was Martin's by default — the last slot, the host's slot, the one that looked like sacrifice — and nobody pointed out that the man who controlled the paper and the bowl had landed there again.

Nate hit record on his phone.

"All right," Martin said. "Tommy's on the clock."

"Don't need to look." Tommy grabbed his green marker and wrote LAMAR JACKSON on the board. "Done. Let's go."

"You're keeping Lamar again," Joel said.

"Damn right I'm keeping Lamar."

"Four years in a row, Tommy."

"Because he's a goddamn cheat code. You just said he's great."

"I said he's great. I'm saying at a certain point you're paying a premium for loyalty over value."

"What premium? He's a top-five quarterback. What the fuck premium are you talking about?"

"Moving on," Martin said. "Cliff."

Cliff studied the ranking sheet for ten seconds. He uncapped his black marker, then turned to Paul. "Quick question. What are you thinking at four?"

"I'm thinking about my pick at four."

"Right, but directionally. Running back? Tight end? Because if you're going running back, I don't need to reach at two, and we can—"

"Cliff. I'm not telling you my strategy."

"I'm not asking your strategy. I'm asking your direction. Those are different things."

"The difference is the same information in a nicer package," Joel said.

"Here's what I'm proposing," Cliff said, ignoring Joel entirely. "An understanding. Not a trade. An understanding where, if you're going running back at four, I take the tight end at two, the running backs fall to you, everybody wins. It's an arrangement that benefits both—"

"A trade," said five people.

Cliff held up his hands and wrote CeeDee Lamb on the board.

The draft went the way it always went: slowly, then all at once, then Tommy calling someone an idiot. By the fourth round the table was louder than the house could hold. Paul was making a measured case for drafting a defense early — citing bye weeks, strength of schedule, the kind of structural argument that made his insurance-adjuster brain happy — and Tommy was telling him that it was the most chickenshit strategy he'd heard in twenty-five years of a league that had seen some profoundly chickenshit strategies, and Cliff was quietly negotiating a sixth-round swap with Doug that Doug clearly didn't follow but had agreed to because Cliff asked nicely and Doug agreed to things.

"You're getting fleeced," Joel told Doug.

"Probably," Doug said.

"You understand what you just gave up?"

"Not entirely."

"Then why'd you agree to it?"

Doug shrugged. "He was very persuasive."

Cliff pointed at Doug. "See? A reasonable man. This is what a reasonable man looks like."

"A reasonable man who doesn't know what he agreed to," Joel said. But he was almost smiling — the closest to relaxed he'd been since arriving.

Nate was narrating pieces of the draft in his broadcaster voice, the one that made Tommy groan and Martin shake his head and that Nate couldn't turn off because the bit was how he processed the evening. But under the bit was the real observation: these guys, this table, this board, this lake going copper through the window in the late September light. Twenty-five years of exactly this. He'd keep recording. At least one of them would say something usable.

Martin overrode a disputed pick in the seventh round. Joel had selected a running back Tommy claimed was injured, and the table split — Tommy pulling up stats on his phone, Joel citing the player's own Instagram post from that morning, Cliff proposing a compromise that was transparently another trade, Paul asking whether the league had ever actually established an injury-report protocol, and Doug saying he thought the guy played last week but wasn't sure.

Martin listened to all of it. Then he said, "The pick stands."

No vote. No debate. No appeal. Joel's pick stood because Martin said it did, and nobody pushed back because Martin's authority in this league was the kind that came from twenty-five years of driving to Dent for sandwiches and hanging the board on the nail and bringing the markers and opening his house every September and never once asking anyone to acknowledge any of it. You couldn't argue with that kind of authority. The argument would require naming the debt, and the debt was too large and too old and too deeply woven into the weekend to bear naming.

"Thank you," Joel said. Flat.

"Don't thank me. I just read the rules."

"You're the rules," Tommy said.

"Somebody's gotta be." Martin reached across the table and topped off Tommy's beer from the pitcher without being asked.

The draft ended at nine-thirty. Martin's team was, by consensus, terrible. Seventh pick every round, the scraps nobody else wanted. He circled his picks on the board in red marker and shrugged.

"Worked out last year."

"Last year you finished fourth," Cliff said.

"Top half."

"Fourth isn't top half. Fourth out of seven is exactly the middle."

"Middle's the new top half. Nate, tell your listeners."

"I'm sure all twelve of them agree," Nate said, and Paul actually laughed, which was generous.

The evening opened up after the draft. The board stayed on the wall — Martin's red circles already looking like the saddest column — and the talk shifted from picks to the upcoming season, from the season to coaching, from coaching to Tommy's cornerback story again, and from the cornerback to the kind of stories that twenty-five years of friendship accumulated like lake silt: the wedding where the DJ played "Dust in the Wind" during the first dance and nobody stopped him. The Super Bowl party where Doug fell asleep on the couch and nobody noticed until the fourth quarter. The year Cliff brought Megan to the draft and the group became so aggressively polite that Martin pulled Cliff aside and said he'd start talking about bowel health if she came back.

Tommy pulled a small ziplock from his jacket pocket and tossed it on the table in front of Martin. Four blue pills rattled inside the plastic.

"Happy birthday, old man."

"My birthday was in June."

"Happy belated. Happy early. Happy whatever you want. Open it."

Martin held the bag up. "This is Viagra."

"It's a wellness product."

"It's four Viagra, Tommy."

"One for each night. Plus a spare, in case you meet a nice lady at the Dent gas station."

"I'm not meeting a lady at the gas station."

"You don't know that. I'm saying be prepared. Denise's friend's husband takes 'em. Says they changed his goddamn life."

"His life needed changing. Mine doesn't."

"You're forty-seven and you've been single for five years, Kess. Take the pills. Stick 'em in your medicine cabinet. Use 'em, don't use 'em, I don't give a shit. It's a gag gift."

"Some gag."

"Next year I'll get you a prostate exam. At least this year's gift is fun."

The table was laughing — Paul with his hand over his mouth, Cliff tilting back in his chair, Nate already building the podcast bit in his head, Doug grinning from his corner of the table. Joel had walked out to the deck five minutes earlier and hadn't come back.

Martin scooped the pills back into the bag and put it in his shirt pocket. "I'm keeping these as evidence."

"Evidence of what?"

"Evidence that you're an asshole."

"That's never been in dispute, buddy."

Martin was already pouring the next round of Lagavulin — the good stuff, the bottle he replaced every year because it never survived the weekend. The hot tub was humming on the deck. Nate could hear its pump through the glass door like a low mechanical purr under everything else.

He and Doug went out to smoke. Doug had started again since the divorce, and Nate smoked when Doug smoked, which was the closest either of them got to a buddy system.

"Steph still not talking to you?" Nate asked.

"She texts about the furniture. That counts, right?"

"Barely."

"Yeah." Doug exhaled into the dark. The lake was flat and black beyond the deck railing, and a loon called from somewhere past the dock. "I'm glad I came up."

"You say that every year."

"I mean it every year." He looked at Nate with an expression that was too open for the joke Nate had been building toward. "I needed this one. You know?"

Nate let the joke go. "Yeah, man. I know."

They stood there for a minute, smoking, not saying anything, listening to Tommy's voice through the window arguing about something that didn't matter with someone who didn't care.

Martin had gone to the far end of the deck — Nate could see him through the glass door, standing at the railing with Tommy, the two of them in what looked like a private conversation. Martin's hand rested on Tommy's shoulder for a moment. Tommy nodded at something. It lasted thirty seconds, maybe less. Martin came back inside first. Tommy followed a beat later, running the back of his hand across his face in a way that could've been the lake air or could've been something else entirely. Nobody mentioned it.

In the kitchen, Cliff had cornered Paul. Not about the draft. "I've been trying to get Martin to close out our position on the Burnsville thing for two years. He keeps deferring."

Paul sipped his scotch and offered nothing.

"I'm sure he's good for it. I'm just saying it'd be nice to see some movement. He's generous as hell when it's his terms — the sandwiches, the house, all of this." Cliff gestured at the room. "Try to collect on the other end and the guy's a wall."

"Cliff." Paul set his glass down. "It's draft night."

"I know it's draft night."

"Then let it be draft night."

"Sure. Yeah, absolutely." Cliff looked at his drink, then back at Paul, and produced the same automatic smile from the afternoon. "Great night."

The evening got late. Nate lost track of the time around eleven, three scotches past good judgment and one past the ability to notice. He was in the basement setting up his cot when he heard raised voices through the floor. One was Martin's. The other was Joel's. They weren't the kind of raised voices that came from arguing about fantasy football picks. They came from somewhere older and meaner and the tone cut through thirty years of un-insulated floorboards without any trouble at all.

He went upstairs. Tommy was in the hallway, leaning against the wall outside Martin's study.

"What's going on?"

"Don't know. Don't want to know."

"Is that Joel?"

"I said I don't want to know, Griggs."

The voices were coming from behind the study door. Nate couldn't make out words — just tone. Martin's was low and controlled. Joel's was climbing toward a register Nate had never heard from him in twenty-five years.

Paul appeared at the top of the stairs. "What the hell is that?"

"Joel and Martin," Tommy said. "Been going at it for ten minutes."

The study door opened. Joel came out like he'd been launched — face white, jaw locked, eyes registering the three men in the hallway without any change in expression whatsoever.

"What was that about?" Paul asked.

"Nothing."

"Joel. That wasn't—"

"I said it's handled." Joel went straight for the front door and out onto the porch. The screen door slapped shut behind him.

Paul looked into the study. The door was still open. Martin was standing behind his desk with both hands flat on the surface, breathing through his nose.

"Martin?"

"Go to bed, Paul."

"You need anything?"

"I need you to go to bed."

Paul went. Tommy went. Nate went back down to the basement. The house settled into the specific quiet of men who had decided to stop talking to each other. The refrigerator cycled on somewhere above him. A loon called from the lake.

Nate lay on his cot and stared at the ceiling and thought about the Viagra bit and whether he'd caught Martin saying "evidence that you're an asshole" on mic and whether Joel was okay and whether it was any of his business.

He fell asleep with the recorder still running on his phone, the mic propped against the chip bowl in the kitchen three floors up, picking up nothing but the refrigerator and the click of the front door opening and closing at twelve-fifteen.

---

Friday was the lake day. They took Martin's pontoon out, fished badly, drank adequately, came back sunburned and quiet the way a full day of water and sun makes people quiet. Joel and Martin didn't speak to each other — or didn't speak where anyone else could hear — and nobody tested the silence. Doug sat in the back of the boat and trailed his hand in the water and looked more at peace than Nate had seen him in a year.

The evening was supposed to be the easy one. The draft was done, the league had structure, and the drinking could be pointless instead of competitive. Martin fired up the hot tub at eight. By nine-thirty all seven of them were in it, which was two bodies past the tub's comfortable capacity and exactly the right number for the kind of conversation that only happens when middle-aged men are mostly submerged and can't pretend to have dignity.

"I'm touching someone's foot," Cliff said.

"That's my foot," Doug said.

"Your foot is on my thigh."

"There isn't room."

"Kess, you said this seats six."

"It seats six people who aren't Tommy."

"I'm a large man," Tommy said.

"You're a large man in a medium tub, and you're displacing everybody else's personal space."

"You want me to get out?"

"Don't get out," Martin said. "You get out and the water level shifts and it gets worse."

Tommy shifted anyway. Water slopped over the edge onto the deck. Nate, wedged between Paul and the jet controls, felt something give in his lower back and decided to address it never.

"This is disgusting," Joel said. He was perched on the rim with only his calves in the water. "This is seven guys making soup."

"Don't say soup," Paul said.

"Man soup. Guy broth. Whatever this is."

"It's a bonding experience," Tommy said.

"It's a health violation."

"Twenty-five years and he's still a snob about the tub," Tommy announced to no one in particular. "Twenty-five goddamn years."

"I'm not a snob. I'm hygienic."

"You're dry. Your feet are wet and you're calling that participation. That's bullshit, Beck. Get in the soup."

Joel lowered himself in with the deliberation of a man who did not want to be there and had decided to be there anyway. Water rose. Cliff swore. Doug pulled his legs in. Martin, at the far end with his arms spread along the rim, watched the whole operation with the quiet satisfaction of a host whose party was proceeding on schedule.

"Now we're in business," Tommy said.

"Now we're in a petri dish," Joel said, but he settled in.

They told stories. The same ones, mostly, refined by twenty-five annual retellings into something between history and myth. The year Paul got food poisoning and spent the entire draft in the bathroom, calling his picks through the closed door. The year Nate accidentally included thirty seconds of Tommy screaming about a referee in a podcast episode and Tommy's AD called him on Monday morning. The year someone — nobody could agree who — pissed off the end of the dock at three in the morning and the neighbor across the lake turned on a spotlight.

"That was Cliff," Tommy said.

"That was absolutely not me."

"It was Cliff. I saw the whole thing. You were standing on the dock with your dick out talking to the moon."

"I wasn't on the dock. I was asleep."

"You were having a full conversation with the moon about interest rates. I remember because it was the most Cliff Haywood thing I've ever witnessed in my life."

"None of that happened."

"Some of it happened," Doug said quietly, and the tub erupted.

Doug almost never contributed stories. But when the laughter hit, his whole body went into it — shoulders shaking, sinking deeper into the water, eyes squeezed shut. He was sunburned across the nose, hair plastered to his forehead, and he looked for the first time that weekend like someone who had no problems at all.

"You know what I love about this?" Doug said.

"The foot contact," Cliff said.

"Shut up. I love that this hasn't changed. Twenty-five years and this part is still the same." His sentence faltered. He moved his hand across the surface of the water, a gesture that tried to hold something the words couldn't. "It's good. This is good."

Nobody argued with that.

They stayed in the tub until the sky was full dark and the water had started to cool and moths were throwing themselves against the porch light. Martin climbed out first, wrapped a towel around his waist, and went inside. The loons were going. Somewhere across the lake a motorboat was coming in late, its running light a white pinprick moving slow against the trees.

"I'm done," Martin called from the doorway half an hour later. He was in sweats and a thermal, last scotch of the night in hand. "Lock up when you're done. Not you, Beckman — you don't know where anything locks."

"I know where the door is."

"That's a start. Good night."

He went upstairs. The third step creaked under him like it always did.

The rest of them stayed up another hour. Tommy and Cliff had a conversation on the deck that Nate caught the edge of — Cliff leaning in with a beer, that particular pitch in his voice that meant he was making an ask, Tommy shaking his head and going inside. Joel sat in an Adirondack chair staring at the lake, his beer going warm in his hand. Nate thought about asking him what the fight with Martin had been about and decided against it. Whatever it was lived between Joel's silence and Martin's closed study door, and it wasn't Nate's.

Paul was the last one to head up. He stood in the kitchen rinsing glasses, the way he always did — the clean-up man, the one who made sure Martin's house looked the same in the morning as it had in the evening.

"You good down there?" he asked Nate.

"Fine. Gonna stay up a bit."

"Don't record me sleeping."

"No promises."

Paul went upstairs. The house emptied out.

Nate went down to the basement, brushed his teeth, and lay on his cot. He couldn't sleep. The day had been too long, or the scotch was too much, or his brain was doing what it always did after a full day of performing — running the highlight reel, cutting the bits, deciding what was content and what stayed in the room. The Viagra was a cold open. The hot tub could be a full segment. Martin's "evidence that you're an asshole" was a closer, if Nate had caught it on mic, which he probably hadn't.

He checked his phone. One-thirty. He should sleep.

He didn't sleep.

At quarter to two he decided he wanted one more thing from the weekend. The bet. He and Martin had been making the same wager since the second season of the league: five bucks on who'd finish last. It was a nothing bet, a ceremony. They shook on it every year in Martin's kitchen at some ungodly hour while the house slept.

Nate went upstairs. Basement stairs, main stairs, the third step that creaked under his weight. The upper hallway was dark. Martin's door was at the end. Nate knocked twice, soft.

Nothing.

He knocked again. "Kess. You up?"

The door wasn't latched. It swung inward a half-inch from the knock. Nate pushed it open.

The room was dark. Martin was in bed on his back, and the room was wrong.

Nate couldn't have said what told him — not immediately, not in words he could've put on a podcast or in a conversation or anywhere else. It was the hand. Martin's left hand was lying on top of the blanket, palm up, fingers loosely curled but not gripping anything, not twitching, not doing any of the small involuntary things a sleeping hand does. The mouth was open. The chest was still.

Nate stepped closer. The scotch smell hit first — scotch and sweat and something underneath both that he couldn't name but that his body understood before his brain caught up, because his hands went cold and his legs stopped wanting to hold him.

"Martin."

He touched Martin's arm. It was warm. Warm and absolutely still.

"Martin. Hey. Martin."

Nate stood there. He didn't know for how long. The Lagavulin bottle was on the nightstand with a finger left in it, and the glass beside it had a dried ring where the scotch had evaporated, and the pine smell of the room was the same smell it had always had for twenty-five years, and the loons were going on the lake, and the refrigerator was humming somewhere below him, and Martin's hand was open and his chest was still and Nate was not narrating this. He was not building the bit. He was not doing any of the things he always did when the world handed him something he didn't know how to hold. He was standing in a dark room next to his friend and his friend was not breathing and the room was wrong.

He sat on the edge of the bed. He picked up Martin's hand and held it for a moment. It was heavy in a way a living hand is not. He set it back on the blanket.

He got up. He left the room. He walked down the hall to Paul's door and knocked until it opened. Paul stood there in a T-shirt and boxers, blinking, and Nate said, "Martin," and that was all he could get out — the name, everything the name meant — and Paul's face changed, and they went back down the hallway together.

The weekend was over.


# Chapter 2

Nate was standing in the doorway of the guest room in his boxers and a podcast-branded T-shirt, and Paul knew before Nate said anything because Nate wasn't performing. Nate was always performing — at the draft table, at the grill, on the phone to his eleven listeners, in his sleep for all Paul knew. But the man in the doorway was small and still and his mouth was working without sound, and that was how Paul Decker learned that his best friend was dead.

"Something's wrong with Martin." Nate's voice came out at a register Paul had never heard from him — flat, airless, like the words had been squeezed through something too narrow. "I went up to — I was gonna ask him about the side bet, and he's — Paul, I think he's —"

Paul was already moving. Bare feet on the cold hallway floor, the bathroom nightlight throwing that sick yellow glow Martin always left on for guests who drank too much and needed a beacon to piss by. Martin's bedroom door was open at the end of the hall. Nate must have left it open. The light from the nightstand lamp spilled into the hallway in a thin stripe.

Martin was in bed. On his back, one arm hanging off the mattress, the other folded across his chest like he'd arranged himself for a photograph. The sheets were pulled up to his waist. His mouth was open. His eyes were closed — Paul would be grateful for that later, grateful he didn't have to see whatever the eyes looked like, but right now he wasn't thinking about later. He was looking at the color of Martin's face, which was wrong. Martin was always ruddy. Flushed from lake sun, from scotch, from yelling about someone's draft pick, from the blood pressure he joked about and never treated. Now his skin was a grayish yellow that Paul had never seen on a living person, and the seeing of it was what told his body before his brain caught up.

He put his hand on Martin's neck and felt nothing. Warm skin, slack muscle, no pulse. The warmth was the cruelest part — it meant recent, it meant close, it meant that somewhere in the last hour Martin Kessler's heart had stopped and the heat was still leaving his body and Paul had been asleep twenty feet down the hall.

"Call 911," Paul said.

"I don't have — my phone's downstairs, I left it on the —"

"Go get it. Go."

Nate didn't move. His face was doing something Paul had never seen from him, a kind of involuntary disassembly, the muscles pulling in directions that had nothing to do with comedy or performance or any expression Nate had practiced. His eyes were wet and he was gripping the doorframe with one hand like it was the only thing keeping him upright.

"Nate. Phone. Now."

Nate turned and went. Paul heard him on the stairs — heard the third step creak, the one everyone knew to skip, because Nate was not thinking about stairs right now — and then Paul was alone with Martin.

He started CPR because you start CPR. That was what you did. You didn't check for a pulse a second time or stand there making calculations or weigh the odds like a man who reviewed actuarial tables for a living. You put your hands on your best friend's chest and you pushed. Paul's training was a weekend course twelve years ago, mandated by his insurance company for field adjusters who might encounter a medical emergency at a claim site, and what he remembered was: thirty compressions, two breaths, don't stop, don't second-guess the depth, and the practice mannequin had smelled like new rubber sneakers and the instructor had said "you can't make it worse" with the cheerful certainty of a man who had never done this on someone he loved.

He laced his fingers and set the heel of his right hand on Martin's sternum and pushed. The first compression produced a sound that was not the cinematic crack of breaking ribs but a soft wet yielding, a thud that Paul felt travel through his palms and up his forearms and into a place behind his eyes where it would live for months. He pushed again. Again. The counting was supposed to help — one, two, three, four — but what the counting actually did was give his brain something to hold while the rest of him processed the fact that the body moving under his hands was the person who had driven four hundred miles to pick him up from a motel in Fargo when Paul's rental car died during his father's funeral trip. Who had shown up at Paul's house the night after Anne's miscarriage with a bottle of Maker's Mark and a roll of trash bags and had cleaned the kitchen without being asked and without saying a single thing about why the kitchen needed cleaning. Who had been Paul's best man and given a toast that made Paul's mother cry, and Paul's mother did not cry, and Martin had known exactly which story would do it — the one about Paul falling asleep in the library junior year and Martin covering him with his own coat and leaving a note that said "you looked cold, you looked stupid, I'll be at the bar."

Fifteen, sixteen, seventeen. Paul tilted Martin's head back and sealed his mouth over Martin's and breathed. The air went in and Martin's chest rose and the air came back tasting like Lagavulin and something underneath the Lagavulin that was metallic and intimate and terrible. He breathed again. He went back to compressions. Twenty-five years of friendship being expressed as a rhythm Paul could not maintain because his arms were already burning and his breath was already ragged and the counting was already losing itself in the gap between what he was doing and what it was accomplishing.

Because he knew. Paul Decker, who had reviewed eleven wrongful death claims in his career, who could cite out-of-hospital cardiac arrest survival rates to two decimal places, who had once described to a jury the difference between a body found within the resuscitation window and a body found after it — Paul knew that what he was doing was not medicine. It was not even hope. It was community theater: a forty-seven-year-old man in flannel pajama pants performing chest compressions on another forty-seven-year-old man whose heart had stopped long enough ago that the warmth was leaving and no amount of rhythmic pressure was going to reverse whatever had already been decided.

He pushed anyway. He pushed until his shoulders felt like they were separating and the count dissolved into meaningless numbers and the sound of his own breathing was louder than anything the body was producing. He pushed until the effort became its own reason — not to save Martin but to not be the person who stopped trying.

Then he stopped.

He sat back on his heels and looked at Martin's face, which had not changed, which would never change again, and he said, "Fuck."

Quiet. Almost conversational. Not addressed to Martin or to God or to the obscenity of dying alone in bed at forty-seven with a half-finished scotch on the nightstand and your friends sleeping down the hall. Addressed to the room. To the lamp and the sheets and the gap between Martin's front teeth that Paul could see because Martin's mouth was still open and there was nothing dignified about death, nothing composed, nothing like the version Paul had been selling to insurance claimants for twenty years when he described their covered family member's passing.

He leaned forward and put his forehead on the edge of the mattress. The sheets smelled like Martin's laundry detergent — the blue bottle, the cheap one, the same brand Martin had used since their freshman dorm at the University of Minnesota. Paul had seen that blue bottle under every sink Martin had ever owned. Apartments, the first house, Linda's house, this lake house. A stupid detail, a nothing detail, and Paul pressed his face into it and his eyes burned and his throat closed and his nose ran onto the cotton, and he stayed there with his forehead on the bed and his hands on the mattress three inches from his dead best friend and he did not move for what felt like a very long time.

---

Nate came back with his phone and Tommy and Joel behind him, all of them half-dressed and blinking in the hallway light. Tommy in coaching shorts and nothing else, the scar on his left forearm visible, his crew cut flattened on one side from the pillow. Joel in boxers and a University of Minnesota T-shirt that had to be twenty years old, its collar stretched and the maroon faded to something closer to brown. They filled the doorway and looked past Paul to the bed, and Paul, who was standing now, who had wiped his face with the back of his hand and straightened up because that was what you did, shook his head.

"Jesus Christ," Tommy said. He said it the way he said everything — loud, definitive — but his face was wrong for the volume. His face was the face of a man who had just been told something he already feared.

"Is he —" Joel started.

"Yeah."

Tommy stepped into the room. He put his hand on Martin's chest, his enormous hand flat on the sternum Paul had just been compressing, and held it there like he was checking for himself, like the determination required a second opinion from a man who coached high school football for a living. He held it there and then he pulled it back and looked at his own hand and the fingers curled slightly and Paul watched something move behind Tommy's eyes that was not grief. Not yet. It was closer to recognition, like a man hearing a sound he'd been listening for.

"Did you call?" Paul asked Nate.

"I tried — there's no signal in the basement. I came back up and got these guys first."

"There's signal on the deck," Paul said. "Or by the road."

Nobody moved toward the deck.

Doug appeared in the hallway behind them, gaunt and blinking, his sweatshirt on inside out, the tag sticking up from his collar like a small white flag. He didn't ask. He looked past the bodies in the doorway at the bed and at their faces and back at the bed, and his mouth made a small shape that didn't become a word. He stepped back against the wall to make himself smaller, which was what Doug did when rooms got too full.

Cliff was last. He arrived in pressed pajama pants and a T-shirt that still had a crease from the fold — because Cliff packed his T-shirts in tissue paper, because Cliff was Cliff — and he stood at the back of the group and looked at Martin's body on the bed and then his eyes moved to the nightstand and the scotch glass and the room's layout, and Paul recognized the expression. He'd seen it on adjusters and attorneys and the kind of people who walked into a situation and immediately started sorting it into categories of liability. Cliff was calculating. Someone was dead in the room and Cliff Haywood was already doing math.

"We need to call," Paul said. He heard the word again — again — and the repetition made it sound less like a plan and more like something a man says to a room that isn't listening.

"He's gone, Deck." Tommy's coaching voice, the one for halftime when a kid was hurt and the injury was real and the ambulance was already on its way and what mattered was keeping everyone else from falling apart. Flat, declarative, controlled. "You can see he's gone. What's the ambulance gonna do?"

"That's not the point. We're supposed to —"

"He's not wrong," Joel said. Joel's voice had the careful, mortared quality it got when the legal architecture engaged, each word placed and weighed before delivery. "We call now, we get an ambulance in thirty minutes that can't help him and a county deputy in forty who takes statements until dawn. We call at seven, we get the same deputy during business hours and the result for Martin is identical." A pause. "The result for Martin is identical either way, Paul."

"Except that a five-hour delay is going to look like something."

"It's going to look like six men who were drinking were woken up at two in the morning and handled it badly. Which is true."

The room was quiet. The refrigerator hummed downstairs in its forty-minute cycle. A loon called from the lake — that sliding, deranged sound that tourists recorded on their phones and locals tuned out, a bird screaming into the dark for no reason a human could use.

Paul looked around the room. At Tommy, who was staring at the nightstand now with an intensity that didn't match the moment. At Joel, whose legal reasoning was sound and whose reasons for wanting it sound were his own. At Cliff, who had stopped calculating and was now looking at the doorway like he was measuring how long it would take to get through it. At Doug, pressed against the hallway wall, arms crossed over the inside-out sweatshirt, saying nothing. At Nate, who was holding his phone with both hands like it was something that had been taken away from him and given back wrong.

Paul wanted to argue. He wanted to pick up the phone and call himself and let the county handle it. But the truth was that Joel was making sense, or making something that sounded enough like sense at 2 AM when you were standing in your dead friend's bedroom with scotch still metabolizing in your blood and your CPR sweat drying on your best friend's chest. The truth was also that Paul did not want to call. Not yet. Not because he didn't want to do the right thing but because he had spent three minutes pressing his hands into Martin's sternum and breathing into Martin's mouth and his saliva was on Martin's lips and his tears were on Martin's sheets and his DNA was on Martin's skin, and some small irrational part of his insurance-trained brain was filing a claim it did not want to file: Paul Decker, forty-seven, present at scene. Physical contact with deceased. Bodily fluids transferred. Close personal relationship, twenty-five years. Only witness to time of discovery: Nate Griggs, who left the room before CPR commenced.

It wasn't rational. He knew it wasn't rational. He was an insurance adjuster, not a suspect, and CPR residue on a dead man's body was evidence of exactly one thing, which was that someone had tried to save a life. He could have written the risk assessment in his sleep. He could have explained it to a jury in four sentences.

But standing in that room, looking at Martin's face — which was settling now into something that was not peaceful or pained but simply vacated, like a house after the tenants have gone and the lights are off but you can still see where the furniture was — standing there, Paul could not stop the calculation. The scene looked wrong. The body was not where it had been when he walked in. One arm was at an angle Martin hadn't put it in. The sheets were twisted. The pillow was half off the bed. CPR did that, of course CPR did that, any responding paramedic would recognize the displacement pattern and note it and move on. But Paul's brain wouldn't let it go. The scene looked like a man whose sleep was interrupted. By someone. By Paul.

"OK," Paul said. "Morning."

The room held its shape for another minute — six men and a dead man in a bedroom at 2 AM, none of them knowing what to do with their hands. Then Doug said, quietly, that he'd make coffee, and the word "coffee" was enough to break whatever was keeping them there, because it was specific and practical and had nothing to do with the body on the bed. The group moved to the kitchen in the useless, shuffling procession of people who have been told there is nothing to do and have not yet accepted it.

Paul stayed. He told them he wanted a minute. They gave it to him.

The minute lasted twenty-five.

What Paul did in Martin's bedroom in those twenty-five minutes was the most irrational thing he had ever done, and he did it with the methodical calm of a man filling out a claims form. He repositioned the body. He moved Martin's arm back across his chest, or close to where it had been — he couldn't remember exactly and the trying to remember was making him sweat in a way that had nothing to do with the room's temperature, a cold sweat that started at his hairline and ran down behind his ears. He smoothed the sheets. He straightened the top sheet across Martin's waist the way he thought Martin had had it, but the truth was that Paul had barely looked at the sheets when he'd started compressions and now he was guessing, and the guessing was worse than not knowing because each adjustment felt like it was adding distance from whatever the original configuration had been.

He put the pillow back. He set the scotch glass upright on the nightstand — it had tipped during compressions, not quite spilling, but it was at an angle that said someone had bumped the nightstand and Paul didn't want the nightstand to say that. He looked at Martin's face, at the moisture around the mouth where Paul's breath and Paul's saliva had been, and he wiped it with the corner of the top sheet. That was the worst thing he did. Worse than the repositioning, worse than the sheets, because wiping a dead man's mouth with a bedsheet was something from a crime show, something a person did when they had done something to the mouth, and Paul had done something to the mouth — he had breathed into it, he had tried to restart the life behind it — but the wiping transformed an act of love into an act of concealment and Paul understood this even as he was doing it and he did it anyway.

Then he wiped down the surfaces he'd touched. The hem of his flannel shirt, wrapped around his index finger, moving across the nightstand top, the bedframe where he'd gripped it for leverage during compressions, the doorknob on both sides. Like something from a network procedural he would have mocked from his couch at home, Anne beside him, a bag of pretzels between them, Paul saying, "Nobody actually does that. Nobody wipes down a scene when they haven't committed a crime."

He was halfway to the hall bathroom to wash his hands before the absurdity of it hit him — he was going to wash off evidence of a thing he didn't do, evidence that proved he'd tried to help, in order to make it look like he hadn't been there, even though five people had watched him walk into the room and stay behind. The logic was broken. The risk assessment, if you could call it that, was the worst he'd ever conducted. But the water was running and his hands were under it and the soap was Martin's soap — Ivory, the bar, the same soap Martin had used at every apartment, every house, because Martin was a man of habits so specific and unchanging that the constancy of his soap had become one of the small fixed facts of Paul's life, and now the soap was making Paul's hands smell like Martin's hands and Martin's hands would never smell like anything again.

He dried his hands on the towel. He turned the bathroom light off. He went downstairs to the kitchen, where Doug had made coffee that was strong enough to strip varnish.

The kitchen was lit by the fluorescent tube over the sink that nobody liked and nobody replaced because it was Martin's kitchen and Martin got to decide what was harsh. Paul sat at the table with both hands around a mug of Doug's punishing coffee and looked through the sliding glass door at the deck.

Tommy was outside, pacing. Even from behind glass, even in the dark, Tommy was unmistakable — the size, the athletic restlessness, the way his hands gripped the back of his own neck and then dropped and then gripped again, a man whose body could not process whatever his brain was running and whose only solution was locomotion. Back and forth across the deck. Back and forth.

Nate was in the great room, visible through the kitchen doorway, sitting on the couch with his phone on his lap and his hands on his knees. Not looking at the phone. Not performing. Just sitting there.

Joel had taken the chair by the dead fireplace and was already asleep, or pretending to be — his legs stretched out, his chin on his chest, his breathing even. Joel's ability to shut down under pressure was either a lawyer's skill or a dissociative gift, and Paul had never been able to tell which.

Cliff was somewhere. Paul could hear him in the hallway for a moment, and then he couldn't.

Doug sat across from Paul with his own mug of terrible coffee, and they looked at each other over the table, and neither of them said anything for a long time. Doug's face was the same face it had been all weekend — hollowed, the bags under his eyes almost purple in the kitchen light, the weight loss that nobody was asking about because the asking would require an answer and the answer might be worse than the not-knowing. Doug looked like a man who hadn't slept well in months, and the not-sleeping had nothing to do with tonight.

"You OK?" Paul asked, because that was the question you asked in kitchens at 3 AM even when the answer was obvious and the asking was performance.

Doug looked at him. "No." A beat. "Are you?"

"No."

"OK."

That was the whole conversation, and Paul would remember it later as one of the most honest exchanges of the weekend — two men admitting the obvious and then sitting with the admission in silence, drinking bad coffee in a dead man's kitchen while the refrigerator hummed and the lake lapped at the shore fifty yards away and a loon called for the second time, that territorial wail, a sound that meant nothing except I am here and this is mine and I am afraid.

The coffee maker gurgled through its cycle. Paul sat there and he listened.

That was when it started.

The first sound was a creak from the stairs. Not the third step — everyone knew about the third step, everyone had been skipping the third step for years, it was one of the house's shared facts, as known and automatic as which burner ran hot on the stove and which bathroom door you had to lift to latch. This was the fourth or fifth step, higher up, and the creak had weight to it — someone heavy, someone who'd remembered the third step and skipped it but whose mass was enough to make the next one protest.

Paul checked. Tommy was still on the deck. Joel was in his chair. Doug was across the table. Nate had been on the couch — Paul leaned back to look through the kitchen doorway, and Nate was still there, hunched forward now, phone in his hands, the screen casting a blue wash across his face.

Someone else was on the stairs. Or the stairs were settling.

Then the upstairs hallway. Footsteps — heavy, deliberate, the stride of a big man trying to walk quietly and failing because big men walk heavily even when they try not to. A pause. Then the sound of Martin's bedroom door, which Paul had left almost closed, swinging open on its hinges. That specific hinge — the top one always gave a faint metallic moan that Martin had said he'd oil and never did.

Someone was in Martin's room.

Paul's first thought was flat and practical: someone's checking on him. Paying respects. Having their own version of the moment Paul had just had. His second thought arrived with the sour taste of Doug's coffee climbing back up his throat: someone's doing what I just did.

He almost got up. Almost went to the stairs and called out. But that would mean admitting he was listening, and listening meant he was alert to the possibility that someone else had a reason to be in that room at 3 AM, and being alert to that possibility meant acknowledging that Paul himself had had a reason, and Paul's reason was nothing, his reason was love and panic and bad risk assessment, and he did not want to establish a precedent of vigilance in this kitchen at this hour.

He sat. He drank his coffee.

Over the next two hours, the house moved. Not the way houses move when they settle — the pine beams adjusting to the temperature drop, the A-frame groaning against the wind off the lake, the sounds you stopped hearing after the first night. The house moved the way a house moves when people are trying to move through it without being heard and are not quite succeeding.

At 3:22 — Paul noted the time because noting times was what his brain did, it was what insurance adjusters' brains did, the filing and cataloging as automatic as breath — at 3:22, a toilet flushed upstairs. Not a casual flush, not the half-pull you give at night when you're trying to be quiet. The handle held down hard, the full-tank flush, the kind of flush you commit to when you need everything to go down on the first try. The plumbing rattled in the walls for a beat afterward, the old copper pipes that Martin was always going to replace.

At 3:48, the study door. Paul knew it was the study because the study had an old-fashioned thumb latch instead of a knob — a wrought-iron piece that Martin's parents had installed in the seventies — and it made a specific metallic snap that was different from every other door in the house. Distinct as a fingerprint. Paul was sitting twelve feet from that latch and he heard it click open and he did not get up because getting up would have meant he'd heard it and hearing it would have meant he cared and caring would have meant there was something in the study worth caring about at 3:48 in the morning.

At 4:10, footsteps overhead again. Lighter this time — not the heavy tread from earlier but someone moving with the particular caution of a person carrying something, their weight redistributed, their steps shortened. Then the soft rattle of the shared bathroom's medicine cabinet, the hinge on that mirror-door that squeaked in a way Martin's mother had probably complained about before Paul was born. Something small and hard met the tile floor — a bottle, a container — and was picked up quickly, the tiny percussion of plastic on ceramic and then the scrabble of fingers retrieving it. The footsteps retreated, lighter than they'd come, moving with the careful rhythm of someone who now had something in their hands they didn't want to drop.

At 4:30 or 4:40, Paul couldn't pin it exactly — his timekeeping had started to fray at the edges, the way precision frays when you've been awake too long and the only clock is the coffee maker's cycle — a phone screen glowed in the hallway outside the kitchen. Not a flashlight, not an overhead light. The specific blue-white of a phone screen, held close to someone's body, the glow moving and pausing and moving again and then going dark. Someone standing in the hallway doing something on a phone that could not wait for daylight.

Paul cataloged all of it. He did not mean to catalog it. His brain did what his brain had done for twenty-three years at Midwestern Casualty: it filed. It cross-referenced. It noted the discrepancies and the patterns and the things that did not match. The heavy footsteps on the stairs were not the light footsteps near the bathroom, which meant at least two different people. The toilet flush was upstairs, consistent with the guest bedroom wing. The study latch was ground floor, twelve feet from the kitchen — and Paul had not seen or heard anyone pass through the kitchen to reach the hallway, which meant they'd come through the great room or down the back stairs from the basement and approached the study from the opposite direction. The phone screen in the hallway could have been anyone, but the glow was low, waist-height, held by someone who was standing still and deliberating, and the deliberation had a quality Paul associated with deletion — the specific attention a person gives to a screen when they are making something go away.

He sat with all of it. He did not discuss it with Doug, who was still across the table, who was on his third cup of terrible coffee, and whose inside-out sweatshirt was now right-side-out, which meant Doug had taken it off and put it back on at some point when Paul was listening to the house and not watching Doug, and Paul thought about that and then set the thought aside in the same mental drawer where the rest of the night's observations were accumulating.

By 5 AM the house was quiet. Or the version of quiet a house achieves when you've been listening so hard that the absence of human sound feels like its own kind of noise. The refrigerator cycled. The pines moved outside the kitchen window. The lake lapped at something — the dock, the rocks, the shore.

The first gray light came through the window and Paul realized he had been sitting in the same chair for two hours, both hands on a mug of cold coffee, running a claims assessment on a house full of his oldest friends.

He stood up. His knees cracked. His lower back had locked into the shape of the chair and protested the new configuration. He walked down the hallway.

The study door was ajar. He had walked past it on his way to the kitchen hours ago and it had been closed. Now it was open two inches, and through the gap Paul could see Martin's desk and the landscape print on the wall — the one with the lake and the pines, the one Martin's parents had bought at a craft fair in Duluth in 1983 — and the print was slightly crooked. Tilted a quarter inch to the right, as if someone had bumped the wall behind it or moved it and tried to put it back and misjudged the angle. Paul knew the angle because he'd sat in Martin's study dozens of times over twenty-five years, talking about nothing, and the print had always hung straight.

He continued to Martin's room. The door was as he'd left it. He pushed it open.

Martin was on the bed, in the position Paul had arranged. The arm across the chest, the sheets smooth, the pillow centered. Paul's work. Paul's theater. But the nightstand was different. The scotch glass was there, the lamp was there, but something was absent, and Paul stood in the doorway staring at the nightstand the way you stare at one of those spot-the-difference puzzles, knowing something has changed but unable to isolate it until the absence resolves itself into a shape.

The pill case was there — Martin's small brown leather supplement case, fish oil, B-12, the vitamins Martin took every morning with the earnest optimism of a man who believed he could drink like a twenty-two-year-old and compensate with capsules. But next to where the pill case sat, there was a gap. A circle of dust-free nightstand where something had been and was not. Paul had seen it during CPR without registering it — a small orange prescription bottle, the standard pharmacy kind with the white child-safe cap, sitting next to the pill case. He hadn't read the label. He'd been busy trying to restart a heart.

The bottle was gone. Someone had taken it.

He backed out of the room and went to the shared bathroom at the other end of the hall. The medicine cabinet was open — not swung wide, just unlatched, the mirror-door floating a quarter inch forward like a drawer that hadn't been pushed fully home. Paul didn't open it further, but he could see the shelves. His own toiletries kit on the second shelf, a tube of toothpaste, Nate's brand of mouthwash that no one else would use. The third shelf, where there had been — Paul was almost certain — other things. More things. He couldn't itemize what was missing because he hadn't inventoried what was there, but the shelf had a cleaned-out quality, a sparseness that felt deliberate.

He went back to the kitchen. Doug was still at the table, in the same position, as though he had not moved since Paul sat down. But his mug was empty and the coffee pot was lower and the sweatshirt was different and the silence was different, and Paul looked at Doug and Doug looked at Paul and neither of them said any of the things that the looking contained.

Tommy was no longer on the deck. Paul could hear the shower running upstairs — the pipes shuddering the way they shuddered when you turned the hot water past halfway, a sound so specific to this house that it had become part of the weekend's ambient texture, as regular as the loons and the refrigerator. Tommy was showering at 5:15 in the morning.

Joel was still in the chair by the fireplace, chin on chest, eyes closed. His breathing was too regular for real sleep — Joel was playing a role, or Joel had genuinely found a way to turn off, and either possibility was equally Joel.

Cliff was in the great room now, sitting where Nate had been, scrolling through his phone with an expression of focused calm that Paul recognized from twenty years of watching Cliff work rooms and deals and dinners: the face Cliff put on when the numbers were bad and the other party hadn't figured it out yet. Cliff looked up when Paul entered the kitchen and gave him a nod that contained nothing — no grief, no solidarity, no meaning that Paul could read. A nod that was a closed door.

Nate was gone from the couch. Paul looked around. Not on the deck, not in the hallway, not in the kitchen. Gone somewhere. Doing something. At 5 in the morning.

Paul poured himself another cup of Doug's devastating coffee and stood at the kitchen window. The lake was visible through the pines, iron-gray in the predawn light, flat and still except for the small lapping at the dock that was the lake's constant, the sound that you stopped hearing after the first hour of every visit and that Paul was hearing now as if for the first time. The dock reaching out into the water. The Adirondack chairs on the deck, wet with dew. The propane grill where Martin had made burgers eighteen hours ago, scraping the grates with the perfectionist's attention he gave to every physical task, scolding Tommy for leaving grease on the drip tray, and Tommy had called him a psychopath and Martin had said "I'm a psychopath with clean grill grates" and everyone had laughed and the burgers were perfect. Eighteen hours ago.

"Deck."

Tommy was in the kitchen doorway. Showered, dressed in jeans and a polo shirt that was wrong for Tommy — too neat, too deliberate, the outfit of a man who had made decisions about his appearance at 5 AM that were not about appearance. His face was scrubbed and raw. His hands were in his pockets, and Tommy's hands were never in his pockets. Tommy's hands were always doing something — holding a beer, gripping a shoulder, gesturing at a point he was driving home. Hands in pockets was a man containing his hands.

"We should call," Tommy said. "Should've called hours ago."

"Yeah."

"I'll do it. I'll call from the deck, there's better signal."

"OK."

Tommy stood there another beat. He looked at Paul with an expression that Paul would spend weeks trying to classify — guilt, or something that wore guilt's face but moved differently behind the eyes, something that used guilt as a shelter for whatever the actual feeling was. Then Tommy went out the sliding door and Paul heard his voice on the deck, his coaching voice, reporting the death of Martin Kessler to the Becker County 911 dispatcher with the clipped factual cadence of a man who had called in injuries before: name, location, situation, time discovered. No emotion in the delivery. All the emotion in the hands that Paul could see through the glass, gripping the deck railing until the knuckles went white.

Paul looked around the kitchen. The fluorescent tube buzzed. The refrigerator cycled on. The morning was arriving whether any of them were ready for it, the light coming through the pines in thin gold lines that hit the lake and turned the gray to something brighter that had no right to be beautiful and was.

He thought about what he'd heard in the night. The footsteps, the flush, the latch, the cabinet, the phone. At least four separate events, produced by at least three different people — probably more, probably four or five, because the weights were different and the locations were scattered and the timing was spread across two hours in a pattern that was too irregular for one person and too purposeful for coincidence.

Each of them had done something. That was what the night added up to. Each of them had gotten out of bed and moved through the house and touched or taken or adjusted or deleted something, and none of them had coordinated with any of the others, and the result — Paul could feel this in his insurance brain, the part that understood how small individual decisions compound into systemic exposure — the result was that the scene upstairs no longer matched the scene Paul had found at 2 AM. A prescription bottle was gone. The study had been entered. Something had been flushed. Something had been done on a phone. And Paul himself had repositioned the body, wiped the surfaces, and cleaned Martin's mouth.

Five separate adjustments to the same death. Five independent decisions, each one probably as irrational as Paul's, each one driven by some private panic that had nothing to do with murder and everything to do with whatever each man was hiding from the others and from himself. And the composite — the sum of all their small, scared, separate acts — looked nothing like a man who died in his sleep. It looked like something designed. Something coordinated. A committee's work.

Except the committee had never met.

Paul Decker, insurance claims adjuster, twenty-three years with Midwestern Casualty, stood in the kitchen of a house on Elbow Lake and assessed the total exposure. That was the phrase his brain produced, automatic and grotesque: total exposure. As if Martin's death were a claim. As if the liability could be quantified and the deductible applied and the payout calculated and everyone could go home with a check and a sense of resolution. He could write the report. He could see the form. He could fill in every field except the one that mattered, the one at the top that asked for the determination, and the determination was: loss. Total loss. Not covered under any policy that existed or would ever exist. Not adjustable. Not fixable. Not a thing that would resolve into a number and a signature and a file closed and stored.

His best friend was dead. His best friend's house had been rearranged in the night by the men who loved him. And when the county came — the deputy, the detective, whoever showed up — they would walk into a scene that looked, to anyone who hadn't lived through it, like something planned. Something calculated. Something that a group of men had agreed to do.

The sun cleared the tree line. The lake turned from gray to gold. Paul put his cold coffee in the sink and rinsed the mug and set it on the drying rack the way Martin would have wanted, because Martin was particular about his drying rack, and that was the thought that finally broke Paul — not the death, not the CPR, not the night of sounds, but the drying rack, the stupid wooden drying rack that Martin had bought at a kitchen supply store in Fargo and been unreasonably proud of, and Paul stood at the sink with water running over his hands and cried without making a sound while the sun hit the lake and Tommy's voice carried through the glass door, talking to the dispatcher, giving directions to a house that none of them would ever come back to the same way again.


# Chapter 3

Tommy couldn't sit down.

He'd been standing for forty minutes, maybe an hour — he'd lost the sequence somewhere between Nate coming down the basement stairs saying Martin wasn't breathing and Paul running up to the master bedroom and coming back down with his glasses crooked and his shirt soaked in something Tommy didn't want to identify. Now it was — he checked the microwave clock — 3:14 in the morning, and he was standing in Martin's kitchen gripping the back of a chair so hard the wood was creaking, and he could not sit down because sitting down meant the situation was settled and the situation was not fucking settled.

Nate was on the couch in the great room, still in the shorts and T-shirt he'd been sleeping in, his face doing something Tommy had never seen on it — a kind of caved-in blankness, all the comedy evacuated. Paul sat at the kitchen table with both hands wrapped around a mug of coffee he hadn't touched. Joel was outside on the deck, which was either smart or suspicious — Tommy wasn't sure which. Doug had gone back to the basement, or maybe he hadn't. Cliff was somewhere. Cliff was always somewhere.

Martin was upstairs in the master bedroom, dead.

Tommy let go of the chair and walked to the refrigerator and opened it. He didn't want anything from the refrigerator. He closed it. Walked to the window over the sink. The lake was black. The pines were black against something slightly less black. A loon called — that idiot laugh they made, deranged and full-throated — and Tommy pressed his forehead against the cold glass and thought: even the goddamn birds.

He'd been doing this since Nate told them. Opening things and closing them. Picking things up and putting them down. Pacing the length of the kitchen, turning at the counter, pacing back. His body wanted to do something and his brain couldn't tell it what, so it was doing everything and nothing, the engine running in neutral while the car sat in the driveway going nowhere.

"We should call somebody," Tommy said. He'd already said this. He'd said it twenty minutes ago and Paul had said they should wait until morning, wait until they could think straight, and Tommy had said OK, and now he was saying it again because his mouth worked on loops when the rest of him broke down.

"In the morning," Paul said, same as before. His voice was flat. Insurance-adjuster flat. The voice of a man who assessed damage for a living, and the damage this time was his best friend dead in a lake house in September.

"We should call now."

"It's three in the morning, Tommy. Who are we calling? The guy's dead. He's —" Paul stopped. He pressed his thumb and forefinger against the bridge of his nose, under the wire rims, and held them there. "In the morning. We'll call in the morning."

Tommy turned from the window. He wanted to say something useful. He wanted to take the room by the shoulders and shake it into functioning. This was what he did — he walked into rooms full of seventeen-year-olds who were down by twenty-one at the half and he told them the play and they ran it. He'd done it for twenty years. He could do this.

"All right, listen," he said. He pointed at Paul. "Decker, you're the most put-together guy in this room right now. You handle the phone calls when the time comes. Family, police, whoever. Griggs —" He turned toward the great room. "Griggs, you good? You need water? Somebody should eat something."

Nate looked at him from the couch with an expression Tommy would later understand as pity but in the moment read as confusion.

"Tommy," Nate said.

"I'm serious. We can't just stand around with our dicks in our hands. We need a —"

"He's dead, man." Nate's voice cracked on the second word, and he said it again, quieter, to the floor. "He's dead."

Tommy's hands went to the back of his neck, both of them, fingers interlocking behind his head. The posture of a coach watching a bad play develop downfield in slow motion, the kind of play where the safety's been beat and the receiver is already gone and all you can do is stand there gripping the back of your own skull and watch. He held it there for five seconds, ten, squeezing until the tendons in his forearms burned.

"Yeah," he said. "I know."

He walked to the counter and braced both palms flat against the laminate and leaned into it, head down, shoulders loaded, the same stance he used calling timeouts he didn't have. The coaching thing had lasted ninety seconds. Ninety seconds of pretending this was a problem that could be organized, delegated, and resolved by the fourth quarter. It wasn't. Martin was dead and there was no play to draw up and the halftime talk had hit the room like a dropped pass in the end zone — a thing Tommy had reached for that his hands couldn't hold.

And then the thought came back.

It had been there since Nate first said the word "breathing." Not a fully formed thought at first, just a cold pressure below his ribs, a sensation that didn't belong to the grief or the shock but lived next to them — a tenant that had moved in while the other emotions were still unpacking. Now, standing in Martin's kitchen at 3:14 in the morning with his palms on the counter and the smell of Lagavulin still living in the air like Martin's ghost had stopped to have one more drink on his way out, the thought landed.

The pills.

Tommy had given Martin the Viagra on Friday evening. That was the joke. That was the bit. Twenty-five years of the same gag — every draft weekend, Tommy brought Martin something stupid. The inflatable woman that year they were all thirty. The Rogaine when Martin started losing his hair. The novelty jockstrap that said FANTASY STUD that Martin wore over his jeans for twenty minutes because Martin committed to bits the way other men committed to marriages. And this year: a sample pack of Viagra, four pills in a foil blister pack, purchased from a guy at Tommy's gym who sold them loose because Tommy was not about to walk into a CVS and explain that he needed a prescription he didn't have for a gift he was buying as a joke for a friend whose love life was none of his business.

He'd tossed the blister pack to Martin at the dinner table Friday night. "For the old man," he'd said, "since Karen's got the Kessler magic working overtime." Everybody had laughed. Martin had called him a goddamn idiot and slid the pills next to his water glass and that had been the end of it. The joke delivered, the joke received, the joke retired to Martin's nightstand where it could die of natural causes.

Except Tommy had seen Martin take one.

Friday night, after the hot tub, after Joel and Cliff had gone to bed and it was just Martin and Tommy and Paul on the deck finishing the Lagavulin. Martin had excused himself, gone upstairs, and when Tommy passed the master bedroom door on his way to the guest room he'd seen Martin standing at the nightstand pressing one pill through the foil with his thumb. Not for Karen — Karen was in Minneapolis, or wherever Karen was. Not for anyone. Just the bravado of it, the escalation of the bit. Martin had seen Tommy watching and given him a look — the look, the one that said "watch this" — and dry-swallowed the pill and winked, and Tommy had told him he was out of his mind and Martin had said "I've been told" and that was the last funny thing Martin Kessler ever said and Tommy was the only person in the house who understood what it might mean.

Viagra and hearts. Tommy was not a doctor. He was a high school football coach with a bachelor's in physical education from the University of Minnesota and twenty-two years of mandatory first-aid certifications that had taught him exactly enough to apply pressure and call 911. But he knew — everybody knew, it was on the commercials, it was the whole reason the joke had teeth — that you weren't supposed to take that shit if you had heart problems. The warning labels. The disclaimers. The part of the commercial where the voice got fast and the text got small. And Martin had been drinking all night and Martin had been in the hot tub for an hour and Martin's face had been ruddy and flushed since before the first beer hit and now Martin was dead at forty-seven of something that looked exactly like what happened when you gave a man a pill his heart couldn't handle and then watched him wash it down with scotch and soak in a hundred-and-two-degree hot tub.

Tommy pushed off the counter. He could feel his pulse in his palms where they'd been pressed against the laminate, the blood pounding in the flat of each hand like something trying to get out.

He looked at Paul. Paul was staring into his coffee.

"I'm going upstairs," Tommy said.

Paul didn't look up. "What for?"

"Bathroom."

Paul nodded. The universal masculine acceptance of the bathroom excuse, the social contract that said a man's stated reason for going upstairs at three in the morning was whatever the man said it was and nobody needed to verify it. Tommy went.

The stairs were dark. He skipped the third step — everybody who'd ever stayed in this house knew to skip the third step, the creak was a twenty-five-year-old hazard, Martin had been saying he'd fix it since Clinton was president — and his foot found the fourth step by muscle memory. The hallway upstairs was lit by a night-light plugged into the baseboard outlet near the shared bathroom, the kind shaped like a shell that Martin's mother had probably installed in 1994 and no one had ever replaced. Its glow made the pine-paneled walls look amber. Tommy could see the door to the master bedroom. It was open. Paul had left it open, or someone had.

Tommy didn't want to go in there. He wanted to go in there the way you wanted a root canal — not at all, with the understanding that the alternative was worse. The alternative was the three remaining Viagra pills sitting in a foil blister pack on Martin's nightstand, waiting for whoever came to deal with the body — the EMTs, the cops, the coroner, the guys whose job it was to catalog exactly what a dead man had in reach when he died — to find them and pick them up and start asking questions Tommy did not want to answer.

He went in.

Martin was on the bed. Paul had done something to the body — Tommy could tell it had been adjusted, straightened, though he didn't know the specifics and didn't want to look long enough to catalog them. Martin was on his back. His face was the wrong color — a grayish purple that bore no resemblance to the ruddy flush Tommy had spent twenty-five years drinking across from. His hands were crossed on his chest, which looked staged, which Tommy filed away and immediately buried because he could not think about what other people were doing tonight. He had his own problem.

The room smelled like Martin: the Lagavulin, the cedar from the closet, the humid funk of a man who'd been in a hot tub four hours ago and hadn't showered. The smell was so specifically, bodily Martin that Tommy's throat closed for a second and he had to stand there in the doorway and breathe through it.

He went to the nightstand. The lamp was off. In the light from the hallway he could see: a water glass, half full. Martin's reading glasses, folded. A paperback — something about the Civil War, a big heavy thing with a cracked spine — facedown with the pages splayed.
