And the blister pack — three pills still sealed in their foil bubbles, the fourth one punched through. Three small blue diamonds behind silver foil, sitting on a dead man's nightstand like a jury that had already reached its verdict.

Tommy picked up the blister pack. His hand was shaking — not the fine tremor of exhaustion but the big, visible, whole-hand shake of pure adrenaline, the kind his players got before state championship games, the kind he'd spent two decades teaching boys to breathe through. He was not breathing through it. He was shoving a blister pack of Viagra into the pocket of his sweatpants and walking out of the room without looking at Martin because if he looked at Martin he was going to make a sound and if he made a sound in this hallway at three in the morning someone was going to come up those stairs and ask why Tommy Renaldi was standing outside the dead man's bedroom with a prescription that didn't belong to either of them.

In the hallway his elbow caught the doorframe and the blister pack crinkled in his pocket, loud in the silence — unreasonably loud, the foil amplifying his exit like a snare drum — and he stopped and pressed both palms flat against the pine-paneled wall and waited. His heart was doing something that felt structural rather than rhythmic, a pounding that seemed to be testing the walls of his chest for weaknesses.

Someone was moving downstairs. He could hear footsteps on the main floor. The front door or the back door opened and closed — he couldn't tell which. One of the other guys going somewhere, doing something. Tommy didn't know what anybody else was up to tonight and he didn't want to know. He had the Viagra. That was his problem. Whatever they were doing was theirs.

He walked to the guest room — his room for the weekend, the one with the twin beds and the loon-patterned quilt that Martin's mother had sewn, the quilt Tommy had been sleeping under for twenty-five Septembers — and sat on the edge of the bed. The blister pack was in his pocket. He could feel the pills through the thin fabric of his sweatpants, the hard ridges of the foil pressing against his thigh.

He should flush them right now. Walk to the shared bathroom, punch the pills out, flush them, throw the empty pack in his duffel. A play. Three steps. He could execute three steps.

But the shared bathroom was twelve feet from the master bedroom and Paul had left that door open and Tommy could not make himself walk back down that hallway and listen to the toilet running and the pipes shuddering while Martin's body cooled on the other side of the wall. Not yet. Not right now.

He sat on the bed and gripped the edge of the mattress with both hands and stared at the wall — pine-paneled, a mounted fish that Martin's father had caught in 1987, a small watercolor of the lake — and thought about Denise and the kids and the coaching job and everything he'd built over twenty years that rested on the assumption that Tommy Renaldi was a simple man who did simple things and had never been responsible for anything worse than a losing season. He thought about what it would look like in a police report: the pills, the heart, the hot tub, the coach who brought a joke that killed his friend. He thought about whether there was a word for what he'd done and whether that word was manslaughter or negligent homicide or something else he didn't know because he wasn't Joel and had never needed to learn the law because the law was for people who had done things and Tommy — Tommy hadn't done anything, he'd told himself for years, decades, he hadn't done anything except what a friend does, what a guy does, what you do and then you don't talk about it and it stays where you left it — he'd just given a man a pill.

He waited for the sun.

The sun came in flat and pale through the curtains at something after six, and by seven-thirty Paul was calling the police on the landline in Martin's study because cell service inside the house was useless, and by eight-thirty two deputies from the Becker County Sheriff's Office were standing in the great room with their hats off and their notebooks out, and Tommy was sitting on the couch with the blister pack of Viagra in the right pocket of his jacket.

He'd put the jacket on when the deputies arrived — the morning was cold and pockets were deeper than sweatpants. The blister pack sat against his thigh like a second wallet, a thing he was aware of every time he shifted his weight.

The deputies were young, both of them. One was maybe twenty-six with a mustache he was clearly still auditioning, and the other looked like he'd started shaving within the last presidential administration. They asked questions — when did you last see the deceased, what time did you go to bed, any medical conditions you're aware of — and Tommy answered in the short declarative sentences that coaches used when the game was out of hand and the post-game press conference was a formality. "Yes." "No." "Around one." "I don't know." They wrote things down. They went upstairs. They came back down. One of them made a phone call on the deck where the signal was better, and the other stood in the kitchen drinking the coffee Nate had made, which was too strong because Nate made coffee like he was trying to punish it.

Tommy sat on the couch and the Viagra was in his pocket and the deputies were in the house and the thing he should have done at 3:14 in the morning — the flush, the disposal, the simple mechanical act of pressing a lever and letting the plumbing do what plumbing did — was now a thing he had to do with two sheriff's deputies twelve feet away eating Martin's granola bars and discussing whether the Vikings had a realistic shot this year.

The one with the mustache thought they could make the wild card. The other one was less optimistic. Tommy sat there and listened to them talk about Sam Darnold's arm strength and thought about the three blue pills in his jacket pocket and wanted to scream.

The deputies went outside. Something about waiting for the county detective, who was driving in from Detroit Lakes. Thirty minutes, maybe forty.

Tommy stood up.

He looked around the room. Paul was in the kitchen rinsing out the coffee pot with the careful, procedural motions of a man who needed something to do with his hands. Nate was at the table, his podcast cap pulled low, staring at his phone but not looking at it. Cliff was leaning against the counter with his arms crossed and a concerned expression that looked rehearsed — not fake, exactly, but positioned, like he'd found the right face in a catalog and was wearing it carefully. Joel was in the study with the door half-shut. Doug was in the basement or the bathroom or one of the other places Doug went when there were too many people in a room.

"Bathroom," Tommy said, to nobody in particular.

He walked down the hallway toward the half bath near the front door. The hallway was fifteen feet of pine floor and pine walls, the specific golden wood of a lake house that had been absorbing summer after summer since the seventies, and Tommy walked it with his right hand in his jacket pocket and his fingers wrapped around the blister pack. He could feel the foil under his thumb, the smooth bumps of the three remaining pills.

He pulled the blister pack out. His hands were worse than they'd been in the dark — a full night of adrenaline and no sleep had done something to his fine motor control that two decades of sideline composure couldn't fix. He pressed the first pill through the foil with his thumb. It popped out clean into his left palm, warm from his pocket. He pressed the second one, and it came out clean too. He was walking and pressing at the same time, which was a coordination task he'd have called basic if it weren't happening in the hallway of a dead man's house with deputies on the deck and a detective thirty minutes out.

The third pill didn't pop clean. The foil tore sideways and the pill shot out at an angle and hit the floor.

Tommy stopped walking.

The pill — small, blue, diamond-shaped, the exact color of a pharmaceutical commercial's reassuring sky, identifiable from ten feet by anyone who'd watched television in the last twenty years — bounced once on the hardwood and rolled. It rolled the way small objects rolled on old uneven floors: not straight, but with a wobble, a slow curving drift toward the baseboard that was both lazy enough to track and fast enough that Tommy's hand, when he lunged for it, closed on nothing but air and the warm space where the pill had been a half second earlier.

It came to rest against the baseboard, three feet ahead of where he was standing.

Tommy Renaldi — six-two, two-fifteen, head coach of the Eastview Lightning for twenty years, varsity record 147-89, three-time conference coach of the year — went down on his hands and knees on the hallway floor of his dead friend's lake house and crawled after a Viagra pill.

His kneecaps hit the hardwood and his palms hit the hardwood and the blister pack was still in his left hand, crumpled and empty, while his right hand reached for the baseboard. He could see the pill against the wood — a small blue diamond against honey-colored pine, absurdly bright, practically glowing, as if the pill itself wanted to be found. His fingers reached it. He felt the smooth coating under his index finger, the tiny rounded edge, and he picked it up and closed his fist around it and stayed there on his hands and knees with his forehead almost touching the baseboard and thought: this is what it looks like. Not the version you'd tell someone, not the version that sounds like anything — just a forty-seven-year-old man on the floor of a hallway, holding a pill, with his dead friend upstairs and a detective on the way.

He pushed himself up. His knees popped — the left one first, then the right, the specific sound of a body that had spent too many years on turf and was starting to complain about it. He looked at the floor where the pill had rolled. No powder, no residue, no blue smudge announcing what had happened here. He checked the baseboard where the pill had rested, and it was clean. He looked down the hallway in both directions — empty. Nobody had seen him. The kitchen was around the corner and the deputies were on the deck and the pill was in his fist and the hallway was just a hallway.

He walked the remaining four feet to the half bath and went in and closed the door.

It was a small room. Toilet, pedestal sink, a framed print of loons on the water that Martin's mother had hung before Tommy ever set foot in this house. A window with frosted glass that let in the mid-morning light — pale September gold, the kind that made everything in the room look like it was being remembered rather than seen. Tommy opened his fist over the toilet. Three blue pills in a sweating palm. He dropped them in. They made a small sound hitting the water, barely anything, and sat there on the white porcelain looking up at him.

He flushed.

The toilet was old. The flush was full-throated and violent — the kind of pipe-shuddering, wall-rattling roar that lake house plumbing produced, the original pipes Martin had been saying he'd replace since the nineties. The sound filled the small room and pushed through the door and probably reached the kitchen. Tommy watched the water swirl and the pills disappear and the bowl refill, clear, no trace of blue, and he thought: OK. They're gone.

He looked at the blister pack in his other hand. Empty foil with three pill-shaped holes punched through. He folded it twice and shoved it deep into his jacket pocket and turned on the sink to wash his hands because that was what a person did in a bathroom and he needed the thirty seconds of being a person who was just washing his hands.

Then he heard the gravel.

The sound came through the frosted window — tires on the driveway, the crunch of a car rolling up the slope to the house. Not the deputies' cruiser, which had been parked outside for an hour and a half — something new. Tommy shut off the water. He stood there with his wet hands dripping on the floor and listened. The engine cut. A car door opened and closed. Footsteps on the front porch — not heavy, not the boots the deputies wore, something lighter. The doorbell rang, the two-tone chime Martin had installed as a joke because the original bell sounded, Martin had told them, "like a dentist's office in hell."

A woman's voice, clear and warm, came through the front door and the hallway and the half bath door and arrived in the small room where Tommy was standing with water on his hands and empty foil in his pocket:

"Good morning! I'm looking for — oh, goodness, let me check my notes here — Mr. Kessner's residence? I'm Detective Mallick, from the county. I'm so sorry for the delay, 59 was just a mess this morning."

Someone opened the front door. Tommy heard the latch, the creak of the hinges.

"Kessler," someone said. Joel's voice. Precise and corrective, even now. "His name was Kessler."

"Kessler! Oh, I'm so sorry. I had it written down wrong — no, I had it right, I just — Kessler. Thank you. Goodness." There was a sound that Tommy couldn't identify — a transfer of something heavy, or ceramic, from one set of hands to another. "I brought this. It's a green bean casserole. It's not much but my mother always said you don't show up to someone's home empty-handed, even when — well. Is there somewhere I can set it down? The counter's fine, anywhere's fine."

More voices. Paul saying something polite. Nate saying nothing, which was wrong for Nate.

"And oh — I'm so sorry, before we get started, is there a powder room? I should have stopped on the way but there's nothing on 59 between Detroit Lakes and here, you'd think they'd put a gas station somewhere but I suppose that's the charm of — is it down this hall?"

Tommy heard footsteps in the hallway. Light footsteps. Coming toward him. Coming toward the half bath.

He looked at his hands. They were wet. He looked at the toilet. The water had refilled, clear and still. He looked at the door. The half bath door had no lock — he noticed this now, with a clarity that felt surgical, for the first time in twenty-five years of coming to this house, the half bath door near the front entrance had no lock — and his right hand found the knob and held it.

"I think that one might be occupied," Paul's voice said from somewhere in the hallway. Measured and careful, the way Paul said everything, the voice of a man who assessed situations for a living. "There's one upstairs, Detective. Second door on the left."

"Oh! Occupied. Of course. I'm so sorry." A small, genuine-sounding laugh. The footsteps stopped. "Upstairs? Second on the left? Thank you. You're a lifesaver." The footsteps reversed direction and headed for the stairs. The third step creaked under her weight — nobody had told her to skip it yet. "Oh! Does that always do that? What a great old house. Is this original woodwork? The banister is just beautiful."

Her voice carried down from the second floor, getting fainter, still talking — something about woodwork, something about how they didn't build them like this anymore — and then the upstairs bathroom door closed and Tommy was alone in the half bath with his hand on the knob and his wet fingers leaving prints on the brass and the detective's muffled voice overhead and the empty blister pack pressed against his thigh.

He thought about the seventeen times in his coaching career when the other team had scored with no time left and the scoreboard changed and the outcome was sealed and there was nothing to do but stand on the sideline and watch your season end — the specific helplessness of a man whose job was to control outcomes standing in a place where the outcome was already decided.

Those had been bad.

This was worse, because those seasons, at least, had ended clean. The buzzer went and the score was final and you shook the other coach's hand and you walked to the bus and you told your kids they'd played hard and the loss stayed on the field where it belonged. It didn't follow you into a bathroom. It didn't sit in your pocket.

He waited until he heard the upstairs toilet flush and the bathroom door open and the detective's footsteps on the stairs — she skipped the third step this time, somebody must have warned her — and then he let go of the knob and dried his hands on his jeans and opened the door and walked back to the great room and sat down on the couch and put both hands on his knees and stared at the draft board still hanging on the wall. Martin's picks were circled in red. Tommy's were in blue. The whole grid of names and numbers that had been a comedy Friday night was now the wallpaper of a room where a county detective was setting her leather notebook on the kitchen counter next to a casserole dish and saying something about how sorry she was and how she just had a few questions and would anyone like some of this casserole because there was plenty, she'd made way too much, she always made too much.

Nate looked at Tommy from across the room.

"You all right?" Nate said. It wasn't a bit. It wasn't a riff or a setup or the opening of a segment. It was just a question from a man who looked like he already knew the answer.

"Fine," Tommy said. "I'm fine."

His right hand was in his jacket pocket, his fingers resting on the folded foil. In the kitchen, Detective Mallick was asking Paul about the casserole dish, whether there was room in the fridge, whether that was the right shelf or would a different one work better, and her voice was warm and friendly and relentless and it filled every corner of the room like weather.

Tommy sat on the couch and did not move and did not speak. The pills were in the pipes. The pills were in the water. The pills were gone and there was nothing left to find and the blister pack in his pocket was just foil, just trash, meaningless.

Then Mallick turned from the refrigerator and looked at him — briefly, the way you checked a room to see who was in it, the way you counted heads — and she smiled, a quick polite smile that didn't seem to be aimed at him specifically, just a smile for the room, and Tommy's hands went cold inside his pockets and the morning light through the kitchen window was suddenly very bright and very specific and he sat there and received it.


# Chapter 4

The two deputies from Becker County had made seven trips up and down the stairs. Joel counted from the kitchen table, where he'd been sitting since quarter to eight with a mug that kept getting refilled because Paul kept refilling it. Every trip, both deputies skipped the third step from the top. Someone had warned them about the creak, which meant someone had already started managing the scene, and Joel's money was on Paul.

He was on his fourth cup of coffee. His hands had the specific tremor that came from too much caffeine layered on no sleep and no food, and twice he'd caught himself doing the thing his clients did in arraignment — sitting very still, watching people with authority move through a space that used to feel safe. Seven years as a public defender in Hennepin County and he'd never once considered what it felt like from the inside. Now he knew. It felt like shit.

The great room still smelled like Martin's scotch — the Lagavulin he'd poured too generously last night, the way he poured everything too generously, the host who couldn't stop hosting even when people told him to sit down. The draft board was still on the wall, Martin's picks circled in red Sharpie, and nobody had touched it because touching it would mean deciding what the board was now. A record. An artifact. Evidence. The morning light through the A-frame windows was gold and stupid, the kind of light that belonged on a lake weekend postcard, not on whatever this had become.

Tommy was on the deck with his hands wrapped around a coffee mug like it was the only solid thing left. His face had a gray, clammy quality Joel had seen before on defendants who'd just heard the charges read. Nate was in the kitchen doorway, explaining something to a deputy about the lake house's history that the deputy had not asked about and did not care about. Doug was on the couch in the corner with his hood up and his knees drawn to his chest, looking at his phone with the particular blankness of a man who wasn't reading anything on it.

Cliff was in the Adirondack chair by the fireplace, legs crossed, coffee balanced on the armrest. He looked like a man waiting for a slightly delayed flight. Everyone else looked like they'd been in a car accident.

Paul came back from the hallway and sat down across from Joel. His wire-rimmed glasses were smudged and he hadn't cleaned them, which was how you knew Paul Decker was not all right — the man cleaned his glasses on a forty-minute cycle, same as the refrigerator.

"Medical examiner's been called," Paul said. "They said a couple hours."

"From Detroit Lakes?"

"Yeah." Paul rubbed the bridge of his nose where the glasses sat. "The deputies are being — they've been professional. Thorough. I told them Martin had some health concerns."

"Did he?"

Paul looked at him. "He was forty-seven and he drank like he was twenty-two. He didn't exercise. You saw what he ate last night."

"That's not a health concern, that's a lifestyle."

"Joel."

"I'm not being a lawyer."

"You're always being a lawyer." Paul said it without malice. He'd been saying it for twenty years.

Joel didn't argue because it was true. He picked up his mug and set it down again without drinking. "Has anyone called Linda?"

"I've got her number. I've been waiting until — I don't know what I'm waiting for. There's no protocol for this."

"No."

"Yeah." Paul stared at the draft board. Martin's first-round pick, circled in red, with the little annotation Martin always made — projected points in his neat handwriting, the numbers that didn't matter anymore. "I know."

The gravel driveway popped and crunched under tires. Both deputies turned toward the front windows. Joel saw a maroon Ford Explorer pulling up behind the sheriff's cruiser, and behind the wheel was a woman in a brown blazer reaching across the passenger seat for something.

What she was reaching for turned out to be a casserole dish.

She came through the front door carrying it two-handed, a Pyrex 9x13 with a foil top, and she was already talking before anyone could figure out who she was. "Oh, good morning. I'm Detective Mallick, Becker County Sheriff's. I'm so sorry about your friend. Is this the Kessner residence?"

The silence was specific. Six men who had all heard the same wrong thing, waiting for someone else to fix it.

"Kessler," Paul said. "Martin Kessler."

"Oh, Kessler! I'm sorry, I had it wrong in my notes somewhere. Kessler. Thank you." She shifted the casserole to one arm and extended her free hand toward nobody in particular. She was maybe five-six, stocky, gray-streaked brown hair pulled back in a low bun, reading glasses on a beaded chain around her neck. She looked like someone's neighbor who'd come over because she heard a commotion. "Now, is there a place I can set this down? It's green bean — my mother's recipe, and I know that sounds like a thing people say, but she really did make it better than anyone. Needs about twenty minutes at three-fifty if you want it later."

Nate was the first to move. He took the casserole from her the way you accept a dish from an aunt at a funeral — reflexively, without processing the absurdity of the gesture. "Yeah, the fridge is right here."

"Thank you so much. And before anything else — I'm sorry, this is embarrassing — could someone point me toward the restroom? The drive from Detroit Lakes and two cups of coffee and I just — well." She smiled. Warm, slightly sheepish. Joel's neck prickled. "Anyway."

"Half bath's by the front door," Paul said. "Just past the—"

"I'll find it. Thank you." She set her leather notebook on the kitchen counter and disappeared down the hallway with the unhurried ease of a woman looking for the bathroom at a friend's house, not at a death scene.

Joel looked at Paul. Paul looked at Joel.

"What the fuck," Tommy said from the deck doorway. He'd come inside and was standing there with his mug, staring at the hallway she'd gone into. "Did she just bring us a casserole?"

"A detective," Nate said, and Joel could hear the podcaster's machinery already whirring, already organizing this into a segment, a bit, something he could narrate. "A county detective brought a green bean casserole to a — I mean, is that standard? Is that a Minnesota thing?"

"It's a casserole thing," Doug said from the couch without looking up.

"Thank you, Doug, yes, it's a casserole thing. A casserole thing. At a death investigation. From a woman who called Martin the wrong name."

"Keep your voice down," Joel said.

"I'm just—"

"I know what you're just. Keep it down."

Mallick came back from the bathroom, drying her hands on the sides of her blazer. She retrieved the notebook from the counter and clicked open a pen, and one of the deputies — the younger one, crew cut, the one who'd been asking about the draft board earlier — gave her a nod that Joel read as deference. Interesting. She outranked them, or they respected her, or both. Hard to tell from a nod.

"Now," she said, pulling up a chair at the kitchen table as if she'd been invited. "I'm sorry about getting here late — there was road work on 59 that had everything backed up, if you can believe that on a Saturday. I know Deputy Sievert and Deputy Kohn have been taking good care of things." She opened the notebook to a fresh page. "So I understand Mr. Kessler was found earlier this morning?"

"Around two AM," Paul said. "Nate found him."

"Nate." She looked around the room. "That's—"

"Me. Yeah. Nathan Griggs." Nate stepped forward like he'd been called on in class. "I found him at — well, it was closer to one-fifty, I looked at my phone right when I—"

"One-fifty. OK." She wrote it down. "And he was in the downstairs bedroom? One of the—"

"Upstairs," Paul said. "The master bedroom. It's upstairs, at the front of the hall."

"Upstairs! Of course, I'm sorry." She wrote. "And you were all here this weekend — how many of you? I had seven in my notes, but—"

"Seven." It came out of Joel before he could catch it. "Six now."

The room flinched. Six now. He heard it land — blunt, too precise, the public defender who couldn't turn it off even when he was the one hurting. Mallick looked at him, and her expression was either sympathy or measurement.

"Six. Of course. I'm sorry." She wrote something. "And you're..."

"Joel Beckman."

"Joel." The pen moved. "And you all come up here every year for this? For the fishing, or is it—"

"Fantasy football," Nate said. "It's a keeper league. Twenty-five years running. Well, twenty-five drafts, we started in—"

"Oh, that's wonderful. My nephew does that — he's always on his phone at Thanksgiving arguing about some waiver wire something. It drives his mother crazy." Mallick looked around the great room with an expression that made Joel think of a real estate showing — appreciative, curious, taking stock. "This is a beautiful house. That ceiling is something. Is that real cedar paneling?"

"Yeah," Paul said.

"My ex-husband always wanted cedar in our den, but we could never — well, that's a different story." She peered through the screen door at the deck. "And is that a hot tub I saw coming in? Nice one. Seats what, six? Eight?"

"Six," Tommy said. His voice sounded like he'd swallowed sand.

"Six. And you all used it last night? Or was that the night before?"

"Last night," Paul said, and Joel watched Paul calculate as he said it — the careful precision of a man who assessed risk for a living, choosing what to disclose and what to let pass. "Some of us. It was a late night."

"Sure, sure. Draft night. Like New Year's Eve but for football." She laughed, a short, warm sound that seemed genuine. "And what time did everyone turn in? Ballpark."

"Midnight, maybe one." Paul looked around for confirmation. He got shrugs, half-nods, the useless consensus of men who'd been drinking and weren't sure about much except that they wished they'd gone to bed earlier and that nothing about this morning made sense. "It was late."

"Of course. Of course." She wrote. "Now — and I'm sorry to ask, I know this is terrible timing — but is there coffee? I saw the pot going and I'm still trying to wake up after that drive."

Joel watched Paul pour her a cup. Because of course Paul would pour a cup for the detective investigating their friend's death. Paul managed situations the way he managed claims — assess the damage, contain the exposure, make sure the environment stayed functional. Joel had watched him do it at Martin's divorce dinner, at Nate's birthday when Nate drank himself into a fight with a bouncer, at a dozen weekends when somebody got sloppy and needed to be handled. Paul was a claims adjuster for human situations: not the cause, not the solution, just the man who made sure the paperwork was in order and nobody's premium went up too much.

Mallick held the mug under her nose and breathed in. "Oh, that's good. That's a nice dark roast. Martin — Mr. Kessler had good taste." She looked at the draft board. "Is that your fantasy football thing? Oh my goodness, that's elaborate. All those names and numbers."

"It's a keeper league," Nate started, and Joel could see the full explanation loading behind his eyes — the league history, the rules, the statistics, the twenty-five years of context that Nate would happily narrate for forty minutes if nobody stopped him.

"Nate," Joel said. "She didn't ask how it works."

Mallick smiled at Joel over the coffee. The smile lasted one beat longer than it needed to. Or one beat shorter than he expected. He couldn't tell which, and that bothered him more than the smile itself.

---

She worked the room for another forty minutes before she got to Joel directly. He watched her circuit the way he watched opposing counsel work a jury — tracking the questions against the answers, mapping what was asked against what was revealed, noting the rhythm.

She talked to Tommy on the deck. Joel couldn't hear most of it through the glass door, but he could see Tommy's body — hunched forward in an Adirondack, hands laced behind his neck, the specific posture of a man who'd just been told something he already knew but wasn't ready to hear. Mallick stood beside him with her coffee and her notebook and at one point she said something that made Tommy look up, and Tommy answered, and she nodded and wrote.

She talked to Cliff in the kitchen. Cliff was Cliff — shoulders back, good eye contact, the salesman's composure that Joel had always found slightly insulting because it meant Cliff was always working a room, even when the room was a kitchen where his friend had died. Mallick said something about the house — the layout, which room was where — and Cliff corrected her. Joel couldn't hear the specifics, but he saw the correction happen: Cliff's hand gesturing toward the hallway, Mallick's pen moving, her murmured thank you. Another wrong thing. Another correction. Another note.

She talked to Doug. Briefly. Doug gave her what Doug gave everyone — monosyllables, nods, the agreeable blankness of a man who had long ago learned that the less you said, the less anyone asked. Mallick didn't push him. She just wrote.

Then she refilled her coffee, walked back to the kitchen table where Joel was still sitting, pulled the chair across from him, set her notebook flat, and said: "So. Joel. You're the attorney."

"Public defender."

"Public defender. That's different, right? Different from—"

"Different from most things people think about when they hear lawyer. Yeah."

"You must see some things in that job."

"I do."

"My sister-in-law is a paralegal over in Fergus Falls. She's got stories that would — well." Mallick sipped her coffee. "How long have you known Martin?"

"Twenty-five years. Since undergrad. We were in the same fraternity."

"Twenty-five years. That's something. That's longer than my marriage lasted." She wrote. "And you drove up Thursday? From the Cities?"

"Hennepin County."

"Hennepin County, right. Close enough to the Cities."

"Close enough."

"Sure." She looked at her notes, or looked at the gesture of looking at her notes. Joel knew the difference. He'd watched prosecutors do it a thousand times — the performance of reviewing information you already had memorized. Or maybe she was actually looking at her notes. Maybe she was exactly what she appeared to be: a county detective from a department that handled property crimes and DUIs, who'd brought a casserole because that's what you did in Minnesota when someone died. He couldn't tell. And not being able to tell was making him crazy.

"Now," Mallick said, "someone mentioned — and I apologize, I don't remember who — but someone mentioned you and Martin had a disagreement? Thursday evening?"

There it was. Joel felt the muscles along his jaw go tight. He'd known it was coming from the moment she'd walked in with the Pyrex dish, but knowing a question was coming and hearing it asked were not the same thing, and the gap between them was where defendants got stupid.

"We had a conversation," he said.

"A conversation. OK." She wrote the word. "And was that Thursday? Or was it Friday — I might have the days confused."

"Thursday."

"Thursday. Thank you." She wrote. "And what was the conversation about?"

"It was private."

Mallick looked at him. The expression on her face was patient and slightly sad, the way a good teacher looks at a student who she knows can do better. Or the way someone looks when they're confused and trying not to show it. Both options fit. Neither felt right.

"Private," she repeated. "I understand. I do. It's just, with the situation, I'm trying to put together a picture of the last—"

"I understand what you're doing, Detective." Joel kept his voice level — the voice he used with judges, the one that said cooperation without offering compliance. "A man died in his sleep and you need a timeline. I'm telling you: we had a private conversation Thursday night, it was heated, and it had nothing to do with his death."

"You're probably right. Most arguments between old friends don't — well." She let it trail off with a small wave of her pen. "Can I just ask, for my notes — what time was the conversation?"

"Late. Eleven, maybe eleven-thirty."

"And where was this?"

"On the deck."

"The deck. OK. Good." She wrote. "And after the conversation, you..."

"Went to bed."

"What time?"

"Midnight. Probably a little after."

"And you stayed in bed until—"

"Until Nate woke me up. Around two."

"Two. And between midnight and two, you didn't get up at all? Bathroom, glass of water, check your phone, anything?"

"No."

"No." She wrote. The pen scratched against the paper. The refrigerator had cycled off and the kitchen was quiet, just the two of them and the scratch of the pen and the low murmur of one deputy's radio from the hallway. "You didn't hear anything? Footsteps, doors, someone moving around?"

"I had my door closed."

"Which bedroom are you in?"

"Upstairs. Second guest room, end of the hallway."

"Not the one near the bathroom?"

"That's Paul and Tommy's side. I'm at the other end."

"Oh — I always get these lake house layouts mixed up. So many rooms." She waved the pen vaguely. "So you didn't hear anything. The first thing you knew was Nate knocking on your door at two."

"Correct."

Mallick put her pen down. She looked at Joel with an expression that was either very honest or very carefully assembled, and the fact that he couldn't tell which was its own answer, or wasn't.

"Joel, can I be straight with you?"

"You can try."

"You're the calmest person in this house. By a distance. And I think part of that's because you're a lawyer — excuse me, public defender — and you're used to being around this kind of thing. I understand that, I really do. But here's where I'm at." She folded her hands over the notebook. "You had a heated argument with Martin the night before he died, you won't tell me what it was about, and you're the only person in this house whose account doesn't have a single loose thread. Everything lines up perfectly. Bed at midnight, door closed, didn't hear anything, woke at two." She paused. "And that's either because you've got nothing to hide, or because you're very, very good at this."

Outside, a loon called from the lake, the wavering cry carrying over the water and through the screen door.

"Nobody's a suspect here," Joel said. He used the word before she could.

"Oh, of course not. No, nobody's — this is just an unattended death, Joel, and I'm making sure I have the picture right." She picked up her pen. "You're right that it was probably a cardiac event. That's almost certainly what happened. A man his age, his habits — I'm sure you're right."

She let the word probably sit on the table between them. Joel let it sit there too. He knew the move. He'd watched prosecutors drop a probably into a silence and wait for the defendant to fill the space, to correct the probably into a certainly, to volunteer the detail that turned a question into a confession. It was a first-year technique, or it was genuine hedging from a county detective who wasn't sure about anything.

"Is there anything else?" Joel asked.

"Not right now. I appreciate your time, I really do." Mallick stood and pushed her chair back. "I might have some follow-up questions, if that's all right."

"I'm not going anywhere."

"Of course. None of you are — well, not today, anyway." She smiled again. "The coffee really is good. Thank you for that."

She walked toward the hallway, notebook under her arm, and Joel sat at the table with his cold mug and the specific hollow that settled after an interrogation that hadn't been an interrogation, and he realized what was bothering him.

She hadn't asked whether he was angry at Martin.

It was the most obvious question in the world. You argued with the deceased the night before he died — were you angry? Any first-year law student at William Mitchell would've asked it within the first three minutes. Joel had been ready. He'd had the answer prepared, the careful qualified response that acknowledged the argument's existence without opening the door to anything that could be repackaged as motive. He'd rehearsed it the way he rehearsed cross-examination questions — contingencies mapped, exits planned, the specific controlled language of a professional who knew how words worked in rooms like this.

She hadn't asked. Which meant she didn't think of it — possible, but hard to believe, even from a detective who brought casseroles and forgot names. Or it meant she already knew the answer and was more interested in watching how long he'd wait before volunteering it.

Joel sat with his cold coffee. On the wall, the draft board listed Martin's team in red Sharpie, the handwriting already starting to look like something from another era. Upstairs, Mallick's footsteps moved slowly from room to room, the floorboards announcing each step. Joel watched a deputy come down the stairs with an evidence bag, and the deputy skipped the third step from the top, the creak that everybody in this house had been avoiding for twenty-five years without ever fixing it.

---

Mallick went upstairs. The group drifted back to the great room the way they'd been drifting back to this room for a quarter century — gravitating toward their usual positions without discussion, dropping into their usual chairs, arranging themselves around the furniture in the specific configuration that twenty-five years of weekends had worn into habit. Martin's leather chair by the fireplace was empty. Nobody sat in it. Nobody looked at it, which was the same as looking at it.

"So," Nate said. He said it the way he launched podcast segments — loaded, building, ready to go somewhere.

"Don't," Joel said.

"Don't what?"

"Don't do the thing where you narrate what just happened while we're still inside of it."

"I'm not narrating, I'm — OK, fine. I'm processing. Some of us process out loud."

"Process quieter."

Tommy had come in from the deck and was standing by the kitchen island with his arms crossed, gripping his own biceps hard enough that his knuckles were white. He hadn't sat down. Hadn't been able to sit down since this morning, as far as Joel had noticed. "She ask you about Thursday night?" he said to Joel.

"Yeah."

"The argument."

"Yeah."

"What'd you say?"

"Nothing. I said it was private."

"Good." Tommy nodded once, the sharp definitive nod of a sideline call. "Good. Don't give her shit. Don't give any of them shit. You don't talk to cops — that's the first thing, that's rule one."

"Tommy—" Paul started.

"What? He knows. He's a goddamn lawyer, he tells his clients this every day. Don't talk to cops. Right?"

"What I tell my clients," Joel said, "is to answer questions truthfully and concisely and not to volunteer information beyond what's asked. There's a distinction."

"Fuck the distinction. The distinction is for courtrooms. This is a lake house and Martin's dead and she's upstairs right now going through his things. I'm saying don't talk."

Paul was sitting forward with his elbows on his knees, hands laced, staring at the floor between his feet. When he looked up, he wore the expression Joel had seen at claims adjustment meetings — the face that preceded an assessment nobody was going to enjoy.

"OK," Paul said. "We should probably coordinate. Before she comes back down."

"Meaning what?" Cliff said from his chair. He'd been quiet through all of this, watching, his coffee balanced on the armrest like a prop in a play he'd already read.

"Meaning if she asks us all the same questions, the answers should be consistent. The timeline especially."

"They'll be consistent," Cliff said. His voice was even and warm, the voice of a man explaining a deal's terms. "We were all here. We went to bed. Martin died in his sleep. That's the story, because that's what happened."

"Right, but the small things—"

"What small things, Paul?" Cliff uncrossed his legs and recrossed them the other direction. "Dinner at seven. Draft at eight. Drinks. Hot tub. Bed. Martin was alive when we went to sleep and dead when Nate found him. That's the story. Simple."

"When you say simple," Joel cut in, "you mean uncomplicated. Not easy."

"I mean both."

The room was quiet. Joel could hear Mallick's footsteps above them — slow, deliberate, the old floorboards creaking. She was in the upstairs hallway. Or Martin's bedroom. It was hard to tell from down here, and the not knowing made the ceiling feel lower than it was.

"She asked me about the hot tub," Tommy said. He'd started pacing — short laps between the kitchen island and the screen door, the agitated circuit of a man whose body needed to move when his mind couldn't. "What time we got in, who was in it, how long. She asked if the water was hot. If somebody adjusted the temperature during the night."

"What'd you tell her?"

"That I didn't remember exactly. We were drinking." Tommy's hand went to the back of his neck and gripped. "And she asked about — she called it the 'outdoor spa.' Like she didn't know what a hot tub was. Or she didn't—"

"She knows what a hot tub is," Joel said.

The room registered this. Tommy's pacing stopped. Nate's mouth opened, then closed. Paul took off his glasses and cleaned them on his shirt, the first normal Paul gesture Joel had seen all morning, and the normalcy of it — Paul cleaning his glasses in the middle of the worst day of all their lives — made something shift in Joel's chest that he chose not to examine.

"You think she's putting it on?" Nate said.

"I think I don't know," Joel said. "And I think that's exactly what she wants us to think."

"Or she's just a small-town detective from Becker County who doesn't deal with this very often and brought a casserole because that's what you do up here," Tommy said. "That's also a possibility. Not everything's a strategy."

"Both could be true," Doug said, and everyone turned because Doug talking unprompted was an event. He was still on the couch, still in the hoodie, but his phone was facedown on the cushion beside him. "She could be exactly what she looks like and still be — I don't know. Paying attention." He paused. "Every time someone corrected her, she wrote it down. She's been wrong about five or six things since she walked in, and she wrote down every single correction. That might be nothing."

Joel looked at Doug for a long moment. Doug looked at his own hands.

"OK," Paul said, pulling the room back. "So here's what I think. We answer her questions honestly, we don't volunteer anything beyond what she asks—"

"I already said that," Joel said.

"—and we don't contradict each other. If she asks about the timeline, we keep it simple. Dinner, draft, drinks, bed. Martin was alive when we turned in, dead when Nate checked on him. That's the truth and that's what we tell her."

"Why did Nate check on him?" Cliff asked.

Everyone looked at Nate.

"I wanted to make a side bet," Nate said. "About his first-round pick. It was a — we had a thing, a running thing about—"

"At two in the morning?" Cliff's eyebrows rose. The gesture was calibrated — just enough surprise to plant the question without making an accusation. Joel thought: this is why nobody in this room fully trusts you, Cliff. Because you're always positioning, even when you don't need to.

"We were all drunk," Nate said. "It didn't seem weird at the time. At the time it was just — it was normal. We do that."

"It's fine," Paul said. "Nobody's saying it's weird, Nate."

"Cliff literally just said it was weird."

"I asked a question," Cliff said. "Questions aren't accusations."

"OK, guys," Tommy said, and the coaching voice was on — the halftime voice, the third-quarter-deficit voice, the one designed to cut through panic and redirect. "Can we just — can we not do this right now? Martin's dead. There's a cop upstairs in his bedroom. Can we get through one day without this?"

The refrigerator kicked on with its loud, rhythmic cycling, the sound that had been the background of every midnight kitchen conversation for twenty-five years. From above, Mallick's footsteps moved from one room to another. A floorboard groaned, then another. She was in the hallway.

Joel looked at his five remaining friends and cataloged what he saw the way he'd cataloged a hundred courtroom galleries — Paul's controlled grief, the effort visible in his jaw and his too-clean glasses; Tommy's coiled, pacing energy, the body that couldn't find a position because every position was wrong; Nate's compulsive narration, the machine that wouldn't stop running even when the gears were grinding; Cliff's polished stillness, the composed surface that Joel instinctively didn't trust; Doug's retreat, the hood and the couch and the hands.

He had known these men for twenty-five years. He had drafted fantasy teams with them, gotten drunk with them, stood in their weddings and helped them through their divorces, and right now — sitting in this room where last night they'd argued about roster moves while Martin was still alive, still pouring scotch, still being Martin — Joel was certain of exactly one thing:

Every single one of them was lying about something.

He didn't know what. He didn't know why. But seven years as a public defender gave you a feel for the specific texture of a room where everyone had something to protect. It wasn't about evidence or inconsistencies — it was atmospheric, like the shift in pressure before a storm. The way people chose their words too carefully. The way they made eye contact at the wrong moments or avoided it at the right ones. The way Cliff's calm was too even and Tommy's agitation was too loud and Nate's talking was too much and Doug's silence was too complete.

The problem was that Joel had something to protect too. Not a tampering — he genuinely hadn't touched anything, hadn't moved through the house, hadn't done a single thing between midnight and Nate's knock except lie in the guest bedroom at the far end of the hall staring at the pine ceiling with the particular insomnia that comes from anger you can't discharge. He hadn't been thinking about Martin's health or the sound of footsteps in the hallway. He'd been thinking about Karen. About Martin and Karen. About eleven years of marriage that ended with a conversation at a Panera in Edina where she'd told him she wanted something different, and the eight months since when that something different had apparently been his fraternity brother, who was now dead in the room above their heads while a detective with a casserole and a leather notebook decided whether Joel's clean hands were the hands of an innocent man or a careful one.

He picked up his mug, realized the coffee was cold, and drank it anyway. It tasted like nothing.

"I'm going to sit on the deck," he said.

Nobody stopped him. He went outside and let the screen door close behind him and stood there in the September morning with the lake shining in the low gold light, loons calling somewhere out past the dock, the pine air cool enough that his breath wasn't quite visible but almost. Martin's house. Martin's lake. Martin's weekend that had outlasted Martin.

On the deck railing, someone had left a beer bottle from last night, the label peeled halfway off the way Tommy always peeled them when he was talking, his thick fingers working the paper while his mouth ran. Joel looked at it. The bottle was empty but it still smelled like beer, warm and stale, the ghost of a night that had felt normal while it was happening and would never feel normal again.

From inside, muffled through the screen door, he heard Mallick's footsteps coming down the stairs. She was done upstairs. He heard her voice — warm, chatty, asking one of the deputies something about the cedar paneling in the hallway.

Joel leaned on the railing and looked at the lake and thought: she's either an idiot or the most dangerous person in this house, and I can't tell, and that's the part that should be scaring me.


# Chapter 5

The casserole was still warming. Mallick had asked the tall one — Decker, the insurance man — to put it in the oven at two-fifty before she started with the lawyer, and now it was ticking away in there, the green bean smell curling through the kitchen like a guest who'd arrived too early and was making itself comfortable. She could smell it from the great room where she'd set up at the long pine table, her notebook open, her reading glasses pushed to the end of her nose, and the county's cheapest ballpoint in her right hand.

She'd finished with Beckman. That had been interesting — interesting the way a locked door was interesting. A public defender who knew exactly how much silence was worth. She'd thanked him and let him go and now she was looking at the draft board on the wall. Martin Kessler's picks circled in red marker. Somebody had pressed hard with that marker. She could see the indentation from here, the way the whiteboard surface dimpled under each circle. A man who cared about his fantasy football picks. A man who was dead upstairs in a room that looked like it had been tidied by a committee.

Mallick wrote: *Beckman — clean. Too clean? Or genuinely clean?*

She underlined *genuinely* and drew a small question mark next to it. Then she capped the pen and went to the kitchen.

The oven light was on. She bent to check the casserole through the glass — the cheese was browning nicely along the edges, and the fried onions on top had crisped into those little curls she liked. Her mother's recipe, technically, though Mallick had switched from canned green beans to frozen ones in about 2008 and hadn't told anyone. She straightened up, felt the pull in her lower back that she felt every time she bent at the oven, and poured herself a glass of water from the tap. The water here tasted like iron and pine. Lake house plumbing.

Paul Decker was standing in the doorway of the great room. He'd been waiting. She could tell because he was holding a coffee mug he'd already finished — the handle turned outward, gripped with both hands, the posture of a man who wanted to appear relaxed and had chosen the wrong prop.

"Oh, Mr. Decker," she said. "Is it Decker? I'm so bad with — hold on." She flipped her notebook back a page. "Paul Decker. Insurance, you said?"

"Claims adjuster," he said. "Mutual of Omaha, but the Minneapolis office."

"Claims adjuster." She wrote it down. She'd already written it down twice. "Now that's a job. My neighbor, Gary, he had a tree come down on his garage last March — big old ash, just split right down the middle — and his claims adjuster was just wonderful. Do you do homeowners, or is it more—"

"All lines," Paul said. "Homeowners, auto, commercial. Whatever walks in."

"Whatever walks in. Sure." She pulled out a chair at the kitchen table and gestured to the one across from her. "Do you want coffee? I think there's still some in the pot. It might be a little old by now, but I can make fresh if you—"

"I'm fine."

"You sure? I can't drink old coffee myself. My stomach. But some people prefer it stronger, so." She shrugged and sat down and opened her notebook to a fresh page. "Now, Mr. Decker. Paul. Can I call you Paul?"

"Sure."

"Paul. I just want to go over a couple of things, if that's all right. Nothing formal. I'm just trying to get the picture straight in my head because, honestly, I get confused sometimes with these — when there's a lot of people in a house and everyone's got a different version." She smiled. "I'm sure you understand. In your line of work."

"I do." He sat down across from her. His posture was good — straight-backed, hands flat on the table, the coffee mug set to one side. The posture of a man who'd conducted interviews himself. She noted this.

"So. Last night. You all had the draft — the football thing — and then drinks, the hot tub, and bed. Is that about right?"

"That's about right." He paused. "The draft went until about ten-thirty. Then we were on the deck for a while, maybe until midnight. Martin went to bed before the rest of us. Paul — sorry, I keep — Tommy went up maybe twelve-thirty, and I think I was one of the last ones. Maybe one, one-fifteen."

She wrote the times down. "And you slept where?"

"Upstairs. The guest bedroom on the right side, if you're facing the lake."

"The right side. And Martin was in the master?"

"Yes."

"And that's on the left?"

"That's on the left. At the end of the hall."

She nodded and wrote. "Now, Nate — Mr. Griggs — he said he found Martin at, oh, he said around two. Does that sound right to you?"

"I think it was a little after two. He woke me up. He was — he came to my room and he was standing in the doorway and he said Martin's not breathing, or something like that. Not breathing. I got up immediately."

"And you went to Martin's room?"

"I went to Martin's room. I tried — I did CPR. For several minutes. But he was —" Paul's jaw worked. Not for effect, she thought. She'd seen enough grief to know the mechanics of it, and this was the real thing — the muscle clenching because the words were in the way. "He was already gone. His skin was cool. Not cold, but. He'd been dead a while."

"I'm so sorry," Mallick said, and she meant it, in the way she always meant it, which was genuinely but with her pen still moving. "That must have been awful. I can't imagine. My husband passed in '09 and I wasn't even there for it — he was at work, just dropped — and I still sometimes think about whether it would've been better to be there or not. I go back and forth."

Paul looked at her. The mention of a dead husband had the effect she expected — a softening around his eyes, a slight dropping of the interview posture. People gave her things when she gave them something first. It was transactional and also true. Pete had dropped at work. She did go back and forth.

"I'm sorry," Paul said.

"Oh, it's been a long time now. But you don't forget how it feels." She flipped a page. "Now, when you went in and found Martin — Nate was already there, right? And you said you tried CPR. When you went in, was he on the bed?"

"He was on the bed."

"On his back?"

Paul paused. She watched the pause. In her experience, a pause before a factual answer meant the fact was being measured, not remembered.

"On his side," Paul said. "His left side. Facing the window."

"His left side. Facing the—" She frowned and flipped back in her notebook. "Oh, that's funny. I thought — the deputies' report said he was on his back when they got there this morning. Flat on his back, arms at his sides." She looked up. "But you're saying he was on his left side?"

"He was on his left side when I got there."

"Huh." She wrote it down slowly, her handwriting deliberately large so he could see she was recording it. "So between when you got there at two-something and when the deputies arrived at — what was it, eight-thirty? — he went from his left side to his back."

The silence in the kitchen was thick enough to hear the oven ticking and the old refrigerator starting its next cycle, that grinding mechanical sound that probably woke everyone in the house every forty minutes.

"I moved him," Paul said. "For the CPR. You have to — the person has to be on their back for chest compressions. So I rolled him onto his back."

"Oh, of course." She wrote it down. "That makes perfect sense. CPR. You'd have to, wouldn't you. And the arms at his sides?"

"I positioned him. For the compressions. You put the heel of your hand on the sternum and you—"

"No, sure, I understand. I took a course once. At the YMCA, if you can believe it." She smiled. "Although I think they've changed it since then. When I learned it there was the breathing part, but now I hear they don't—"

"They still recommend rescue breaths for people trained in it," Paul said. The insurance precision was coming through now, the need to have the right figure, the right protocol, the right claim. She could practically hear the form fields filling in. "I was trained. I did compressions and breaths for about four minutes. Then I stopped because there was no response and his — I could tell it wasn't going to work."

"Four minutes. That's very precise."

"I was looking at the clock on the nightstand. The digital one. It said two-fourteen when I started and two-eighteen when I stopped."

"Two-fourteen and two-eighteen." She wrote both numbers down, then drew a small box around them. "See, that's what I mean. Most people would say 'a few minutes.' You've got timestamps. That's your training, I bet."

"Probably."

"My husband was a roofer. When he estimated a job he'd measure to the quarter-inch and then add ten percent for waste. He said people who round up are lying and people who round down are selling you something." She wrote *clock on nightstand — verify position, verify model* in the margin of her notes and then drew a small circle around it.

"Paul, that's really helpful," she said. "I appreciate you being so specific. In my line of work, most people say 'a few minutes' or 'a while,' and that's not always helpful when I'm trying to put a timeline together." She paused, then added, as if it had just occurred to her: "You know, it's a little like claims work, isn't it? Getting the details right."

"I suppose it is."

"Do you mind if I ask — when you left Martin's room, did you close the door?"

"I closed the door."

"And you went back to bed?"

"I went back to bed."

The answer came too fast and too flat, and they both heard it. Paul's hands were still on the table but his thumbs had moved — tucked under his palms, a small compression that she filed away with the body position and the four minutes and the clock on the nightstand. A man who knew exactly how the body was oriented, exactly how long he'd performed CPR, and exactly what he'd touched. A man who'd adjusted a corpse and then gone back to bed and closed the door.

"Well," she said, standing up. "I think that's all I need for now. Would you send in — oh, who's next on my — " She checked her notebook as if she hadn't planned the order three hours ago. "Haywood? Is Mr. Haywood around?"

"Cliff," Paul said. "I think he's on the deck."

"Cliff. That's right. Cliff Haywood. Would you mind? Oh, and Paul — try the casserole if you get hungry. I always make too much."

"Thanks."

"The secret is the fried onions on top. French's. Don't let anybody tell you the fancy ones are better."

Paul nodded and stood and carried his empty coffee mug to the sink and rinsed it. She noted that too — a man who rinsed his mug in a dead friend's house, a man who cleaned up after himself automatically, a man whose instinct when holding a thing was to make sure it showed no trace of him.

---

Mallick moved the interview to the deck. The kitchen smelled too much like casserole now, and she wanted Haywood in a different setting than Decker — people behaved differently depending on where you put them. The kitchen made Decker precise. She suspected the deck would make Haywood expand.

She was right. Haywood came out with his own coffee, a fresh cup, and pulled one of the Adirondack chairs around to face her at a companionable angle rather than across from her. He'd changed his shirt since this morning — she'd seen him in a navy polo at seven, and now it was a sage green linen thing, open at the collar, the kind of shirt that cost more than it looked like it cost. She knew the type. Her second cousin sold commercial real estate in Fargo and dressed the same way — the studied casualness of a man who was always, at some level, making a pitch.

"Detective, I appreciate you taking the time with all of us," Cliff said, settling into the chair. "I know this can't be easy, coordinating — what is it, six interviews? Seven, if you count—"

"Oh, I lose count." She laughed. "I'm not very organized, honestly. My sergeant says I'd lose my own head if it wasn't attached, which, you know, he's probably not wrong." She flipped through her notebook, passing Decker's pages. "Now, you're Cliff — Cliff Haywood — and you do..." She trailed off and looked at him expectantly.

"Real estate development. Commercial and residential, mostly outer-ring suburbs. Plymouth, Maple Grove, that area."

"Oh, nice. Good market out there?"

"It's got its challenges." The smile was practiced. Not fake — practiced. The smile of a man who'd learned early that the appearance of confidence was a fungible commodity. "But we're positioned well."

"Positioned well. I love that." She wrote it down. She wrote down everything. Most of it was useless, but the act of writing put people on a tape delay — they watched her pen and forgot to watch her eyes. "So. Last night. The draft, the drinks, the hot tub. Same as everyone else?"

"Same as everyone else. Although I turned in a little earlier than the rest — maybe eleven-thirty? The hot tub does me in. My back."

"Oh, I hear you. My back is terrible. You know, this house — the deck is beautiful, by the way. Is this original cedar?"

"I think Martin had it redone maybe six, seven years ago. He mentioned it."

"It's gorgeous. And the view." She looked out at the lake. The September light was hitting the water at an angle that turned it amber near the shore and steel-gray farther out. A breeze was coming off the water, warm enough for September but with something underneath it — the first real suggestion that October was a thought the weather was having. Two loons were working the shallows by the dock, and she could hear one of them calling, that sound like someone laughing at a joke they didn't want to be laughing at. "I'd never leave."

"It's something."

"Did Martin use the place a lot? Year-round, or just—"

"Mostly summer and early fall. The draft weekend, obviously. Some winter weekends, I think, for ice fishing. He wasn't up here full-time."

"And he kept it nice. The inside — I was admiring the study. That little room off the hallway, is that the—"

"That's not the study," Cliff said. "The study is two doors past the half bath. Farther down the hall."

She stopped writing. Looked up. Let the look hold for exactly the amount of time it took Cliff's expression to adjust — a quarter-second of openness replaced by the smooth surface reforming.

"Two doors past the half bath," she repeated. "I thought it was that first room, the one with the—"

"No, that's a coat closet. Or a linen closet, I'm not sure what Martin used it for. The study is the next one. It's got a desk, bookshelves, a painting of the lake."

"A painting of the lake. Of course." She smiled and wrote it down and didn't look up. "Thank you for that. I always get turned around in these big houses. My place is eight hundred square feet, so anything with more than one hallway—" She laughed. "You've been to the study, then? During your visits?"

The pause was shorter than Decker's had been. Haywood was faster at recalibrating. "Over the years, sure. Martin and I had some business together. He'd show me paperwork sometimes. It's not — I mean, we're friends. Were friends. People go in each other's rooms."

"Of course they do. I wasn't suggesting — oh, goodness, no. I was just impressed you knew the layout so well. I've been to my sister's cabin a dozen times and I still can't find the bathroom." She shook her head, self-deprecating, and flipped to a new page. "Now. When you went to bed last night — eleven-thirty, you said — did you hear anything during the night? Anybody moving around, doors, that sort of thing?"

Cliff took a sip of his coffee. The sip lasted a beat too long. "I'm a heavy sleeper. Once I'm out, I'm out."

"Oh, I envy that. I haven't slept through a whole night since about 2004. The cats wake me up. I've got two, and they—" She stopped herself, waving a hand. "Sorry. Nobody needs to hear about my cats. So you didn't hear anything. And you woke up — when? What time this morning?"

"Six-fifteen, six-thirty. Around there."

"And Martin was — you knew already? Somebody told you?"

"Paul told me. He was in the kitchen when I came down. He said Martin was — he said Martin died during the night."

"Paul told you in the kitchen. Was anyone else there?"

"Tommy. Tommy was there. And I think Nate was somewhere — I could hear him on the phone, maybe in the other room."

She wrote it all down. Haywood had relaxed again. The study correction sat in her notebook like a seed she'd water later.

"Cliff, I really appreciate this. You've been so helpful. Can I ask you one more thing?"

"Of course."

"The casserole I brought — is it still in the oven? I can't remember if I asked someone to take it out."

"I think Paul put it in."

"Bless him. I hope it's not burning. My casseroles tend to — well, they're better a little overdone, honestly." She stood up. "I'll go check on it. Would you mind sending in — I wanted to talk to the quiet one. The one who was in the basement. What's his name — Whitney?"

"Whitfield. Doug."

"Doug Whitfield. That's right. Is he around?"

"He's been down by the water. The dock."

"The dock. Okay. Well, if you see him, would you ask him to find me when he's ready? No rush."

Haywood nodded and stood and shook her hand — he shook her hand, which nobody else had done, which told her everything she already suspected about the way he moved through the world. A handshake to end an interview. A deal closed. His grip was firm and calibrated, the handshake of a man who'd read an article about handshakes and absorbed it into his bones.

She returned the grip and smiled and went inside. On the way through the great room she passed the big one — Renaldi, the coach — coming out of the half bath. He was drying his hands on his jeans and he looked at her the way you'd look at a car coming the wrong way down a one-lane road: total focus, no expression. She said "Oh, excuse me" and he said "Yeah" and moved past her with his shoulders turned slightly, the way a large man does when he doesn't want to touch you in a hallway, and she thought about that for the rest of the afternoon — the shoulders, the single syllable, the hands still damp on the denim.

---

The casserole was fine. A little dark on the left side where the oven ran hot, but the structural integrity was sound, the green beans still distinct under the cheese. She pulled it out with a dish towel — she'd forgotten to bring an oven mitt, which was typical — and set it on the stovetop to cool.

Then she stood at the kitchen counter with her notebook open and her reading glasses pushed up on her forehead and she was not folksy anymore.

Decker knew the body's position. Left side, facing the window. The deputies found the body on its back with arms at his sides. Decker said he'd rolled Martin for CPR and positioned the arms, which was perfectly reasonable and also meant that between two and eight-thirty in the morning, Paul Decker had been close enough to the body to reposition it and had chosen to leave it looking like a man who'd died peacefully in his sleep, which was not how people looked after four minutes of chest compressions. She'd seen CPR aftermath. There should have been bruising on the sternum, displaced clothing, maybe vomit. The body upstairs was tidy. Too tidy for a man who'd been worked on.

Haywood knew the layout. Not just knew it — corrected her with the precision of a man who'd walked that hallway recently and with purpose. Two doors past the half bath. He hadn't said "I think it's" or "if I remember right." He'd said it the way you describe a route you drove this morning. Haywood was in Martin's study, and he was in there recently enough that the muscle memory of the walk was still warm.

And then there was Whitfield.

She'd seen him twice today and he hadn't said more than six words either time. Once in the kitchen this morning when the deputies were getting names — he'd given his name and his phone number in a voice so quiet the deputy had asked him to repeat both. Once on the deck an hour ago, when she'd passed him on her way to check the hot tub and said good morning and he'd said "morning" and looked at her and then looked at the lake and she'd felt the look land somewhere behind his eyes and stay there. Not evasive. Not guilty. Something else. A man who was watching the world from a distance he hadn't chosen.

He wasn't on her list yet. She'd interview him, probably tomorrow, and she'd be interested to see what a man who spoke six words in a day corrected her about. Sometimes the quiet ones gave you nothing. Sometimes the quiet ones gave you the thing the loud ones were burying.

Mallick sat down at the kitchen table and turned to a fresh page in her notebook. The refrigerator kicked on with its grinding cycle. The loons were calling from the lake — that wavering, unhinged sound that tourists found beautiful and that she'd grown up hearing as just the sound of evening in this part of the state.

She wrote:

*Decker — knows body position. Moved body. Says CPR. Scene too clean for CPR. Wiped?*

*Haywood — knows study. Two doors past bath. Definite. Was in there. When? Business with Kessler. Financial? Check debt/estate.*

*Beckman — lawyer. Won't explain argument. Didn't tamper (only clean hands?). Angry. Not at me. At Kessler? At himself?*

*Griggs — talked too much. Interview Monday. Will crack himself.*

*Whitfield — talked too little. Interview Monday. Won't crack at all.*

*Renaldi — haven't gotten to yet. Loud one. Coached voice. Shoulders turned in hallway. Note: was in the bathroom when I arrived. Long bathroom visit. Hands damp three hours later. Check.*

She drew a line under the names and then sat for a moment looking at the list. Six men. Five of them claimed they went to bed, slept through the night, and woke up to the news that their friend was dead. One of them — Beckman — was too angry to tamper and too proud to explain. The other five had each, as far as she could tell, moved through this house in the small hours of a Saturday morning doing things they now needed to explain away or avoid mentioning entirely.

She uncapped her pen and then capped it again and held it between her fingers the way she'd held cigarettes for twenty years before she quit — the muscle memory of thinking.

She thought about her mother's rule for casseroles, which was also her mother's rule for crime scenes: if you can see where one set of hands has been, you can feed a family. If you can't tell who touched what, somebody brought the wrong dish.

Five men. A body repositioned, a scene wiped, a study visited, a bathroom occupied, and — she was guessing now, but the guesses were the part of the job she was good at — at least one or two more things she hadn't found yet. A house rearranged between midnight and dawn by people who claimed to be sleeping.

Mallick looked at the draft board across the room. Martin Kessler's picks in red. Everybody else's in blue or black or green. She thought about the dead man's handwriting pressing into that whiteboard, the way the red circles dimpled the surface, and she thought about how you could learn a lot about a man from how hard he pressed.

She tore the page out of her notebook — she always tore the assessment pages out, folded them into her blazer pocket where they couldn't be read alongside the interview notes — and stood up. The casserole was cooling. She'd cut it later and offer some to whoever wanted it, because her mother was right: you didn't show up empty-handed, even to the worst places. Especially to the worst places.

She washed her water glass at the sink the way Decker had washed his mug, and she thought: everyone in this house is cleaning up after themselves. The question is what they're cleaning.

Outside, the late afternoon light was on the lake, turning it copper. She could see Whitfield sitting alone on the dock bench, sixty or seventy yards away through the pines and birch. He was sitting perfectly still, looking at the water. He might have been praying. He might have been sleeping with his eyes open. He might have been doing what she was doing — counting the things that didn't add up and coming up short of any total that made sense.

She put her notebook in her blazer pocket alongside the torn page and went to find her phone. She needed to call the BCA in St. Paul and ask about expediting the autopsy. She needed the cause of death, because everything she'd seen in this house said these men were hiding something, and she couldn't figure out what it was until she knew whether what they were hiding from was a murder or a natural death or just the specific unbearable thing that happened when your friend died in the night and you found yourself, at two in the morning, doing something with your hands that you couldn't explain.

The refrigerator cycled off. The house went quiet. Somewhere upstairs, the third step from the top of the stairs creaked — someone going up or coming down, she couldn't tell — and she wrote that down too.


# Chapter 6

Nate Griggs had been holding his phone toward the basement ceiling for three minutes, tilting it at different angles like a man trying to divine water from a satellite that had better things to do. The search page loaded in pieces: Google logo first, then a white void, then the top result emerging letter by letter like a telegram from someone who wanted him to suffer.

*Can police recover deleted voicemails from a phone*

The first result was a legal advice subreddit, which was exactly the kind of source a serious person would not consult and exactly the kind of source Nate was going to read with the focus of a man studying his own biopsy results. He thumbed it open. The page loaded in strips, each paragraph arriving on its own schedule.

*Depends on carrier. Verizon keeps metadata (call time, duration, number) for 1-2 years. Actual voicemail audio is generally retained on their servers for 30-40 days after the user deletes it. Law enforcement can access recordings through a subpoena or court order—*

Thirty to forty days. He was on day one.

Nate sat down on the pull-out sofa. The mattress was thin enough that the metal frame bit into the backs of his thighs through the fabric. Doug's duffel bag sat on the other pull-out, zipped tight as a vacuum seal — Doug packed like a man trying to prove he took up no space in the world. The lake was audible through the small casement windows, a steady lapping that Nate's brain, helpfully, converted into a countdown.

The voicemail. The goddamn voicemail.

He'd left it around eight on Friday evening, after the draft was done and they were all on the deck with beers and Martin was doing his thing where he pretended to be above trash talk by simply not responding to trash talk, which was Martin's most infuriating move and had been for twenty-five years. Nate had taken his phone to the basement bathroom — the one with the shower that smelled like lake water and old PVC — and he'd called Martin's cell and left a voicemail because that was their thing, the bit, leaving long ranting voicemails about each other's draft picks because who leaves voicemails anymore, that was the joke, they were the last seven guys in America who used voicemail on purpose—

Six guys. Six.

He'd gone up to Martin's room two hours ago, thinking he could check the phone one more time, make sure the deletion had taken, see if there was a "recently deleted" folder he'd missed in the dark at three-thirty in the morning. The nightstand had been bare. Martin's phone, charger cord, watch, wallet — all of it bagged and taken by the county deputies who'd arrived at seven. The bedroom door was shut now with a strip of yellow tape across it that looked like it came from a hardware store. Martin's phone was in an evidence room somewhere in Detroit Lakes, and Nate couldn't check whether the deletion had actually worked, couldn't verify that the voicemail wasn't sitting in some purgatory folder waiting for a detective with a subpoena to bring it back to life.

Which brought him to the Quora answer.

*Great question! Even after a user deletes a voicemail, carriers typically retain the audio file for a minimum of 30 days. Standard law enforcement procedure involves serving a preservation request followed by a subpoena, with typical turnaround of 5-10 business days—*

Five business days. Next Friday. Mallick could have the voicemail playing through her car speakers by next Friday.

The voicemail. What he'd actually said. He could hear it in his own voice, the podcast cadence, the buildup rhythm he used when he was doing his weekly "Roster Autopsy" segment, and the word *autopsy* was another one he wished he could take back from the universe:

*Kessler. Martin. My guy. I've been staring at your roster for fifteen minutes and I am composing your obituary right now. This team is dead. You are dead to me, Kessler. Dead. You took Addison in the fourth — the fourth, Martin — when Nico Collins was right there, just sitting there, staring at you, begging you to take him, and you went with a guy who had six targets last week. Six. That's not a wide receiver, that's a bystander at the scene of a crime. And then — AND THEN — you took the Vikings defense in the seventh round. A defense. In the seventh. I'm at your funeral, man. I am standing at the podium delivering the eulogy and I'm telling people, 'He was a good man. A generous man. And he took a defense in the seventh round and deserved everything he got.' And I haven't even gotten to the Pacheco pick, which — I'll save that for the pod, because I want to do a full segment on how a man murders his own season in front of six witnesses.
