Your roster is a corpse, Kessler. A corpse. I'm performing the autopsy Monday morning. Rest in peace, your playoff hopes, 2024 to 2024.*

It went on. It went on for another ninety seconds after that. He'd riffed on Martin's backup quarterback as a "death wish," called the bench "a graveyard," and signed off with "this message will self-destruct, much like your season and your legacy." He'd said *dead* four times, *kill* twice, *murder* once, *funeral* once, *obituary* once, *corpse* twice, and *rest in peace* at the end like a sign-off for a man who was going to be dead in six hours.

Nate put his phone face-down on the mattress and pressed his palms against his eyes. He could still see the afterimage of the search results through his eyelids. Thirty to forty days. The voicemail was out there, sitting on a server in some Verizon data center in Iowa or wherever they kept their servers, and he had sent it there himself when he'd deleted it from Martin's phone in the dark at three-thirty in the morning, thinking that was sufficient, because he was a fantasy sports podcaster with twelve hundred subscribers, not a person with a working knowledge of telecommunications infrastructure.

What he'd done at three-thirty in the morning: he'd gone into Martin's room, where Martin was still in the bed, still dead, his arm hanging off the edge at the angle that had made Nate check on him in the first place. He'd picked up Martin's phone from the nightstand — the screen protector cracked in one corner, the home screen still a photo of the lake at sunset — and his hands had been shaking so badly he'd had to use both thumbs to navigate the voicemail app. He'd found the message, his own name next to it, 8:17 PM, three minutes forty-one seconds. He'd pressed delete. The phone had asked him to confirm. He'd confirmed. And then he'd stood there in the dark room next to his friend's body and thought, with the involuntary clarity of a man who cannot turn off the part of his brain that formats experience into content: *this would make an incredible episode.*

He hated himself for that thought. He hated himself with specificity and precision for that thought, and yet even the self-hatred had the shape of a podcast segment — the confessional beat, the "getting real with the audience" moment, the producer-Nate inside his head telling talent-Nate to lean into the vulnerability because vulnerability was engagement. He processed grief by narrating it. He processed fear by narrating it. He processed the fact of his friend dead in a lake house in Becker County, Minnesota, by reaching for the rhythms of a show that had a 2.3-star rating on Apple Podcasts and generated four hundred dollars a month before Mia's student loans, and if that wasn't the saddest thing about Nathan Griggs at age forty-six then he didn't want to know what was, because knowing would also become content.

His phone buzzed. A text from Mia, timestamped six hours ago: *How's the weekend going? Miss you.*

He hadn't answered. He hadn't answered because what was he going to say — *Martin's dead and there's a detective upstairs who brought a casserole and I committed a felony at three in the morning*? He left the phone on the mattress.

The ceiling above him creaked. Someone walking through the kitchen. The old refrigerator shuddered into its loud cycle — a compressor grinding to life with the shuddering complaint it made every forty minutes, a rhythm Nate had been unconsciously tracking for three days because that was what his brain did, it cataloged patterns and produced commentary and converted raw experience into material. Even now. Even here. He was filing the refrigerator's cycle under "ambient texture" and Martin's death under "season-ending injury" and his own terror under "character development," and he could not make himself stop.

He needed to go upstairs. He needed better signal. He needed to find out if Verizon kept the actual audio or just the call metadata, because if it was just metadata they'd see a three-minute call from Nate's number to Martin's at 8:17 PM and that was nothing, that was friends calling friends, but if they had the audio file with Nate saying *you are dead to me, Kessler* and *I'm at your funeral*—

He stood up too fast. Head rush. Six beers and a scotch last night, maybe ninety minutes of sleep total, and twelve hours of adrenaline working on him like a stimulant he hadn't consented to. His "Griggs on the Grid" cap was on the floor by the sofa, the logo that Paul once called "the saddest thing in podcasting." He picked it up, put it on, took it off. The cap was a prop, part of the show, and the show was the last thing he needed right now, but his hand wanted it the way his mouth wanted to narrate and his brain wanted to riff — it was all the same muscle, and the muscle did not know how to be still.

He went upstairs. The third step creaked. He always forgot about the third step and he always stepped on it, twenty-five years of stepping on that step, and now there would not be a twenty-sixth year of anything in this house.

Paul was in the kitchen, standing at the counter with a glass of water and the particular expression of a man trying to project that he was simply a man drinking water and not a man who had rearranged a crime scene nine hours ago. Nate recognized the expression because his own face was wearing something similar.

"Hey," Nate said.

"Hey." Paul took a sip. "You doing all right?"

"Yeah. No. I mean — yeah." He held up his phone. "Trying to get a hold of Mia, but the signal down there is — it's nothing. I don't know how Martin dealt with it." He heard himself use past tense and felt the podcaster in his skull whisper *great moment, the casual past tense, very usable*, and he wanted to walk into the lake.

"Deck's better," Paul said. "Or up by the road."

"Right. The deck. Yeah."

Nate didn't move toward the deck. He stood in the kitchen doorway and looked at Paul drinking his water, and he thought about asking Paul if Paul knew what carrier Martin used, and whether Paul thought voicemail servers retained audio files after deletion, and whether Paul had any professional opinion on the legal distinction between deleting a voicemail and destroying evidence, and then he thought about not asking any of those things, and then he thought about the segment he could do on the universal impulse to confess when you haven't technically done anything wrong, the way guilt manufactures itself out of proximity and bad timing, and then he thought about Martin, who was dead, who was actually dead, whose body was at a morgue in Detroit Lakes being examined by someone who would determine cause of death and whether the scene had been tampered with—

"I'm gonna go to the deck," Nate said.

"OK," Paul said.

The deck was getting cold. September in northern Minnesota did that — the afternoon had been golden and warm, the lake flat and bright, and then the sun dropped behind the white pines and suddenly you needed a hoodie and your breath was considering becoming visible. Nate sat in one of the Adirondack chairs and held his phone out and watched the signal bars flicker between one and two.

He loaded a new search: *Verizon voicemail retention policy law enforcement subpoena*

The results confirmed what the Quora answer had promised. All major carriers retained voicemail audio for a minimum of thirty days. Law enforcement access required a subpoena with a standard turnaround of five to ten business days. One article from an industry blog noted that some carriers had begun retaining data for up to ninety days as part of updated compliance protocols.

Ninety days.

Nate put the phone in his lap and looked at the lake. A loon called from across the water, the sound arriving across the flat surface with the clarity of something designed to be heard at distance. He thought about how the voicemail would sound played back in a courtroom. He thought about how it would sound on a true crime podcast — not his podcast, a real one, one with listeners, one that would play the recording with the key phrases captioned on screen:

*"You are dead to me, Kessler. Dead."*

*"I'm at your funeral, man."*

*"Your roster is a corpse."*

And a narrator, in that measured true-crime voice that every podcast had now: *Six hours later, Martin Kessler was found dead in his bed. The voicemail was discovered missing from his phone.*

Nate could hear the episode. He could hear the ad breaks. He could hear the Reddit thread.

The sliding glass door opened behind him. He flinched hard enough to knock his phone off the armrest.

"Mr. Griggs?" It was Mallick. She was holding a coffee mug and smiling like a woman who'd run into a neighbor at the co-op. "I was hoping we could have a little chat, if you're not in the middle of something. Oh — did you drop your phone? These chairs, they're beautiful but the arms are so narrow."

---

She'd set up at the kitchen table. Her leather notebook was open, her reading glasses were on the beaded chain around her neck, and the green bean casserole she'd brought that morning sat on the counter behind her, still covered in foil, untouched by anyone because nobody was going to eat a casserole brought by a detective to the house where their friend had just died. Also because it was green bean casserole and nobody actually liked green bean casserole, which was a thought Nate cataloged and filed under *the kind of observation your brain produces when it's using every available neuron to avoid thinking about the thing you can't stop thinking about*.

"Sit, sit," Mallick said, pulling out the chair across from her. "I'm so sorry to bother you — I know this has been just an awful day. Can I get you anything? I think there's still coffee in the — oh, shoot. I think I turned it off. Did I turn it off? I'm always doing that at the station, Debbie gets so mad at me."

"I'm good," Nate said. He sat down. The chair legs scraped on the floor and the sound was too loud for the room.

"So." She put her reading glasses on and looked at her notebook. "Nathan Griggs. You go by Nate?"

"Nate. Yeah. Just Nate."

"And you mentioned you're a podcaster? That's so neat. What's the show — 'Griggs on the Go'?"

"'The Grid.' 'Griggs on the Grid.' It's a fantasy football show — draft strategy, weekly matchups, start-sit decisions, that sort of thing."

"Oh, I'm sorry — 'the Grid.' Like the football field? The lines?"

"Sort of. More like the roster grid, the player — it's a spreadsheet thing, kind of." He watched her write something in her notebook. She'd gotten the name wrong and he'd corrected her and she'd written something down and Joel had told them all, two hours ago, that she did this with everyone, the wrong detail followed by the correction followed by the note.

"How fun," Mallick said. "My nephew Tyler does something like that, I think. The thing where you pick the players?"

"That's it. Yeah. So you draft real NFL players onto a fake roster, and then their real-game stats generate points for your team, and it's a whole — I mean, it's a huge industry. Thirty-three million Americans play. I've done a twelve-part series on the market size alone, not that — anyway." He was doing it. He was podcasting at a detective. He could feel the segment structure underneath his sentences, the setup-context-payoff rhythm that was as automatic as breathing, and Mallick was sitting there with her coffee and her notebook and her reading glasses and she was letting him go, which was the worst thing she could have done, because Nate Griggs in front of a permissive audience was a car rolling downhill with the parking brake off.

"Thirty-three million," Mallick said. "My goodness. And you all came up here to do that? To pick your teams?"

"Twenty-five years. Same weekend, same house, same guys. Martin set it up our first year out of college and it just — it became the thing. The one thing nobody missed." He stopped. "Well. Martin's the one who can't — who didn't." He stopped again. He was terrible at this. He was specifically, measurably terrible at talking about death in a way that wasn't a bit, because death had always been a bit for him — the weekly "Roster Autopsy" segment, the "Season Obituaries" episode in January, the "Rest in Peace, Your Playoff Hopes" tagline that fourteen people had bought on a T-shirt.

"Twenty-five years," Mallick said, and it sounded warm rather than evidentiary. "That's real loyalty. And how did the draft go? Thursday night?"

"Good. It was fun. The usual — guys arguing over picks, Tommy yelling, Joel trying to rules-lawyer every trade. Cliff doing Cliff stuff where he offers you something that sounds great until you realize you just gave up your first-round pick for a backup tight end and a promise."

"And Mr. Kessler? Did he have a good draft?"

Nate laughed before he could stop it — reflexive, genuine — and then heard it land in the kitchen and it was wrong, a man laughing about the draft performance of a man who was in a morgue. "No. He had a terrible draft. Martin always had a terrible draft and then somehow made the playoffs anyway, which — I've actually done a statistical analysis on the pod, his projected finish versus his actual finish over twenty-five years, and the variance is—"

"Oh, that's thorough. What made it so bad? His draft?"

Here was where Nate could feel it coming. The way you feel the back end of a car start to slide on a wet road — your hands still on the wheel, your foot already knowing the brake won't help, the physics of the situation decided before you got involved. Because the things that made Martin's draft terrible were the things Nate had ranted about in the voicemail. They were the same picks, the same complaints, the same language. And his mouth was already open and his podcaster brain was already cueing the segment and the only defense he had was silence, and silence was the one tool that Nate Griggs had never in forty-six years learned to use.

"He reached for guys. He always reached. He took Addison in the fourth when there was — look, there was clear value sitting there on the board, and he went with the name he recognized instead of the guy the numbers said to take. And then a defense in the seventh, which is — that's a cardinal sin. You don't draft a defense until the last two rounds. Everyone knows this. And the kicker in the tenth — I mean, we all gave him shit about it. That's the whole point. The draft's not just about picking players, it's about—" He caught himself. His brain had been accelerating toward *the trash talk is the game, the insults are the love language, when I said his team was dead that was just—* and he swerved. "—it's about the arguing. The competition. It's the only time most of us see each other."

Mallick nodded, sipping coffee. "Now, you mentioned you record things for your show. Were you recording Thursday night?"

"Parts of it. Not — it's not a hidden camera situation. Ambient draft audio, reactions, the group ripping each other. I always get some tape for the pod. Martin was fine with it. Everyone was fine with it." He was explaining things she hadn't asked about, defending a position no one had attacked, which was what he did when the anxiety was running ahead of the conversation by three or four sentences.

"Oh, that's wonderful. We might want to have a listen to that, if you wouldn't mind."

"Sure. Absolutely. It's just the draft. It's just us being us."

"Great." She made a note. "And your phone — was that the only way you'd be in touch with Mr. Kessler? Texting, calling, that kind of thing?"

"Yeah, texts mostly. We've got a group chat that's been going for — I mean, years. Thousands of messages. Draft talk, trades, the league standings. The group chat is basically the league and the league is basically the group chat."

"Mm-hmm. And phone calls?"

"Less. We're all texters mostly. Sometimes you'd call to say come out to the deck, or ask somebody to bring more ice up from the basement, that kind of thing."

"So no calls to Mr. Kessler that Friday? The day before?"

The question arrived clean and Nate could feel every door in it, the three or four ways he could answer that would be short, functional, and safe: *Maybe, I don't remember. Probably a quick one. Nothing important.* He had clean exits and his mouth walked past all of them because Nate's mouth did not take direction from the part of his brain that understood consequences.

"I mean — yeah, probably, at some point? We were all here, you call people. And voicemails, that's — we do this thing. A voicemail thing. Where you leave long, stupid voicemails about each other's picks, because nobody leaves voicemails anymore, right? So it's this ironic — it's a bit. Commentary on the medium. You leave a three-minute rant about someone's terrible roster because the format itself is funny, because voicemail is basically a dead—" He heard it, the word *dead* arriving in the sentence like a siren, and he tried to steer around it. "—a thing nobody uses. So we use it. As a joke."

"That is funny," Mallick said. She was looking at him with the warm, full attention of a woman who had absolutely nowhere to be. She was not writing anything, which was either because what he was saying didn't matter or because she was letting him build speed, and he didn't know which was worse.

"And the language — this is the thing I want to explain, because it sounds — if you heard it without context, the language we use, it's — you say things like 'your team's done,' 'you're finished,' 'rest in peace your season,' and that's not — when I said — look, when you say a guy's roster is dead, you don't mean—"

He stopped. His hands were frozen mid-gesture, both of them, in the air between him and the detective, shaped around a word he was trying to take back. He'd said *when I said*. Not *when you say*. Not *when someone says*. *When I said.* Past tense. First person. A man describing a thing he had done.

Mallick set her coffee mug down on the table. She picked up her pen.

"I'm sorry," she said. Her voice was the same voice it had been, the neighborly voice, the my-nephew-plays-that voice. "When you said — his roster was dead? To Mr. Kessler?"

"That's just how we — everyone says that—"

"Oh, sure, sure. I'm just — I want to make sure my notes are right. I didn't have any messages from you to Mr. Kessler that evening. But you're saying there was a voicemail?"

The kitchen was quiet. The refrigerator was between cycles. Through the window above the sink, Nate could see the last light on the lake, flat and amber and indifferent to what was happening at this table. He looked at Mallick's notebook, open to a page covered in her handwriting, small and even, and he thought about the eleven other guys at Hennepin County Public Defender who'd sat across a table from a client and said *don't talk to the police, just don't talk*, and he thought about how none of those clients had been fantasy football podcasters whose professional skill set was talking and whose personal pathology was talking and whose response to every stimulus the world had ever offered them was to open their mouth and narrate it until the stimulus became content.

"I left a voicemail," Nate said. "Friday night. On his phone. It was — I was doing a bit on his draft. How bad his picks were. And the language, it's — I said things like 'you're dead to me' and 'I'm at your funeral' and 'your roster is a corpse,' and those are all just — that's just how you talk about fantasy football. That's the dialect. Thirty-three million people talk like that."

"I understand," Mallick said. She was writing.

"And I deleted it."

He said it fast, the way you'd yank a splinter, with no strategy and no lead-up, because Nate Griggs had never been capable of strategic silence and wasn't going to start now. He was a man who narrated his own life, who turned every experience into a segment, who had built an entire career — such as it was, such as it barely was — on the principle that talking was the same as thinking and thinking was the same as understanding and understanding was the same as being safe.

"I went to his room. After we found him. Early, like three-thirty in the morning. And I went on his phone and I deleted the voicemail. Because I heard it back in my head after he died and it was — I'm saying 'you're dead to me' and 'I'm at your funeral,' and that's just sports, that's just the game, but if someone listened to it who didn't know—"

"Without the fantasy football context," Mallick said.

"Yes. Exactly. That's exactly it. Without context, it sounds like — I mean, it would sound insane. So I went in and deleted it. And that's — that's what I did."

Mallick's pen stopped moving. She looked at what she'd written, then looked at him over her reading glasses.

"Mr. Griggs, I just want to make sure I have this right. You entered Mr. Kessler's room after he was found deceased, you accessed his phone, and you deleted a voicemail you had left earlier that evening. Is that accurate?"

Put like that — her words, her rhythm, her clean restatement stripped of every piece of context Nate had spent five minutes building — it sounded like what it was. A man going into a dead man's room and tampering with a dead man's phone. The three-minute riff on draft picks and the twenty-five years of voicemail tradition and the thirty-three million Americans who talked the same way and the entire elaborate architecture of explanation that Nate had constructed, all of it collapsed into one sentence in a detective's notebook.

"It was a fantasy football voicemail," Nate said. His voice was smaller now. The performance was over — the pacing gone, the hand gestures gone, the building rhythm gone. The sentences were short and had nowhere to go. "Three minutes. Just me being an idiot about his picks. That's all it was."

Mallick closed her notebook. Not abruptly — she closed it the way you'd set down a book at the end of a chapter, the bookmark already placed, the stopping point chosen. She took off her reading glasses and let them hang on the beaded chain.

"Well," she said. "I really do appreciate you telling me that, Nate. Thank you."

She used his first name. She hadn't used his first name before. He wasn't sure whether that was better or worse and he couldn't ask because asking would also become information, and everything he said to this woman became information, and the only way to stop giving her information was to stop talking, and stopping was the one thing he had never learned to do.

"I think we might need to follow up on a few things," she said. She stood and took her mug to the sink. "Oh — can I warm you up some coffee? Shoot, I did turn it off. I'm sorry about that. I always do that."

Nate sat at the kitchen table. The refrigerator cycled back on, the compressor shuddering to life with its forty-minute complaint, and he stared at the foil-covered casserole on the counter and the draft board still hanging on the great room wall with Martin's picks circled in red marker, and he thought: *if I were doing a segment on this I'd call it something like 'The Voicemail' or 'The Correction' or 'How to Confess to Evidence Tampering in Front of a Detective Who Brought a Casserole,' and it would be about how a man's greatest skill can also be the thing that ends him, and it would be three parts funny and one part something else, and Mia would listen to it and say 'that was a good one, babe,' and twelve hundred other people would listen or they wouldn't, and none of it mattered now because the voicemail was on a server and the confession was in a notebook and Nate Griggs, for the first time in his adult life, had nothing left to say.*

His phone sat in his pocket. He still hadn't called Mia back. Through the kitchen window the lake was going dark, the amber light pulling off the surface in a long retreat, and the loons were starting up across the water, and the house was quiet except for the refrigerator and the settling of old wood and the scratch of a pen from wherever Mallick had gone, making notes about the man who'd talked himself into exactly the place he'd spent all day trying to talk himself out of.


# Chapter 7

Doug took his pills in the basement bathroom with the door locked, which was unnecessary because Nate was upstairs — Doug could hear him through the floorboards, explaining something to someone, the specific pitch of Nate's voice when he wanted you to know he was handling it. The Lexapro first. He shook it out of the bottle and into his palm. Escitalopram oxalate, 10 milligrams, once daily with or without food, though the clinical data showed slightly better absorption with food and Doug hadn't eaten since yesterday's lunch. He swallowed it dry, the chalky scrape of it on the back of his tongue as familiar now as brushing his teeth. Three months. Same length of time as the divorce. The Lexapro arrived two weeks after Steph left, which meant his serotonin reuptake was being inhibited on roughly the same timeline as his marriage's dissolution, and he didn't know if the drug was working or if he was just getting used to the new shape of his life.

The Ativan was the other one. Lorazepam, 0.5 milligrams, as needed. It was needed. He'd sold both of these drugs for years — not these specific generics, the brand-names, back when the brand-names still mattered and pharmaceutical reps still got invited to lunch. He knew the dependency curve on benzodiazepines the way a bartender knows the ABV of draft beers: professional knowledge that had become uncomfortably personal. He swallowed the Ativan with a handful of water from the tap and studied his face in the mirror. Six feet of gaunt. Bags under his eyes that hadn't been there in June. The hoodie hanging off his shoulders like it was still on the hanger. He'd lost twenty-seven pounds since the divorce, which was the most effective diet he'd ever been on and the only one he couldn't recommend to clients.

Both bottles were in his duffel bag now, in the zippered side pocket, under a rolled pair of socks. Not in the shared bathroom upstairs, where he'd stowed them Friday evening when they'd all arrived and dumped their kits on the counter the way they'd been doing for twenty-five years — toothbrushes and razors and Tommy's industrial-sized bottle of Advil and Cliff's moisturizer that nobody was allowed to comment on. Doug's two prescription bottles had sat between Tommy's Advil and a tube of Paul's toothpaste, and they'd sat there for approximately fourteen hours before Martin died and Doug went to get them.

He'd gone upstairs around three in the morning, after Nate had shaken him awake to say Martin was dead. After the group had gathered in the great room. After the shock, the disbelief, Paul doing CPR that wasn't going to work, someone saying call 911, someone else saying wait. After all of that, while the house creaked and footsteps moved in the dark above him and a toilet flushed and a door opened and closed, Doug had gone upstairs. Skipped the third step because it creaked — everyone knew to skip the third step. Opened the medicine cabinet. Took both bottles, held them against his chest so they wouldn't rattle, and came back down to the basement and put them in his duffel.

Not because of Martin. Not because of evidence or detectives or what anything looked like. Because he'd pictured Mallick — the detective with the casserole who hadn't arrived yet but would — opening the medicine cabinet the way detectives in every show he'd ever watched opened medicine cabinets. Finding the bottles with WHITFIELD, DOUGLAS R. on the labels. Escitalopram. Lorazepam. And then asking about them. In front of everyone.

*Are you currently under psychiatric care, Mr. Whitfield?*

That was what he'd pictured. Not the question itself but the six faces behind it. Tommy's careful blankness, the way Tommy went neutral when something uncomfortable surfaced, like a coach who'd just been told a player was in trouble. Joel's quick eyes cataloging the information for future use, because Joel cataloged everything. Paul's measured concern, the insurance-adjuster kindness that was genuine and also an assessment. Nate's inability to not react — his face was a transcript of whatever he was thinking, and what he'd be thinking was *shit, Doug*. And Cliff, Cliff especially, who would file the information away and never mention it again and somehow make the not-mentioning worse than mentioning would have been, because Cliff's silences were transactions too.

Twenty-five years of being the easy one. The one who went along. The one who got more ice, who laughed at the right moments, who volunteered for the beer run. Doug Whitfield: doesn't cause problems. The Lexapro would be a problem. Not because any of them would judge him — they were better than that, mostly — but because it would change what he was in the group, and what he was in the group was the only thing about himself he hadn't had to renegotiate since the divorce.

So he'd taken the bottles. The most innocent tampering of the night. He was almost sure of that. Whatever else was happening in the house — the footsteps, the flushing, the muffled thump of something being set down in a room that should've been empty — Doug's contribution to the collective crime scene renovation was hiding the fact that he was sad.

He turned off the bathroom light and went upstairs.

---

The kitchen was bright with morning sun off the lake. Paul had made coffee, because Paul always made coffee — twenty-five years of mornings at this house and Paul always got up first, always found the filters, always made it too strong because Martin liked it that way and Paul deferred to Martin on things that didn't matter so he could push back on things that did. Martin's scotch — the Lagavulin — still haunted the great room, a smoky trace that seemed to have soaked into the pine paneling over decades of pours. The bottle itself was on the kitchen counter, two fingers left, the cap sitting next to it because Martin never put the cap back on. Martin would never put the cap back on.

Doug took a mug from the cabinet — the blue one with the chip on the handle, the mug nobody else wanted, which was why it was always available — and poured. Black. He'd stopped taking cream when the weight started falling off, not as a diet choice but because the taste of coffee with cream reminded him of weekend mornings with Steph, her feet on the couch, the specific way she held her mug with both hands and blew across the surface even when it wasn't that hot.

Tommy was on the deck. Doug could see him through the sliding glass door — pacing the length of the railing, cracking his knuckles, gripping the cedar and letting go, gripping and letting go. Tommy's body under stress was a coach's body: it needed a play to run, a formation to call, and when there was no play it just moved, burning fuel with no direction. His hands were huge and raw-knuckled and never still. Doug noticed the scar on Tommy's left forearm, the thin white line that caught the morning light when Tommy gripped the railing. Charcoal grill accident, Tommy had told them once, years ago. It looked older than that. But Doug didn't ask questions. Not asking questions was one of the things he contributed.

Joel sat at the kitchen table with nothing in front of him. No coffee, no phone, no book. Just Joel, his sharp features aimed at the middle distance, his jaw working like he was cross-examining a thought and the thought wasn't cooperating. He'd been like this since yesterday — wound tight, the lawyer armor so thoroughly deployed that Doug could practically see the seams. Joel had always been the prickliest of the seven, the one most likely to turn a casual observation into a line of questioning, but this was different. This was a man rehearsing something he wasn't ready to say.

"You eat?" Paul asked Doug, pouring his own refill.

"Not hungry."

"You should eat something."

"Yeah," Doug said. "Probably."

Paul studied him for a second — the adjuster scan, assessing the damage, calculating coverage — then let it go. Paul was good at letting things go. It was the thing Doug appreciated most about him and trusted least, because a man who let things go that easily was a man who was filing them for later.

Nate came down the stairs and straight into a monologue. Something about whether Mallick had asked anyone about the hot tub — had she asked about the hot tub? Because there was a whole — he'd been thinking about it and the hot tub chemicals, the chlorine or bromine or whatever Martin used, and whether that was relevant, whether that was something they should've mentioned or not mentioned, whether the hot tub itself was — and his hands were going, and the podcast cap was slightly crooked, and Doug wondered if Nate knew it was crooked or if the crookedness was part of whatever Nate was always doing.

"Nobody asked about the hot tub," Joel said, without looking up.

"Right, no, I know, but if she does—"

"She won't."

"How do you know that?"

"Because the hot tub isn't relevant."

"Everything's relevant, man. That's the whole — she's asking about everything. She asked me about the coffee maker."

"She asked you about the coffee maker because you were standing next to the coffee maker."

Nate looked at Doug, maybe wanting an ally, maybe just needing another face to talk at. Doug gave him a small nod. Nate accepted it as agreement and kept going, his voice climbing toward the register he used on his podcast when he was building to a take that he thought was important and that his co-host — if Nate had had a co-host, which he didn't, because the last one quit — would have cut off three sentences ago.

Cliff appeared from the hallway, showered, hair combed, wearing a fresh polo and pressed chinos as if he'd packed for a different weekend — one where nobody died and there were restaurant reservations. He poured coffee, thanked Paul for making it, and asked if anyone wanted toast. Cliff offering food was Cliff doing what Cliff always did: managing the room by being useful, positioning himself as the one who held it together, the reasonable presence. Doug watched him pull bread from the bag with his tanned, ring-less fingers — the second divorce had been two years ago but the tan line was finally gone — and thought about how Cliff was the only person in this house whose crisis response was grooming.

"Doug," Cliff said, holding up two slices. "Toast?"

"I'm good."

"You sure? It's sourdough."

"I'm fine."

Cliff put both slices in for himself. The toaster's coils glowed orange.

Doug took his coffee to the great room and sat in the corner of the sectional, close to the window where the morning light came in low and gold off the lake. The draft board was still on the wall. Martin's picks were circled in red marker — the shorthand they'd used for twenty-five years to mark the commissioner's selections. Martin had taken Kelce in the second round, which everyone had groaned about, and Lamar Jackson in the third, which was a reach that had sparked a five-minute argument, and in the seventh round he'd taken a rookie receiver nobody had heard of, and Nate had done a whole bit about it — who is this guy, where did you find this guy, are you drafting him or adopting him. Martin had just smiled. Martin's smile was the thing Doug missed most, which surprised him because he would have guessed he'd miss the conversations — Martin was the only one who asked questions that weren't setups for his own answers. But it was the smile. The gap between Martin's front teeth when he was pleased with himself, which was most of the time, because Martin moved through the world with the specific confidence of a man who'd inherited a lake house and built a life that fit inside it.

Now the lake house had a dead man's things in it and a detective in it and six men in it who couldn't look at the draft board without seeing the red circles and remembering Friday night, when Martin had been alive and confident and wrong about Lamar Jackson in the third round.

Doug drank his coffee and watched the light shift on the water outside. He was good at watching. It was the thing he contributed — not opinions, not comedy, not legal analysis, not money, not coaching authority, not mediation. He watched, and he didn't bother anyone with what he saw. The pharmaceutical sales career had trained him for this: fifteen years of reading rooms, reading doctors' body language during a detail, reading the micro-shift in a nurse practitioner's expression that meant the pitch was dead and it was time to pivot to the sample bag. He just applied those skills to his own friends now, and what he read this morning was not encouraging.

They were all performing. Every one of them. Tommy's performance was physical: movement as alibi, the body saying *I'm just a guy who can't sit still, nothing to see here*. Joel's performance was stillness: the lawyer playing it so carefully he'd turned himself into a statue, which was its own kind of tell. Nate's performance was the most obvious and the most genuinely him — narrating the disaster until it became material, because material was something you could use and using it meant you'd survived it. Paul was performing competence, the way Paul always performed competence, because competence was the thing Paul had instead of feelings he could show in public. Cliff was performing a Sunday morning.

And Doug was performing nothing, which was also a performance — the longest-running one of his life, the one he'd been rehearsing since college, the one where Doug Whitfield sits in the corner and watches and goes along and gets more ice and nobody ever wonders what Doug is thinking because Doug has made not-being-wondered-about into an art form.

He wondered sometimes if it was the depression that made him invisible or if the invisibility had made him depressed, and whether it mattered, and whether the Lexapro had an opinion.

---

Mallick found him on the deck after lunch, sitting in one of the Adirondack chairs, not reading the thriller he'd brought — a book Steph had left on the kitchen counter of the apartment when she came to pick up the last of her winter clothes, and Doug hadn't read a page of it but bringing it to the lake house had felt like something a person who was fine would do.

"Mr. Whitfield? Doug? Do you have a minute?"

He did. He had nothing but minutes.

She sat in the chair next to him, her leather notebook in her lap, her reading glasses on their beaded chain. She was shorter than he'd expected from her voice — five-six, stocky, the gray-streaked bun slightly loose on one side from a morning of conversations. She had a pen that she clicked, and the clicking was probably deliberate, the way everything about Mallick was either deliberate or the performance of not being deliberate, and Doug couldn't tell which.

"I know we chatted briefly yesterday," she said. "I just want to fill in a couple of gaps, if that's all right. How'd you sleep Friday night?"

"Fine."

"All the way through?"

"Yeah."

"Because some of the fellas mentioned hearing noises. Footsteps and whatnot. You're in the basement, right?"

"Yeah."

"Must be quieter down there."

"It is."

She wrote something. The pen's scratch on paper was louder than anything Doug had said.

"And Martin — how'd you two know each other? Were you close?"

"College. Same group." He paused. "We were friends."

"Good friends?"

"He was a good friend to me."

"That's nice. What kind of friend, if you don't mind my asking? Was he the type who checks in, calls on birthdays, that sort of thing?"

Doug thought about it. Martin had called him in July, after Steph. An actual phone call, not a text — Martin was the only person under sixty who still made phone calls that weren't about scheduling. He'd asked Doug how he was doing, and Doug had said fine, and Martin had said *bullshit*, and Doug had almost told him. Almost. He'd gotten as close as "it's been a little rough," which in Doug's vocabulary meant he'd spent three consecutive days on the couch in the apartment in Richfield without showering, eating peanut butter from the jar because grocery shopping required shoes and a destination, and Martin had said *come to the draft*, and Doug had said yeah, and Martin had said *I mean it*, and Doug had said I know.

"He checked in," Doug said.

"That's sweet. So Friday night — after the draft, after the hot tub, after the evening. You went to bed in the basement. What time was that?"

"Midnight, maybe. Twelve-thirty."

"And you slept straight through?"

"Yeah."

"Didn't hear Nate get up? He's down there with you, right?"

"He's a quiet — I didn't hear him. I sleep pretty hard."

Mallick nodded, wrote something else. She looked at him with that patient, warm curiosity that was either genuine neighborly interest or a trap so well-upholstered you'd be comfortable right up until it closed.

"Is there anything else you noticed over the weekend? Anything that seemed out of the ordinary before — before Saturday morning?"

"No."

"Nothing between any of the guys? Arguments, tension, anything like that?"

Doug could have said something. About Joel — Joel's white-knuckled silence at the draft table, the way he'd watched Martin all evening like he was preparing closing arguments. About Tommy's jittery energy that went beyond his normal coaching-mode restlessness. About Cliff being a little too interested in the house, asking Martin about the roof and the property taxes in a way that wasn't casual. He could have mentioned any of it. He saw more than anyone here, and Mallick, sitting there with her beaded chain and her notebook, was probably the first person in the room designed to actually receive what he saw.

"I don't think so," he said.

"No?"

"They're always like this. We've been coming here twenty-five years. There's always — you know. Tension."

"Sure. Fantasy football." She smiled. Doug didn't smile back, not because he was being cold but because the social energy required for a reciprocal smile wasn't available. The Lexapro handled some things but not that.

She asked two more questions — about the layout of the basement, about whether the basement door locked from inside — and he gave her the same flat answers. Yes. No. He didn't know. She thanked him and stood up and paused in the way she'd been pausing after every interview, that deliberate gap where she waited for the person to fill the silence with something useful. Doug let the silence sit. He was better at silence than anyone in this house, including Mallick, and she seemed to recognize it — a small adjustment behind her eyes, the expression of someone who'd reached for a tool and found it didn't grip. She gave a nod that was more recalibration than farewell and went inside.

He sat in the Adirondack chair for another minute. Then he got up and walked down the stone path through the pines and the birch, eighty yards of needles and duff underfoot, the light dappled and gold through the canopy, to the dock.

---

The dock was thirty feet of weathered planking extending into Elbow Lake. Martin had replaced boards over the years — Doug could tell the new ones from the old by color, the silvered gray of the originals versus the warmer brown of recent lumber that hadn't yet earned its weathering. A swim platform at the far end that none of them had used since their thirties. A bench bolted near the dock's edge, its green paint curling at the corners. Doug sat on the bench and let his legs hang over the side.

The water was clear enough to see the bottom. Sand and a few scattered stones and some beer caps that had sunk over twenty-five years of summers and lay there like small dull coins. Three feet deep, maybe less near the shore where the sand sloped up. Still enough today that the surface was almost a mirror, reflecting birch trunks and the underside of the dock's planking. A school of minnows held position near one of the pilings, twenty or thirty of them trembling in the same direction, catching light when they turned.

He sat there.

The sun was low enough in the afternoon to turn the lake gold. That specific September light that Doug associated with this place and nowhere else — softer than summer, warmer than what was coming, the kind of light that made everything look the way you remembered it rather than the way it actually was. The trees on the far shore were beginning to turn, patches of orange scattered through the green, and a motorboat buzzed somewhere out of sight, its wake arriving at the dock minutes later as a set of soft slaps against the pilings.

Doug thought about his apartment. The one-bedroom in Richfield with the beige carpet and the neighbor who watched *Jeopardy!* at a volume that suggested either hearing loss or hostility. He'd moved in the week after Steph said she was done, and the apartment had the specific emptiness of a place where no one had ever been happy — not actively miserable, just absent of any history that might soften the walls. He'd hung nothing on them. He'd bought a mattress and a coffeemaker and a set of towels. The towels were gray because gray was the color you bought when you didn't care about the color of your towels and wanted to stop standing in the aisle at Target.

Here, at least, the walls had things on them. The lake had history. The dock had twenty-five years of splinters and beer caps and the time Joel had cannonballed off the swim platform and come up bleeding from his shin, and Martin had called him an idiot, and Joel had called Martin a cheap bastard for not putting rubber bumpers on the platform, and they'd argued about it for the rest of the weekend, referencing it in draft trades like it was a war injury. *My client has a permanent scar from your negligent dockwork*, Joel had said. *I demand compensatory picks.* Martin had given him a sixth-rounder out of guilt and Joel had used it on a kicker, which Martin considered a personal insult.

Doug smiled. Not much of one. The kind that came without being asked.

He was glad he'd come. That surprised him, given everything — Martin dead, the detective, the pill bottles in his duffel bag like contraband. But he was glad. These men were exhausting and oblivious and at least half of them were lying about something, and being around them was still better than the apartment. Than the *Jeopardy!* neighbor and the gray towels and the mattress on the floor because he hadn't gotten around to buying a bed frame and at some point not getting around to it had become its own statement, a commitment to non-commitment that Steph would have had a word for. Steph had a word for everything. Her new boyfriend Todd — the physical therapist, the one with calves that could crack walnuts, according to their mutual friend Dana who didn't know when to stop sharing — probably had a bed frame. Todd probably had a headboard.

Doug's most dangerous secret: he thought about Todd's calves more than was healthy.

He came to this weekend every year for the same reason he took the Lexapro: not because it fixed anything, but because the alternative was worse. Twenty-five years of showing up to be the quiet one, the agreeable one, the one who got more ice, and in exchange he got a few days where his phone didn't ring with quarterly quotas and his apartment didn't close in on him and he could sit on a dock and watch minnows and feel, for a stretch, like a person who was part of something.

Martin had understood that. Martin hadn't known about the Lexapro or the Ativan or the three days on the couch or the gray towels, but he'd understood the shape of it. *Come to the draft. I mean it.* And Doug had come, and Martin was dead, and Doug was sitting on the dock where Martin's father had taught Martin to fish forty years ago, and the lake didn't know. The lake just did what it did — reflected the light, held the minnows, accepted the beer caps and the wake from distant boats. The minnows turned and caught the light and turned again.

A loon called from somewhere out on the water, that wavering cry that always sounded like a question that had been asked so many times nobody expected an answer anymore. Doug listened to it trail off and the silence come back, and the silence was fine. The silence was what he had.

He should eat something. The Lexapro worked better with food — he'd known that since before it was his, back when it was a product he detailed to physicians over catered lunches in clinic break rooms, back when depression was a market segment and serotonin was a talking point on a laminated card and the specificity of human unhappiness was something he packaged into a thirty-second elevator pitch for people who could prescribe their way out of someone else's problem. Now it was his problem and the elevator pitch was gone and the doctor who prescribed it had asked him, carefully, if he was having thoughts of self-harm, and Doug had said no, which was true. He wasn't. He was having thoughts of nothing. Long stretches of blank, like the beige carpet, where he didn't want to die but didn't particularly want to do anything else either. The Lexapro was supposed to fill those stretches in, give them texture, and maybe it was — he was here, after all. He'd packed a bag and driven four hours north and shaken hands and made small talk and eaten nothing and slept poorly and watched his friend die from a distance of one floor and the length of a hallway, and he was still here. On a dock. Watching minnows. Sitting in September light that made the lake look like something worth coming back to.

That had to count for something.

The wind picked up slightly, pushing small ripples across the surface. Doug pulled the sleeves of his hoodie over his hands and watched the ripples reach the dock and flatten against the pilings. He thought about going inside and decided against it and then kept deciding against it as the sun moved and the gold went amber and the amber went gray, which was a color he knew well.

When the light was mostly gone he went inside, ate a piece of the sourdough toast Cliff had left on the counter, and took the basement stairs down to the pull-out sofa. He lay there in the dark and listened to the refrigerator cycle above him, its asthmatic hum kicking on, running, clicking off, the forty-minute rhythm of a machine that had been keeping Martin's food cold for longer than Doug had known Martin. He didn't sleep. He didn't cry. He didn't do anything at all except exist in a house full of men who were hiding things, his own hidden thing the smallest and most ordinary of the lot — two pill bottles in a duffel bag, and a marriage that had ended, and the quiet fact of being alive when he wasn't sure what for, which wasn't a thing a detective would ask about and wasn't a thing his friends would notice and wasn't a thing that mattered to anyone but him, and only barely.


# Chapter 8

The house was worth four hundred thousand, maybe four-fifty in this market. Cliff had been appraising it since Thursday, the way he appraised everything — automatically, compulsively, the numbers ticking behind his eyes like a meter he couldn't shut off. Cedar siding, original but maintained. Wraparound deck, probably eight years old, the boards starting to silver where the stain had worn through. Stone fireplace, real stone, not the manufactured veneer he'd been selling clients for the past three years because real stone cost four times as much and most people couldn't tell the difference. Vaulted A-frame ceiling that photographed well and was a nightmare to heat. Lake frontage, eighty feet, mature pines and birch, dock in decent shape. He'd built his career on knowing what things were worth before anyone told him, and Martin Kessler's lake house on Elbow Lake was worth four hundred to four-fifty, which was not what Cliff needed it to be worth, because what Cliff needed was not a house.

What Cliff needed was forty thousand dollars.

The number had been living in his chest since March of last year, a low-grade infection that flared whenever he opened his laptop or checked his bank balance or drove past the duplex on Dale Street that had eaten his capital and his partnership with Martin and most of his ability to sleep through the night. Forty thousand dollars. Not forty thousand in the abstract, the way people said "forty thousand" when they meant "a lot of money" — forty thousand specific dollars that Martin Kessler had owed him for twenty-three months from a joint real estate deal on a duplex in Roseville that had gone sideways in the particular way that only real estate went sideways: slowly, expensively, and with both partners telling different stories about whose idea the purchase had been.

Martin had brought the property lead. Cliff had put up the capital. The property had needed a new foundation, a new roof, and remediation for asbestos that the inspection had somehow missed — the inspector being a guy Martin knew, a guy Martin had used before, a guy who had apparently inspected the duplex the way Martin inspected the refrigerator in this lake house, which was to say he'd looked at it, decided it was fine, and walked away. The duplex was gone. Sold at a loss that split unevenly because the capital had been Cliff's. Martin's half of that loss was forty thousand dollars, and Martin had been making sounds about repayment for twenty-three months — the right sounds, the "I know, I know, I'm working on it" sounds, the sounds that Cliff recognized from a career of dealing with people who owed more than they could pay and had learned to substitute conversation for checks.

Now Martin was dead, and dead men didn't write checks, but dead men had estates, and estates had obligations, and somewhere in this house there was documentation that would make Cliff's forty thousand an obligation rather than a memory.

Sunday morning. The kitchen smelled like bad coffee and the lake. Cliff stood at the counter with a mug he wasn't drinking from, watching the others move through their versions of a morning routine in a house where the host was dead. Paul was at the kitchen table in the wire-rimmed glasses that made him look like a disappointed accountant, thumbing through something on his phone with the particular concentration of a man doing work to avoid doing grief. Insurance claims, probably. Paul adjusted claims the way other people bit their nails — compulsively, under stress, as if the act of evaluating other people's losses could insulate him from his own. He'd been wearing the same button-down since Saturday and hadn't noticed.

Joel was on the deck in one of the Adirondack chairs, facing the lake with the focused blankness of a man who'd been told not to leave the property and was trying to decide if that was a request or an order. He had a coffee too. He hadn't touched it. The steam had stopped rising ten minutes ago and Joel hadn't noticed that either.

Doug was somewhere. The basement, probably. Doug had retreated to the basement the way a cat retreats under a bed — not because the basement was comfortable but because it was the place in the house where the fewest people would look for him. Cliff had noticed Doug's weight loss on Thursday, the way the hoodies hung on him now, the jawline that used to be soft and was now sharp in a way that looked medical, not disciplined. He hadn't said anything. It wasn't his business, and Cliff had learned a long time ago that commenting on another man's body was a conversation that went nowhere good.

Nate was near the front door, pacing the three steps between the coat hooks and the doormat that got the best cell signal, talking to someone with the desperate energy of a man who processed reality by narrating it. "No, I'm fine, I'm fine," Nate was saying, his free hand conducting an invisible orchestra. "It's just — no, listen, it's — they haven't said we can leave yet. The detective, the — yeah, the one with the casserole. I know. I know it sounds — yeah."

Tommy was on the deck too, in the other Adirondack chair, not talking to Joel. The two of them sat six feet apart facing the same lake, and the silence between them was the particular silence of two men who had nothing to say to each other and couldn't leave because the chairs were there and the lake was there and going inside would require a decision they hadn't made yet. Tommy's hands were on the armrests, big hands, the knuckles white from gripping. His crew cut was growing out unevenly, the gray at the temples catching the morning light. He looked like a man waiting in a doctor's office for results he already knew.

Mallick had left an hour ago. She'd told Paul — Paul being the one she treated as the group's designated adult, which Paul accepted the way Paul accepted everything, with a small nod and the air of a man adding another claim to his caseload — that she'd be back in the afternoon and that nobody should leave the property. She'd said it with a smile that was supposed to make it sound like a suggestion rather than an instruction. Cliff didn't buy the smile. He didn't buy anything about Mallick, because Cliff sold for a living and he recognized performance when he saw it, the same way a magician recognized another magician's setup. She was good. The casserole, the wrong name, the bathroom before the body — all of it was choreographed to look like it wasn't. Whether she was good enough to matter was a separate question that Cliff didn't have time to answer right now.

What mattered was that she was gone. What mattered was the study.

Cliff had been in Martin's study once already — Saturday night, in those scrambled hours between Martin's death and dawn, when every man in this house had apparently decided, independently and for private reasons, to creep through the darkness doing something stupid. Cliff's stupid thing had been this: he'd gone to the study looking for the promissory note Martin had signed. Or not signed — that was the problem. Martin had acknowledged the debt in texts, in emails, in conversations that Paul and Tommy had both overheard, but acknowledgment was not documentation, and if Martin's estate was going to settle debts, it would settle documented debts first, and Cliff's forty thousand existed primarily in Martin's word, which had depreciated to a market value of zero as of approximately 1:30 AM Saturday.

He hadn't found the promissory note. What he'd found was Martin's desk, Martin's filing cabinet — locked, a keyed lock, and Cliff hadn't wanted to force it at three in the morning with the house full of sleepless men — and Martin's wall safe. The safe was behind a framed print of a lake that looked nothing like Elbow Lake, a stock image in a frame that cost more than the print, which was Martin all over: invest in the container, neglect the contents. The safe was a SentrySafe, wall-mounted, combination dial. And the dial had been sitting one tick off from the final number — not unlocked, but one turn from unlocked, the way you'd leave a door on the latch when you were coming right back.

That was either Martin's trust or Martin's laziness, and in twenty-five years of friendship Cliff had never determined which one it was, because the thing about Martin was that his carelessness and his generosity looked identical from the outside. Martin left the safe one tick off. Martin left the front door unlocked. Martin left forty thousand dollars unrepaid. It was all the same gesture — a man who believed that things would hold together without being secured, and who had been right about that up until the moment his heart stopped.

Saturday night, Cliff had opened the safe, seen the contents — folders, envelopes, cash — and then heard the third stair from the top creak and closed the safe and left the study and gone back to the pull-out sofa in the basement where his heart had hammered against the mattress springs for twenty minutes. He'd folded. Cliff Haywood, who had sat across from bank loan officers with underwater properties and city zoning boards with rejected permits and told them things that were not entirely true with a resting heart rate of sixty-two, had folded because a stair creaked.

Now it was Sunday morning and Mallick was gone and the staircase was empty and Cliff's heart rate was currently seventy-four — he'd checked in the bathroom, two fingers on his wrist, because he was the kind of man who wanted data points even when the data was his own body failing to be calm. Seventy-four was fine. Seventy-four was a man about to go for a walk, not a man about to break into a dead friend's safe for the second time in thirty-six hours.

He rinsed the mug. Set it in the sink. The refrigerator cycled on with its loud, asthmatic hum, the compressor grinding through a rhythm it had probably been grinding through since 1998. Martin had never replaced it. Martin had never replaced anything in this house except the deck, and only because the old one had literally rotted through. This was one of seven reasons the Roseville duplex had been a disaster — Martin's conviction that maintenance was optional, that things could be sustained indefinitely through inattention, was a philosophy that worked for a cabin on a lake and failed catastrophically for rental property in a market that punished deferred maintenance with vacancy and code violations.

"Gonna make a call," Cliff said to the room. Paul nodded without looking up from his phone. Nate, still pacing near the door, gave a thumbs-up that nobody had requested.

Cliff walked down the hallway. Past the half bath with its nautical-themed hand towels that Martin's ex-wife Linda had probably bought and that Martin had probably not noticed in the five years since the divorce. Past the framed photographs — the annual draft weekends, twenty-five of them, a time-lapse of seven men aging in the same room. Cliff glanced at the 2003 photo without stopping. They were all twenty-three, twenty-four in that one. Tommy had a full head of dark hair and a sunburn and was grinning with his arm around Martin, and Martin was leaning into it, and the two of them looked like what they were — young men at a lake with nothing behind them and everything ahead. A year after the fire. Whatever showed on their faces, it wasn't guilt.

Martin's study was the last door on the left. Cliff turned the knob. Stepped inside. Closed the door behind him and stood still for three seconds, listening. The house gave him nothing — coffee sounds from the kitchen, the refrigerator, the barely audible murmur of Nate's voice. Nobody was coming.

The study smelled like Martin's desk — printer ink, paper dust, the specific mustiness of a man who kept documents he should have shredded. Desk, filing cabinet, bookshelves stacked with financial planning guides, Tom Clancy novels, and the college textbooks Martin had kept because Martin had kept everything. The window faced the front of the house, the gravel driveway, and Cliff checked: Mallick's county-issue sedan was not in the drive. Just their cars — Paul's Accord, Joel's Volvo, Tommy's F-150, Nate's Subaru with the podcast bumper stickers, Doug's Camry, and Cliff's Lexus. The Lexus was leased. The lease payment was fourteen hundred a month. Cliff was currently three weeks late on it, which put him in the grace period, which was a term that meant the same thing in car loans as it did in religion: you were forgiven right up until you weren't.

He pulled the lake print away from the wall. The safe was where he'd left it, which was where Martin had left it, which was where it had probably been since Martin's parents had installed it sometime in the eighties. Cliff turned the dial that single remaining tick. The handle gave. The door opened.

Inside: three manila folders, one business-size envelope, one legal-size manila envelope, and a stack of bills held with a binder clip. Cliff took the bills first. Counted without removing the clip — twenties and fifties, roughly two thousand dollars. He put them back. Two thousand wasn't the answer to anything. Two thousand was three weeks of the answer.

The first folder was labeled HOUSE. Deed, tax records, homeowner's insurance with a carrier Cliff recognized as midrange. The house was paid off — no mortgage. That was good. An unencumbered asset in the estate.

The second folder was INVESTMENTS. Edward Jones brokerage statements, a Vanguard index fund, some municipal bonds. Cliff scanned the most recent statement: total portfolio value around two-forty. Not rich. Comfortable. The kind of money that looked like enough until you subtracted a divorce settlement, two decades of property taxes, and forty thousand dollars in unrepaid debts.

The third folder was PERSONAL, and here Cliff's hands slowed. PERSONAL in a dead man's safe could be anything — love letters, medical records, the specific secrets that men kept in locked boxes because they couldn't keep them in their heads. What Cliff needed it to be was a signed promissory note.

He opened it. Birth certificate, Martin Kessler, born January 14, 1977, Hennepin County. Passport, expired. Military discharge papers — Martin had done two years in the Army after high school and before college, a fact that Cliff always forgot because Martin never mentioned it. Divorce decree, Martin A. Kessler and Linda M. Kessler, five years ago, Becker County District Court. And at the bottom, a single handwritten page on loose-leaf paper:

*I acknowledge that I owe Cliff Haywood $40,000 (forty thousand dollars) for his share of the capital loss on the Roseville property at 1847 Dale St. I have not yet repaid this and I intend to repay it in full by end of calendar year.*

No date. No signature. No witness line, no notarization, no language that any lawyer would call binding. Just Martin's square, neat handwriting and Martin's intention, which was worth exactly as much as Martin's heartbeat.

Cliff read it twice. It was something. It was evidence of intent. It was the kind of document his lawyer would look at and say "it's helpful" in the specific tone that meant "it's not enough," and then bill him four hundred an hour for the distinction.

He put the folder back. Picked up the business-size envelope — it was Martin's will, two pages, printed, witnessed by a notary in Detroit Lakes. Cliff skimmed it. The will was the estate's problem and the estate would be Paul's problem, because Paul was the executor, because of course Paul was the executor. The relevant line: the lake house was left to — Cliff stopped. Read it again. The lake house was left to Thomas Renaldi.

Tommy. Martin had left the house to Tommy.

Not to Paul, his best friend. Not divided among the group. Not to Linda, his ex-wife. To Tommy. Cliff let that sit for a moment, the way he let surprising numbers sit when a deal showed him something he hadn't expected. It was unusual. It was a data point he didn't yet know what to do with. He put the will back.

Which left the manila envelope.

It was thick, legal-size, sealed with a metal clasp that had been bent open and reclosed enough times that the metal was fatigued — the clasp wobbled when he touched it. The tab was labeled in Martin's handwriting, two letters in black Sharpie:

**RT**

Cliff's pulse ticked up. RT. His first thought was mechanical, automatic, the way his brain categorized letters: RTI Holdings, the LLC Martin had used for the Roseville deal. RT could be real estate transaction. RT could be a property code, a reference number, something connected to the duplex, to the money, to the paper trail that would make forty thousand dollars into a legal obligation instead of a dead man's vague handwritten promise.

He bent the clasp open. Pulled the contents out.

Not a contract. Not a partnership document. Not anything Cliff's brain had built in the three seconds between reading the tab and touching the paper.

It was a letter. Handwritten, Martin's handwriting again, four pages on yellow legal pad paper, blue ballpoint ink. The pages were soft at the edges, the particular softness that paper developed when it had been handled and then left alone for a long time — taken out, read, put back, taken out, read, put back, over years. The ink had faded slightly at the creases where the pages had been folded.

The first page began:

*August 17, 2002*

*I'm writing this down because somebody should, and because I don't trust myself to remember it right if I don't write it now while it's still clear. This is what happened at the cabin on Wolf Creek Road, Washburn County, Wisconsin, on the night of August 11, 2002.*

Cliff shifted his weight against the edge of Martin's desk. From the front of the house, he could hear Nate's voice, fainter now, and the clink of a mug in the kitchen. The lake lapped at the shore through the open study window. He read.

The first page was context. Martin and Tommy had driven up to a cabin outside Minong that belonged to a friend of Tommy's uncle, a man named Gunderson. They were closing it up for the season — draining pipes, shuttering windows, the kind of favor you did in your twenties when someone asked and you had a free weekend. The cabin had a caretaker, a man named Ray Toomey, who lived in a single-wide on the adjacent lot and maintained the property in the off-season. Toomey was supposed to have cleared out before they arrived. His truck — Martin specified a blue Ford pickup, the detail of a man who wanted to get this right — was in the driveway when they got there Friday night.

Second page. Tommy had been drinking since they'd left Minneapolis. Cliff could see it without effort — twenty-five-year-old Tommy, the same Tommy from the 2003 photo in the hallway, big and sunburned and careless, a twelve-pack riding between his feet in the passenger seat of whatever car Martin was driving in 2002. They'd found Toomey's truck but no Toomey. The cabin was dark. Tommy went in first. Martin wrote that he heard Tommy banging through the kitchen, swearing at the furniture, the particular noisy gracelessness of a big drunk man in a dark unfamiliar room. Then the sounds changed. A crash, then a different quality of silence, and then brightness in the kitchen window, and Martin smelled smoke before he could see the fire through the glass.

Third page. Martin's handwriting got smaller here, tighter, the letters compressing the way handwriting did when the hand was trying to keep pace with a memory that didn't want to be slowed down.

Tommy had knocked over a kerosene heater. The spill had caught on the pilot flame and the kerosene had splashed across the kitchen floor and the kitchen floor was pine, old pine, dry from a drought summer, and the fire had moved faster than either of them could think. Martin ran in. Found Tommy standing in the front room, not moving, watching the kitchen burn with the particular stillness of a man who understood what he had done but could not yet understand what it meant. Martin grabbed his arm. Tommy didn't resist. They got out the front door as the kitchen ceiling came down behind them, a sound Martin described as "like someone dropping a piano from a second floor." Four minutes. The cabin was fully involved — Martin's phrase, "fully involved," a firefighter's term that he must have looked up later — within four minutes.

Ray Toomey had been asleep in the back bedroom.

They didn't know he was there. His truck was in the driveway, but they'd assumed he'd walked to a bar or gotten a ride with a neighbor or any of the other things you assumed when you were twenty-five and a building was on fire and your brain was looking for reasons not to go back inside. The volunteer fire department found what was left of Ray Toomey in the wreckage the next morning. The county coroner's report — Martin didn't include the full report, but he referenced it — listed the blood alcohol at .14. Toomey had been drunk and asleep with a dead smoke detector battery and hadn't woken up, and the fire had done the rest.

Fourth page. This was the part that made Cliff read standing still, not leaning, not shifting, the air in the study suddenly specific and close.

Tommy had wanted to call 911 from the road. Martin had talked him out of it. Not because Martin was cold — the letter made that clear, the agitation of the handwriting, the crossed-out words and restarts — but because Martin had done the math the way Martin always did the math: Tommy was drunk. Tommy had entered the cabin without invitation. Tommy had knocked over the heater. A man was dead. The math on Tommy's future came out to involuntary manslaughter at minimum, and Martin had made a decision in the dark on a county road outside Minong that he'd then spent twenty-two years living inside.

Martin drove back to the cabin site at three in the morning. Alone. He wore gloves — work gloves, from the trunk. He repositioned the kerosene heater to look like it had malfunctioned in place rather than been knocked over by a person. He adjusted the spill pattern, as much as he could with the char. The fire investigation ruled it accidental: faulty heater, unattended cabin, intoxicated caretaker, dead smoke detector. Case closed. Ray Toomey was buried in Minong. Nobody asked Martin or Tommy anything, because nobody knew they'd been there.

The last paragraph:

*Tommy is not a bad person. He was drunk and it was dark and he didn't know Toomey was there. But a man died because of what Tommy did and what I did after, and I need to write it down. I don't know what I want done with this. I don't know if I want anything done with it. I need it to exist somewhere outside my head. If it's only in my head then it's just a thing I think about, and it has to be more than that. It has to be a thing that happened.*

*— Martin Kessler*

Cliff set the pages down on the desk. The study was the same study it had been six minutes ago. Filing cabinet, bookshelves, Tom Clancy novels, the window facing the empty gravel drive, the lake print leaning against the wall where he'd moved it. The refrigerator hummed in the kitchen. Someone — Paul — was running water in the sink. The loons were making their low, warbling call from somewhere out on the lake.

He stood there for maybe thirty seconds without thinking anything. Just standing in the room with the pages on the desk and Martin's handwriting in front of him and the name Ray Toomey in his head, a name he'd never heard before, a man he'd never met, a man who had been dead for twenty-two years because Tommy Renaldi had been drunk in a dark kitchen and knocked over a kerosene heater.

Then the thirty seconds ended and Cliff's brain did what Cliff's brain did. It started running numbers.

Tommy Renaldi. Forty-seven. High school football coach. PE teacher. Married to Denise, three kids — two in high school, one at St. Cloud State or maybe Mankato. Head coach of the varsity program for eleven years, the kind of tenure that made you a permanent fixture in a suburban Minnesota community, the kind of man whose name was on a field or a gymnasium or at minimum a glass case of trophies in the school lobby. Cliff didn't know which school, but he knew the type. He'd sold houses in those suburbs. He knew what a coaching career was worth in a town where Friday night football was the social calendar.

The statute of limitations on involuntary manslaughter in Wisconsin was six years. Cliff wasn't a lawyer, but he'd sat through enough deal closings and contract disputes to know when a clock had expired. Tommy couldn't be prosecuted. Probably. The concealment was a different question, and Martin's involvement was a different question, but Martin was dead and the criminal exposure was, in Cliff's assessment, limited.

But the criminal exposure was not the kind that mattered. The kind that mattered — the kind that had no statute of limitations, no expiration date, no appeals process — was what happened when a high school football coach's community learned that he had started a fire that killed a man and then let his friend cover it up and then spent twenty-two years coaching other people's children while a dead caretaker's family believed it was an accident. That kind of exposure didn't have a price because it was total. It was the end of the coaching career, the marriage, the reputation, the version of Tommy Renaldi that existed in the world. It was everything.

And everything was worth more than forty thousand dollars.

Cliff was not proud of the arithmetic. He would have preferred to be the kind of man who read a dead friend's twenty-two-year-old confession and felt moral weight, the appropriate gravity that Paul would feel, the legal complexity that Joel would catalogue, the quiet grief that Doug would absorb without comment. He was not that man. He was a man whose business had three projects stalled and one in active default. A man whose second ex-wife's lawyer had filed a motion to increase alimony that Cliff's lawyer couldn't contest because Cliff owed his lawyer eleven thousand dollars in unpaid fees. A man whose leased Lexus was forty-five days from repossession. A man who had put suits and restaurant tabs and client gifts on a credit card that was now at its limit, making minimum payments that didn't cover the interest, watching the balance grow like something alive and hungry.

A man inside those numbers did not read leverage and think about morality. He thought about arithmetic.

He wasn't going to use it crudely. He wasn't an idiot. He wasn't going to walk up to Tommy and say "I know about the fire, give me forty thousand dollars." That was blackmail, and blackmail was a crime, and more importantly blackmail was a bad deal — it was a one-time transaction with no repeat business and maximum risk. What Cliff was going to do was have a conversation. He was going to help Tommy understand that there was an arrangement available — Tommy's influence with the group, with the estate, with whatever mechanism existed to make Martin's debt an enforceable obligation. Tommy could make the forty thousand happen. Tommy had relationships with Paul, with the executor, with the people who would administer Martin's affairs. A word from Tommy, some pressure applied in the right places, and Cliff's money would surface through the estate process the way it should have surfaced two years ago. Tommy wouldn't even have to write a check himself. He'd just have to make sure the right conversations happened.

It wasn't blackmail. It was a restructuring.

Cliff took his phone from his pocket. He photographed each page, front and back — four sheets, eight images. The camera shutter sound was off, had been off since 2019 when he'd started photographing competing agents' listing sheets at open houses, a practice that was frowned upon by the ethics board of the Minnesota Association of Realtors and practiced by every agent Cliff had ever met. He checked each image for focus and legibility. Martin's handwriting was clear. "Tommy" was clear. "Ray Toomey" was clear. "Washburn County" was clear. The date was clear. The signature was clear.

He put the pages back in the manila envelope. Bent the clasp closed, careful to match the angle it had been at when he'd opened it. Put the envelope back in the safe — behind the folders, in front of the cash, exactly where it had been. He closed the safe door. Turned the dial back one tick from locked, Martin's habitual position, the position Cliff had found it in, the position it would be in when the estate eventually sent someone to inventory the contents and they found what they found.

He rehung the lake print. Adjusted it until the frame sat level on its hooks. Stepped back to check. The print really was terrible — a generic lake, generic pines, the kind of image you'd find in a dentist's office.
