Martin could have had a local artist paint Elbow Lake itself, the actual view from the actual window three feet away, and instead he'd hung a stock photograph of someone else's lake. That was Martin. Why fix a thing when you could leave it slightly wrong and live with the wrongness for twenty years.

Cliff opened the study door. The hallway was empty. He could hear Paul and Joel in the kitchen now, their voices low, one of those careful conversations about logistics and next steps that men had when the emotional conversation was too large to start. He walked to the half bath. Washed his hands — not for fingerprints, his prints were already on the safe from Saturday night, and if Mallick dusted for prints she'd find six men's fingerprints throughout the house because they'd all been in this house for three days. He washed his hands because they were shaking and the hot water helped and because a man washing his hands in a bathroom after using the bathroom was the most invisible man in any house.

He looked at himself in the mirror above the sink. Silver hair that he paid sixty dollars to maintain at a barber in Edina. The permanent tan that came from spending fifteen years walking property lines and standing on half-built decks pointing at lake views. The collared polo he'd bought on a credit card that was now maxed. The watch — a Tag Heuer, a gift to himself in 2017 when the business had been good, the last year the business had been good. He looked like a man who sold lakefront property and tipped well at dinner. He looked like a man who had his affairs in order.

He dried his hands on the nautical towel. Went back to the kitchen. Poured a fresh mug of the bad coffee. Paul was still at the table. Joel had come in from the deck and was leaning against the counter, arms crossed, looking at nothing.

"Mallick say what time she's coming back?" Cliff asked. Even voice. Two hands on the mug.

"Afternoon," Paul said. "Didn't specify."

"Okay." Cliff nodded. Sipped. The coffee was genuinely bad — Martin had never figured out the ratio, always used too much water, and the can of Folgers in the cabinet was the same brand Martin had been buying since college. Twenty-five years of bad coffee. The man had a two-hundred-forty-thousand-dollar investment portfolio and couldn't buy decent beans. "Think I'm gonna take a walk. Clear my head a little."

"Sure," Paul said. His eyes were back on his phone.

Joel said nothing. Joel had said almost nothing since Saturday, and when he did speak it was in short, precise sentences that sounded like deposition answers. Cliff nodded to him anyway and Joel gave a nod back that was more acknowledgment than greeting, and Cliff took his coffee and walked out the front door.

He sat on the porch step where no one inside could see him. The gravel drive was bright in the morning sun. The birches across the road were just starting to turn — a few yellow leaves among the green, the first signal of a Minnesota September tipping toward October. The air smelled like pine sap and lake water and the faint sweetness of leaves beginning to decompose. It was a beautiful morning. It was the kind of morning that sold properties — Cliff had used mornings like this in listing photos, had positioned his camera to catch the golden light on the water, had written copy about "peaceful lake living" and "your own private retreat" for properties that were forty minutes from the nearest grocery store and had septic systems that cost eight grand to replace.

His phone was in his pocket with eight photographs on it. RT. Not real estate transaction. Not RTI Holdings. Ray Toomey. Caretaker. Dead at whatever age he'd been — Martin hadn't included that, just a name and a truck and a man asleep in a back bedroom who didn't wake up.

From the deck, Cliff could hear Tommy. Not words — Tommy wasn't talking. Just the sound of a big man in an Adirondack chair, the creak of the wood when he shifted, the particular heavy breathing of someone who was sitting still but not at rest. Cliff had noticed it before without understanding it. The way Tommy got quiet when the group conversation moved to anything before 2005 or so. The way Tommy came to the draft every year without fail, even the year his daughter was born, even the year his team made the state tournament, even the year Denise had asked him not to go and he'd gone anyway. The way Martin always made sure Tommy came, called him, included him, protected the seat even when other members of the group had suggested maybe this year Tommy should sit it out.

Twenty-two years. Martin had been managing Tommy's guilt for twenty-two years, and Tommy had been showing up to be managed, and now Martin was dead and the management had ended and Tommy was sitting on the deck of a lake house that Cliff had just learned Martin had left to Tommy in his will, gripping the armrests with hands that were white at the knuckles.

A loon surfaced about fifty yards out, its black head slick and shining. It dove without ceremony, slipping under so cleanly it left barely a ring on the surface. Gone.

Cliff drank the coffee. It was still bad.

He'd have the conversation with Tommy soon. Not today — today was too raw, too close to Saturday, too close to Mallick. He'd wait for the right moment. A quiet moment on the deck, maybe. Two beers. A casual opening, the way you opened any negotiation: establish common ground, acknowledge the difficulty, present the proposition as a solution rather than a demand. Cliff had been having conversations like this for twenty years. He knew how to frame a restructuring so the other party felt like they were participating in a decision rather than receiving one.

He sat on the porch step and finished the bad coffee and watched the light move on the lake and planned the conversation he was going to have with Tommy Renaldi, a conversation about money and influence and the reasonable resolution of a financial obligation, and he believed — as completely as he had ever believed any deal was sound, any listing was priced right, any client would close — that the conversation would go the way he planned it.


# Chapter 9

Joel Beckman had been counting the hours since anyone last asked him about the argument. Fourteen. A personal best for this weekend, though the bar was roughly ankle-high. The previous record had been six hours, overnight, because even these people had to sleep eventually.

He sat at the kitchen table with a turkey sandwich he wasn't eating and watched Nate try to scrape the last of the mustard from the jar with a butter knife. Cliff was at the counter cutting an apple into mathematically precise slices. Tommy was on his second sandwich, or possibly his third — Joel had stopped tracking. Paul sat across from Joel with a cup of coffee he kept warming in the microwave, the same cup he'd been nursing since morning, which Joel found either disciplined or insane.

Doug was eating a bowl of cereal, because Doug had apparently decided that cereal was a valid Sunday lunch option, and nobody had the energy to argue.

The chair at the head of the table was empty. Nobody had mentioned it. Everyone had noticed.

"Mustard's done," Nate announced, holding up the jar. "We're officially post-mustard. I want that noted for the record."

"There's more in the garage fridge," Paul said.

"The garage fridge. Of course. Because Martin had a condiment annex." Nate set the jar down and took a bite of his dry sandwich. He chewed for a while. "His garage fridge had better beer than my actual fridge. That's not a bit. That's just true."

The kitchen was quiet except for the refrigerator's cycling hum and Tommy's methodical chewing. Afternoon light came through the windows at a low angle, catching dust in the air above the table. Martin's scotch — the Lagavulin — still sweetened the air in the great room beyond the kitchen, a smell that Joel had started to associate with the specific flavor of being accused of something he didn't do.

"So what happens now?" Tommy said. He said it to the table, to nobody specific, the way coaches posed questions they already had answers to.

"Mallick said she'd be back this afternoon," Paul said. "She's at the station going through her notes."

"I mean with us. The house. The — all of it."

"Martin's estate will handle the house," Cliff said, and Joel noted the smoothness of it, the way Cliff's voice flattened the question into real estate terms. Cliff knew things about Martin's finances that the rest of them didn't — or thought he did, anyway. Joel had been watching him for two days now, the way Cliff moved through the house like he was appraising it.

"I just want to go home," Doug said quietly. Then, catching himself: "Not that — I mean. We all do. Obviously."

"Mallick hasn't cleared us yet," Joel said.

Tommy looked at him. "She hasn't cleared anybody, Beck."

"She's cleared some of you more than others."

"What's that supposed to mean?"

Joel took a bite of his sandwich to avoid answering. The turkey was dry. Martin always bought the good deli turkey, the kind you had to ask for at the counter, and somehow it was already going stale. Two days. That was how long it took for the provisions to start outlasting the host.

Nate was watching him with the expression Joel had learned to recognize as the precursor to a bit — eyebrows up, mouth slightly open, the podcaster warming up. But Nate didn't launch into anything. He just chewed his dry sandwich and looked at Joel with something that wasn't quite sympathy and wasn't quite curiosity.

"Can I ask you something?" Nate said.

"You can ask."

"Are you — and this is just, I'm genuinely asking, not for anything, just between us — are you gonna tell us what you and Martin were fighting about?"

The table went quiet. Not silent — Tommy was still chewing, the fridge was still humming — but the conversational temperature dropped in a way Joel could feel against his skin.

"I told Mallick it wasn't relevant."

"You told Mallick it wasn't relevant to her investigation," Paul said, carefully. "That's not the same as it not being relevant."

"It's not relevant."

"Bullshit," Tommy said. Not angry — matter-of-fact. Coaching-mode. "You show up Thursday night already pissed off, you're in his face at midnight screaming loud enough that I could hear it from the hot tub, and Martin's dead twelve hours later. That's relevant."

"Martin died of a cardiac event."

"We don't actually know that yet," Paul said.

"We effectively know that."

"Joel." Paul set his coffee down. The microwave-warmed cup made a soft thud on the table. "We've been here for three days. Mallick keeps coming back. Nobody's been cleared. And you're sitting here telling us the argument that the entire investigation is centered on isn't relevant."

"The investigation isn't centered on the argument."

"It's centered on you," Cliff said, without looking up from his apple. "Which is the same thing."

Joel pushed his plate away. The turkey sandwich sat there, one bite taken, reproaching him with its dryness.

The truth was that Joel had been waiting for this conversation. Not wanting it — waiting for it, the way you wait for a dental appointment you've already rescheduled twice. The argument was the one piece of information that belonged to him and not to the investigation, and holding it had become physically exhausting in a way he hadn't expected. His jaw ached. His shoulders had been knotted since Saturday morning. He'd been sleeping three hours a night in a guest bedroom that smelled like Martin's fabric softener, lying in the dark rehearsing both the speech he'd planned to give Martin and the speech he now had to give these men, and the two speeches kept bleeding into each other until he couldn't tell which one he was rehearsing for which audience.

"It's personal," he said.

"We know it's personal," Nate said. "That's why we're asking."

"You don't know what kind of personal."

"Then tell us what kind."

Doug was looking at his cereal. He hadn't spoken since the "I want to go home" admission and its immediate retraction. But Joel could see Doug's eyes moving between the men at the table, the quiet surveillance that Doug had perfected over twenty-five years of being the one nobody watched.

"Look," Tommy said, leaning forward, elbows on the table. "I'm not trying to be a hard-ass here. But we're all in this. We're all stuck in this house with a dead friend and a detective who keeps asking us the same questions different ways, and you're the one piece of this that doesn't add up. If you've got something to say, say it. If you don't, fine. But stop acting like the rest of us are stupid for wanting to know."

Joel almost laughed. It was such a Tommy speech — the coach in the locker room at halftime, demanding effort and accountability, broadcasting certainty about the right play. Tommy Renaldi, who had flushed evidence down a toilet while a detective stood on the other side of the door, lecturing Joel about transparency.

"Okay," Joel said. The word came out smaller than he intended. "You want to know what the argument was about."

"Yes," said four voices, roughly simultaneously. Doug's mouth moved but no sound came out.

Joel looked at the empty chair at the head of the table. Martin's chair. The chair where Martin Kessler had sat for twenty-five drafts, running the league, managing the group, making sure everyone had a drink, making sure Tommy came back every year, making sure the whole machine kept turning. The chair where the most generous, most controlling, most thoughtfully selfish man Joel had ever known had presided over a weekend that was equal parts reunion and performance.

"Martin was fucking my ex-wife," Joel said.

---

The word sat on the table like a dropped plate.

Nate's sandwich stopped halfway to his mouth. Tommy's jaw paused mid-chew. Cliff set down his knife. Paul closed his eyes — not a flinch, more like a man bracing for an impact he'd been anticipating without knowing it.

Doug looked at Joel. Just looked at him, with an expression Joel recognized because he'd seen it in his own mirror for the past month — the face of a man processing a betrayal that rearranges everything he thought he knew about someone.

"Karen?" Paul said.

"That's the one."

"Jesus Christ," Tommy said.

"For eight months. Since January." Joel's voice was steady, which surprised him. He'd imagined this conversation a hundred times since Karen told him, and in every version he'd been shouting. Instead the facts came out flat and precise, the way he delivered opening statements — evidence first, emotion later, let the jury assemble the picture. "She told me a month before the draft. We were in the parking lot of Ellie's dorm, dropping off her stuff for fall semester. Karen waited until Ellie went inside and then she told me in the car. In the parking lot of Slichter Hall. She said she wanted me to hear it from her."

"Hold on," Nate said. "A month before the draft. So you came here knowing."

"I came here to talk to him."

"You came here to confront him," Cliff corrected.

"I came here to talk to him. I was going to be — I had a plan for how to be calm about it. I was going to wait until Saturday morning, when everyone was hungover and slow, and I was going to take Martin down to the dock and I was going to ask him why."

"Instead you screamed at him Thursday night," Tommy said.

"I didn't scream."

"Beck, I heard you from the hot tub."

"I raised my voice. There's a legal distinction."

"There's really not."

Joel rubbed his face with both hands. The legal precision was habit, the sarcasm was habit, and neither of them were doing what he needed them to do right now, which was to explain to five men how it felt to sit in a dormitory parking lot watching your daughter wave from a second-floor window while your ex-wife told you that your friend of twenty-five years had been inside her since New Year's.

"Martin and Karen," Paul said, still with his eyes closed. "How."

"How what?"

"How did it start? How long did you — how did Karen—"

"I don't know how it started. Karen said it started at a New Year's party. I wasn't there because I had Ellie that weekend. They ran into each other and one thing led to another, and apparently one thing kept leading to eight months of another." Joel heard his own voice going sharp and clipped, the cross-examination tone taking over. "She said it wasn't planned. She said she didn't mean for it to happen. She said all the things people say when they absolutely meant for it to happen."

"Did Martin know you knew?" Doug asked. His voice was so quiet that everyone turned to look at him, startled by the interruption from the man who never interrupted.

"Yeah. I called him. Three weeks before the draft. Told him I knew. He said—" Joel stopped. Swallowed. "He said he was sorry. He said it was over. He said he'd ended it in August."

"Had he?" Cliff asked.

"I have no idea. Karen says yes. Martin said yes. I can't exactly cross-examine a dead man."

"So Thursday night," Nate said. "The argument."

"Thursday night I got drunk and all the calm shit I'd planned to say turned into the actual shit I wanted to say. Which was louder and less articulate." Joel picked up his abandoned sandwich, looked at it, put it back down. "I called him a piece of shit. Several times. In several configurations. He stood there and took it because that's what Martin did — he absorbed things. He was good at absorbing things. He stood there with his hands in his pockets and his head tilted like he was listening to a weather report he found mildly interesting, and I wanted to hit him so badly I could taste it."

"Did you?" Tommy asked.

"No. I went to bed. I lay there for three hours thinking of every goddamn thing I should've said and didn't, and then Paul banged on my door at two in the morning and Martin was dead."

Silence. The refrigerator cycled off and the kitchen became genuinely quiet — the kind of quiet where you could hear the lake through the windows, the small insistent lapping that didn't care about anything happening inside this house.

Paul opened his eyes. He looked at Joel with an expression Joel couldn't fully read — grief and something else, something that might have been the recognition that his twenty-five-year image of Martin had just acquired a crack that would keep spreading.

"I'm sorry," Paul said.

"Don't be sorry for me. Be sorry that he died before I could finish telling him what I thought of him." Joel's voice caught on the last word, an involuntary crack that his legal training couldn't smooth over. "That's the part that — I had things to say to him. I had a whole — I had things prepared. And he just fucking died."

Tommy leaned back in his chair and blew out a long breath through his teeth. He looked at the ceiling, then at Joel, then at the empty chair.

"Shit, Beck," Tommy said.

"Yeah."

"That's — yeah. Shit."

---

What followed was, by Joel's professional assessment, the most structurally bizarre conversation he'd ever participated in, and he had spent sixteen years in Hennepin County criminal court.

They moved to the deck because the kitchen had gotten too small for what was happening, and because Nate said he needed air, which Joel suspected meant Nate needed space to process this at the volume his personality required. Tommy brought beers from the garage fridge — the good ones, Martin's IPAs, which added a layer of irony that nobody acknowledged. Paul brought his coffee. Doug followed the group the way he always did, taking the Adirondack chair farthest from the center of conversation, his cereal abandoned inside.

The September afternoon was golden and ridiculous. The lake was flat, the trees were starting to turn at their edges, and two loons were calling to each other from somewhere Joel couldn't see. It was the kind of afternoon that Martin had built this entire weekend around — the kind that made you forget you were forty-seven and your friend was dead and you were under suspicion for it.

"Okay," Nate said, settling into a chair with his beer. "I need to say something."

"Do you," Joel said.

"I need to say something that's gonna sound insensitive, and I want you to know I'm aware of that in advance."

"Noted."

"Was she — is Karen — I mean, was Martin..." Nate made a vague gesture with his bottle. "How serious was it? Because there's a difference between, like, a thing and a — like, was it a relationship, or was it—"

"Nate, I don't know the details. I didn't ask for the details."

"Right, right, no. I'm just trying to understand the, uh, the parameters."

"The parameters," Joel repeated.

"Of the violation," Nate said, and Joel could see the word coming, could see Nate's brain reaching for the only framework these men had for discussing ethics: "Because in league terms—"

"Oh, Christ," Paul said.

"No, hear me out. In league terms, this is — okay. An ex-wife is like a player you've traded. You don't own the rights anymore. The trade was completed. She's on a different roster."

"She's not on a roster, Nate."

"I'm using a metaphor."

"Use a different one."

"Fine. She's a free agent. Karen's a free agent. Joel released her — or she released him, I don't know who filed—"

"I filed."

"Okay, so Joel released Karen. She's a free agent. Technically, any team in the league can sign a free agent. There's no tampering rule for free agents."

Tommy pointed his beer at Nate. "There's a gentleman's agreement."

"Is there, though? Is there actually a gentleman's agreement about exes?"

"There's absolutely a gentleman's agreement about exes," Tommy said, with the moral certainty of a man whose own moral ledger was substantially more complicated than anyone at this table knew. "You don't go after your buddy's ex. Period. I don't care if she's been divorced ten years, I don't care if she's seeing someone else, I don't care if she's — look, I don't care. You don't do it."

"What about a statute of limitations?" Cliff said. He was leaning against the deck railing with his apple, the precise slices gone, eating the core now like a man who didn't waste anything. "Seriously. Is there a number? Two years? Five? At some point, doesn't the—"

"No," Tommy said.

"What if they were only married for six months?"

"Doesn't matter."

"What if the divorce was mutual and everyone's cool?"

"Doesn't matter. It's not about the math. It's about the principle. You don't touch your friend's ex. That's the play. That's the only play."

Joel sat in his Adirondack chair and listened to five men who had known him for a quarter century argue about whether Martin Kessler had committed a friendship violation by sleeping with his ex-wife, and something happened that he couldn't have predicted. The wire in his chest — the thing that had been tight since Karen told him in that parking lot, the tension that had been cinching for a month — eased by exactly one notch. Not because anyone was saying the right thing. Tommy's moral absolutism was blunt and performed. Cliff's statute-of-limitations framing was characteristically transactional. Nate's free-agent metaphor was offensive and also, Joel had to admit, not entirely without structural merit. But they were arguing about it. They were taking it seriously, in the only shared language they had, and Joel's pain was the text of the argument rather than the subtext, and for the first time in a month he felt like someone other than Karen and his therapist knew that it existed.

"Paul," Nate said. "You're quiet."

Paul was staring at his coffee. He hadn't touched his beer. His wire-rimmed glasses caught the afternoon light, and Joel could see Paul's jaw working slightly, the small muscle flex that meant Paul was editing his thoughts before releasing them.

"He was my best friend," Paul said.

"He was your best friend who was screwing Joel's wife," Tommy said.

"Ex-wife."

"See, and this is what I mean about the statute of limitations—" Cliff started.

"Shut up, Cliff," Tommy and Joel said at the same time, which was the first thing they'd agreed on all weekend.

Paul looked at Joel. The expression on Paul's face was the one Joel had been dreading — the look of a loyal man confronting the fact that the person he was loyal to had done something genuinely shitty. Paul Decker, claims adjuster, mediator, the group's designated adult, was sitting in an Adirondack chair trying to be fair to Martin and fair to Joel and realizing that this particular claim couldn't be adjusted.

"I didn't know," Paul said. "I want you to know that. If I'd known, I would've told you."

"Would you?"

Paul flinched. It was small, but Joel caught it and regretted the question immediately — not because it was unfair, but because it was the kind of question his legal training produced automatically, the reflex to test a statement even when the person making it was trying to be decent.

"Yeah. I would've."

Joel nodded. He believed Paul. That was the worst part — he believed Paul didn't know, which meant Martin had hidden it from his best friend too, which meant the secrecy wasn't about protecting Joel's feelings. It was about Martin wanting to keep his image intact across all his relationships simultaneously, managing each person's version of him the way he managed the fantasy league: quietly, thoroughly, with control that looked like generosity.

"You know what the fucked-up part is?" Joel said.

"Which fucked-up part?" Nate asked. "Because I've got a running list."

"The fucked-up part is that I came here to fight with him. I planned a whole weekend around getting to yell at him. I drove four hours with a speech in my head. And he died in his sleep like a — like he just opted out. Like he checked out of the conversation. Martin Kessler, who never let anyone else have the last word in twenty-five years, died before I could finish a sentence."

"That's..." Doug said. Everyone turned to look at him. Doug's face was doing something Joel couldn't quite parse — the bags under his eyes darker than they should be, his mouth open slightly, and something working behind his expression that Joel recognized not as sympathy but resonance. Doug understood this. Not the specifics — the shape. Being left without resolution by someone who was supposed to still be there.

"That's really fucked up," Doug said, quietly and completely, without trailing off, without walking it back, and the deck went silent because Doug finishing a sentence was an event.

"Yeah," Joel said. "It is."

Tommy cracked his knuckles — the sound sharp in the afternoon air, a physical punctuation. He leaned forward, elbows on knees, the posture of a coach about to draw up the final play.

"Here's what I think," Tommy said. "And I say this with — look, I loved Martin. We all loved Martin. But what he did was wrong. Full stop. There's no version of this where it's okay. I don't care that Karen was technically available, I don't care that they were adults, I don't care about any of that bullshit. You don't do that to a guy in your crew. Period."

Joel watched Tommy deliver this verdict with the clean moral certainty of a man who believed in rules, in codes, in the bright-line distinctions between what you did and what you didn't do. Tommy Renaldi, whose own relationship with Martin had apparently involved private conversations nobody else was invited into and twenty-five years of Martin making sure Tommy always came back to the draft. Tommy's certainty sounded right. It just didn't sound true — like a well-coached answer to a question about sportsmanship, technically correct and ringing off something hollow underneath.

"Martin wasn't a bad person," Paul said, and the qualifier was already bleeding in, the insurance-trained habit of reframing the damage. "He did a bad thing. Those aren't the same."

"That's a real estate distinction," Cliff said. "A house with termites and a bad house are functionally the same. You're still out the repair costs."

"Thank you, Cliff. That's very helpful."

"I'm just saying."

"What about Karen?" Nate asked. "I mean, she gets a say in this, right? She's not — she's a person. She made a choice too."

Joel looked at Nate. "Karen's choices are Karen's business. I'm not mad at Karen."

"You're not?"

"Karen didn't owe me anything. We're divorced. She can fuck whoever she wants." The word came out harder than Joel intended, the casual profanity suddenly carrying weight because of the specific image it forced into the room — Karen and Martin, Karen in Martin's apartment or Martin in hers, the domestic geography of the betrayal that Joel had spent a month trying not to map in his head. "Martin owed me something. Martin sat across from me at this table for twenty-five years and he owed me the basic goddamn courtesy of not putting his dick in my ex-wife."

"Okay," Nate said, both hands raised. "That's — yeah. That's fair."

"Fair," Joel said, and almost laughed. "What's fair? Is there a trade deadline for this? A waiver claim? What's the league precedent for your dead friend fucking your ex-wife — do I get compensatory picks?"

And there it was. The laugh. Not from Joel — from Nate, a short involuntary bark that Nate immediately tried to smother behind his hand. Then Tommy, a low rumble that built against his will. Cliff's thin smile, the developer's appreciation for a well-timed close. Even Paul's mouth twitched, the mediator's composure cracking for half a second before it reassembled.

Joel sat there in an Adirondack chair on a dead man's deck, holding a dead man's IPA, cracking jokes about a dead man's affair with his ex-wife, and the absurdity of it was so total that something in him gave way. Not dramatically — he didn't cry, didn't break, didn't deliver a speech. He just exhaled something he'd been holding for a month, and the wire in his chest loosened by one more notch, and he took a drink of Martin's beer and it was cold and good and he was going to finish it even though the man who bought it had been sleeping with Karen since January.

"Compensatory picks," Nate repeated, shaking his head. "Goddamn, Beck."

"I'm not joking. I want a grievance filed with the league."

"The league doesn't — the league doesn't have a rule for this."

"Then we need one."

"We're not drafting legislation about ex-wife — about—"

"The Kessler Rule," Tommy said, and there was something mean in it that Joel appreciated, the willingness to name the dead by their sin instead of their virtue. "No member of this league shall engage in romantic or sexual conduct with another member's former spouse. Penalty: forfeiture of all draft picks and permanent league shaming."

"Define 'former spouse,'" Cliff said. "Does this extend to ex-girlfriends? What about—"

"Cliff, I swear to God."

"I'm asking a legitimate question about scope."

Paul stood up. His coffee was cold again and he didn't go to the microwave this time. He stood at the deck railing and looked out at the lake, and Joel could see the tension in his shoulders — the straight line of a man who was managing his reaction the way he managed everything, carefully and thoroughly and with the growing suspicion that care and thoroughness weren't going to be enough.

"Martin was wrong," Paul said, to the lake. "I want to say that clearly. He was wrong, and I would've told him he was wrong, and he should've told Joel instead of hiding it. He should've told me." Paul's voice tightened, the qualifiers gone now, the way they went when Paul stopped adjusting and started meaning something. "He didn't tell me. Twenty-five years and he didn't tell me this, and I'm starting to think he didn't tell me a lot of things, and I don't—"

Paul stopped himself. Joel could see it happening — the claims adjuster putting the lid back on, filing the thought for later, recognizing that this was Joel's conversation and not his. But something in Paul's face had changed. The grief that had been clean and uncomplicated — best friend, twenty-five years, the worst loss of his life — was curdling into something messier. The suspicion that Martin's friendship had contained rooms Paul was never invited into.

"He loved you, Paul," Joel said, and was surprised to hear himself say it. "Whatever else he was doing, he loved you. I know that because he talked about you like you were the only person in this group who mattered. Constantly. Every draft, every phone call. Paul this, Paul that. If anything, I should be jealous of you."

Paul turned from the railing. His eyes were bright behind the wire-rimmed glasses and he nodded once — a short, hard nod that closed the subject.

"Thank you," Paul said.

Doug was sitting in his chair at the edge of the group with his hands folded in his lap, watching the exchange with that quiet attention that Joel was beginning to understand wasn't passivity but witness. Joel caught Doug's eye and Doug gave him a nod — not agreement, not sympathy exactly, just acknowledgment. I hear you. I've been somewhere like that. The nod of a man who had spent the past three months learning that the people you loved were capable of things you couldn't have prepared for.

"So where does this leave us?" Tommy said, because Tommy always needed the next play, the next whistle, some direction to run in.

Joel looked at the empty Adirondack chair — not the one at the kitchen table but the one out here on the deck, Martin's chair, the one he'd sit in with his Lagavulin and his scorecard, holding court from the best seat in the house. The afternoon sun was hitting it at the angle that would have warmed Martin's face if Martin had been alive to sit in it, and Joel felt the familiar surge of directionless rage at a dead man who had checked out of every conversation that mattered.

"It leaves us with a dead friend who was better than this and also exactly this," Joel said. "Same as it's always been."

Nate opened his mouth and closed it. For once, the podcaster had nothing to add. Tommy took a long drink. Cliff finished his apple core and looked out at the water with an expression Joel couldn't read — the smooth surface of a man whose thoughts were somewhere else entirely. Paul sat back down in his chair and wrapped both hands around his cold coffee.

The loons called from somewhere far across the lake. The afternoon kept happening, the way afternoons do when something has finally been said and nobody knows yet what to build on the ground it cleared. Joel drank Martin's beer. It was good. It was cold and bitter and good, and Martin was dead, and Karen was back in Minneapolis probably wondering whether Joel had told these men yet, and he had, and it hadn't fixed a single thing, but the wire in his chest was looser than it had been in a month.

That was something. It wasn't much, but it was something.


# Chapter 10

The kitchen in Martin Kessler's lake house had the sort of refrigerator that announced itself. Every forty minutes or so — Mallick had timed it, because twenty years of police work had made her the kind of person who timed refrigerators — the compressor kicked on with a shudder that rattled the glasses in the cabinet above the sink. She'd been sitting at the kitchen table since seven-thirty, and the refrigerator had cycled four times, which put it close to ten o'clock, and she was nowhere.

Her leather notebook was open to a page that would have embarrassed her if any of her colleagues at the county office saw it. She'd drawn a grid. The columns were labeled with initials — PD, CH, NG, DW, TR, JB — and the rows were labeled with the physical evidence. She'd filled in check marks, question marks, and, in two cases, small x's that meant "confirmed absent." The grid looked like a badly designed crossword puzzle, and it told her less than she wanted and more than she expected.

She took off her reading glasses and set them on the table. The beaded chain caught on her coffee mug and she untangled it with the patience of a woman who'd been untangling the same chain from the same kinds of things for a decade.

Martin Kessler was dead of cardiac arrest. The medical examiner had been clear: hypertrophic cardiomyopathy, undiagnosed, exacerbated by alcohol and sildenafil and prolonged heat exposure from the hot tub. It was a natural death. People died like this. Healthy-looking men in their late forties dropped dead and their friends stood around looking stunned, and the medical explanation was usually sufficient.

But Martin Kessler's house had been rearranged while his body cooled.

She put the glasses back on and looked at her grid.

The body. Martin had been found in bed, and by the time the deputies arrived Saturday morning, he was on his back with his arms at his sides. Tidy. Composed. The problem was that people who died of cardiac arrest in their sleep didn't end up tidy and composed. They ended up in whatever configuration their last involuntary movement left them — twisted, half off a pillow, one arm thrown, a leg bent. Paul Decker, bless his insurance-adjusting heart, had corrected her on Saturday about which way Martin's head had been turned. He'd said it casually, almost reflexively — "I think it was more to the left, actually, toward the window" — and the correction told her two things. One: Paul had seen the body in its original position. Two: the body was no longer in that position when the deputies arrived. Someone had moved Martin and straightened him out, and Paul either didn't realize he'd just told her that or his training in accurate claims assessment was stronger than his instinct for self-preservation.

The wiping. The BCA team — she'd had to wait until Sunday morning for them to drive up from St. Paul, which was its own comedy — had found partial cleaning on the bedroom doorframe, the edge of the mattress, and the headboard. Partial. Whoever wiped did the right side of the headboard and missed the left. They got the doorframe at shoulder height and skipped the door handle entirely. This was not professional cleaning. This was someone on their hands and knees at three in the morning, wiping surfaces they'd touched, probably crying. The wiping matched the body work — same room, same panic, same person. Paul.

The safe. The wall safe in Martin's study was hidden behind a landscape painting, which was exactly where a lake house wall safe would be hidden. Martin had left the combination dial one tick off from locked, which meant anyone who jiggled it would find it open. Cliff Haywood had corrected her on Saturday about which room was the study — "The study's the second door, not the first" — when she'd pretended to confuse it with the half bath. She hadn't confused anything. Cliff's correction placed him in a room he had no stated reason to enter, and it placed him there with enough familiarity to know the door order in the hallway. Financial records were inside the safe. Cliff was owed forty thousand dollars by the dead man. She didn't need to be the county's best detective to draw that line, and she wasn't the county's best detective — she was the county's only detective, which was a different thing.

The voicemail. Nate Griggs had spent forty-five minutes telling her about his podcast, his audience metrics, his recording setup, his microphone preferences, and the precise linguistic conventions of fantasy football trash talk, and in the middle of minute thirty-eight he'd referenced "the thing I left on Martin's phone, the voicemail, which — I mean, not that it matters, because it was just draft stuff." He'd said this with the specific desperation of a man who had rehearsed not mentioning something and then mentioned it anyway. Martin's phone, which was now in evidence, showed a deleted voicemail from Nate's number, received at 8:47 PM Friday and deleted at approximately 3:15 AM Saturday — after the death, before the call to police. Nate had deleted a message from a dead man's phone because the message contained the words "dead to me" in the context of fantasy football, and he was terrified that a county detective in rural Minnesota wouldn't understand the genre.

She took a sip of coffee. It was lukewarm and she drank it anyway.

The medications. The shared upstairs bathroom had a medicine cabinet that was too clean. Six men in their late forties sharing a bathroom for a weekend and the cabinet held a tube of toothpaste, a disposable razor, and a bottle of Advil. No prescription bottles, no allergy medication, no heartburn tablets, no sleep aids. Nothing. She'd opened the cabinet on Saturday afternoon and noted the emptiness the way she'd note a rug that had been recently vacuumed in a house where nobody admitted to vacuuming. Someone had cleared that cabinet out, and the only reason to do that was if the cabinet had contained something that someone didn't want found. Given that these were men approaching fifty, the most likely contents were the kinds of pills that middle-aged men didn't like other middle-aged men knowing they took. Antidepressants. Anti-anxiety medication. The pills that said you were struggling in a way the group's bluster and sports talk didn't make room for.

She wrote "meds — shame, not guilt" in her notebook and underlined it.

And the ghost. Sildenafil in Martin's blood, and no Viagra anywhere in the house. Not in his bedroom, not in his bathroom, not in his luggage. The ME had confirmed the level was consistent with a single dose taken four to six hours before death. Someone had been in a position to know that Martin had Viagra, and someone had removed every trace of it. She thought about the bathroom near the front door Saturday morning. When she'd arrived, someone had been in it. She'd heard the flush, the water running, the particular sounds of a person in a small room moving quickly. Tommy Renaldi had emerged a minute later looking like he'd run a half mile in a sauna. She'd asked him if he was feeling alright and he'd said "Fine, yeah, fine" in the way that people said fine when they were not fine but couldn't think of a better word on short notice.

Five.

She looked at her grid. Five separate acts of tampering. Five separate people. Five completely different motivations.

The body-mover was afraid of physical evidence — his touch on a dead man, his DNA in the wrong context. The safe-opener was looking for money. The voicemail-deleter was afraid of his own words. The medication-remover was afraid of his own prescriptions. The Viagra-remover was afraid he'd killed his friend with a joke.

She took off her glasses again. The chain clinked against the table.

She had been working this for thirty-six hours on the assumption that someone had either killed Martin Kessler or had helped cover up his killing. Every instinct she'd developed over twenty years told her that a scene this contaminated pointed to someone with a plan — one person, one motive, one cover-up that produced this mess.

But this wasn't one cover-up. This was five.

And none of them knew about the others.

She sat with that for a moment. The refrigerator cycled on. She waited for the glasses in the cabinet to finish rattling.

Five men in their late forties — soft in the middle, bad knees, reading glasses, the whole collection — sneaking through the same lake house between two and six in the morning. Each one convinced he was the only one with something to hide. Paul creeping into the bedroom to straighten Martin's body while Nate was downstairs in the basement deleting a voicemail from a dead man's phone. Tommy in the half bath flushing pills with shaking hands while Cliff rifled through the study safe twenty feet down the hall. Doug — it was Doug, it had to be Doug, the quiet one, the one who barely spoke during his interview, whose silence had made more noise than Nate's forty-five minutes of chatter — clearing his prescriptions from the upstairs cabinet while at least two other men were still moving through the house on their own midnight errands. All of them hearing the same floorboards, the same doors, the same third step from the top of the stairs that everyone knew to skip. All of them freezing when they heard someone else in the dark. All of them deciding it was nothing and going back to their tasks.

She laughed. It came out louder than she'd expected in the empty kitchen — a genuine, helpless sound that she covered with her hand, which was a habit from living alone. There was no one to hear her, but the habit persisted, the way her cats still knocked things off the nightstand even though she'd stopped keeping things on the nightstand three years ago.

A committee that never met.

That was the phrase that landed. Not a conspiracy. A committee of the whole — the county board language she'd sat through at too many Tuesday evening meetings in Detroit Lakes. A committee where every member had an independent agenda, no one coordinated with anyone else, and the collective output was more chaos than any single person could have produced on purpose.

It was the most absurd case she'd ever worked. She'd once investigated a snowmobile reported stolen in Richville that turned out to have been repossessed by the dealership, reported stolen by the owner who was six months behind on payments, and then actually stolen by the dealership owner's nephew, who rode it across Otter Tail Lake in March and went through the ice forty yards from shore.

This was better. This was five middle-aged men independently deciding, in the small hours after their friend's death, that each of them had something worth hiding. Five separate panic attacks, not one murder.

She opened the refrigerator and took out her casserole dish. She'd made the green bean casserole Friday evening when the call came in about a death at a lake house on Elbow, and she'd brought it Saturday morning because her mother had raised her to believe you don't show up empty-handed to a place where people are suffering. The foil was still on top, crimped neatly at the edges. Nobody had touched it. Twenty years of bringing casseroles to grief and she could count on one hand the number that actually got eaten, but she kept making them because the gesture mattered, or because she liked the cooking, or because it was easier to walk into a room full of suspects holding a Pyrex dish than holding a badge.

She peeled back the foil and ate a spoonful standing at the counter. It was better the second day. It was always better the second day.

She replaced the foil and put the dish back and sat down at the table. She wrote in her notebook: "Not a murder. Five tamperings. Five motives. No coordination." She underlined "no coordination" twice.

But the pen hovered.

If it wasn't murder — and the ME said it wasn't, the heart said it wasn't, the undiagnosed cardiomyopathy said it wasn't — then five people had independently decided that a natural death needed covering up. Why?

Paul: his DNA on a body he'd tried to resuscitate. He'd done CPR. It hadn't worked. He'd panicked about his fingerprints, his touch, his proximity — irrational, but grief made people irrational, and Paul was the kind of man whose profession ran on controlling exposure, and he'd been standing there looking at the biggest uncontrolled exposure of his life.

Cliff: the forty thousand. The dead man's estate, the documents, the financial records. A man whose business was failing and who'd been chasing that debt for two years saw an opportunity and took it. Not admirable, but not murderous.

Nate: a voicemail that said "dead to me" about a man who was now dead. Not incriminating to anyone with half a brain, but Nate Griggs was a man who processed the world through narration and performance, and his narration had turned on him with the timing of a bad joke.

Doug: medications. Shame. The quietest man in the group not wanting his oldest friends to know he was on pills for depression or anxiety or whatever the specific shame was. The saddest of the five tamperings, and the most innocent.

Tommy: the Viagra. He'd given Martin the pill as a joke. Martin was dead of a cardiac event. Tommy believed the pill killed his friend. It was a contributing factor, not the cause — the ME was clear — but Tommy didn't know that yet, and a high school football coach who'd handed his buddy a Viagra at a party and then that buddy dropped dead was a man with a guilt problem that didn't require a law degree to understand.

Five explanations. Five clean, sad, human explanations.

She should close it. Write it up. Five tamperers, one natural death, no criminal charges beyond the tampering itself, which she could pursue or not pursue depending on whether the county attorney wanted the paperwork.

But she didn't close her notebook.

Something in the shape of it. Not the five — the five made sense. What bothered her was the house itself. Martin Kessler had lived alone, divorced, no children, and he'd built a weekend ritual that brought six men to his lake house every September for twenty-five years. A man that deliberate, that careful about maintaining relationships — what else had he been maintaining? What other obligations lived in this house that she hadn't found?

She wouldn't know until she tested the theory. She closed the notebook, put on her blazer, and went to find someone precise.

---

Mallick found Paul Decker on the back deck, sitting in one of the Adirondack chairs with a beer he wasn't drinking. The September evening had turned cool enough to see breath, and Paul had a fleece pullover zipped to his throat. The lake was a flat dark sheet beyond the tree line. A loon called from somewhere across the water, and another answered from the left, and the two-note exchange was the only sound until Mallick let the screen door bang shut behind her.

"Oh, Mr. Decker. I'm sorry — I didn't realize anyone was still out here."

She had realized. She'd seen him through the kitchen window for the last twenty minutes.

"Just getting some air," Paul said. He didn't look at her.

"I don't blame you. That's some air in there tonight." She settled into the Adirondack chair beside his, which required negotiation — the chairs were built for taller people and her feet didn't quite reach the deck slats when she sat back. She tucked them under herself. "Isn't this something, though. The lake at night. You can see the stars coming out over the far shore."

"Yeah."

"My mother had a place up near Park Rapids when I was growing up. Not as nice as this. An A-frame too, though. She loved an A-frame. There's something about those ceilings, the way they come down on both sides. Makes you feel held, doesn't it?"

Paul glanced at her. She could see him calculating — whether this was a conversation or an interview, whether the A-frame observation was small talk or the first layer of something. Paul Decker was the kind of man who calculated, not out of suspicion but because his profession ran on risk assessment and the accurate categorization of incoming information.

"It does," he said. "Martin's parents built this one in the seventies."

"Mmm. You can tell. The paneling's got that warm look to it. My mother's place had the same. Pine, is it?"

"Knotty pine. Yeah."

"Knotty pine. There it is." She adjusted her blazer against the chill. "I should let you sit in peace. I just had a question — and it's probably nothing. You know me and my questions."

Paul set his beer on the wide armrest. His posture shifted by a degree that someone less attentive would have missed — the shoulders squaring, the spine straightening against the chair's slant. "Sure."

"I was going over my notes — I'm terrible with notes, I write them in this chicken scratch and then I can't read half of what I put down. But I was looking at Saturday morning, the sequence of events, and I just wanted to clarify something about Martin's phone."

"His phone?"

"His cell phone, yes. The big one, one of those Samsungs. Did you happen to notice anyone near Martin's phone that night? After you found him?"

Paul's brow creased. Not the crease of a man formulating a lie — the crease of a man searching an honest memory and finding nothing there. "His phone? No. I was — I wasn't thinking about his phone. I was with him. With Martin. I was trying to—" He stopped. "I was in the bedroom."

"Of course. Of course you were. You were right there with him." She let the silence sit for a beat. The loon called again. "So you wouldn't have seen if anyone, say, picked it up? Used it?"

"No. I was in the bedroom. I was doing CPR and then I was — I was just with him." The repetition wasn't evasion. It was the sound of a man whose Saturday night had a single fixed point, and everything else had dissolved around it.

She nodded. "Sure. That makes sense." She looked out at the lake as if the question had been answered to her satisfaction, which it had. Paul's confusion was genuine — the clean confusion of a man who'd been on his knees beside a body and hadn't registered anything outside that room. The phone was someone else's errand entirely.

"While I'm bothering you," she said, as if the thought had just occurred to her, "the upstairs bathroom. The one you all share."

"What about it?"

"Did you notice what was in the medicine cabinet? Before Saturday morning, I mean. Friday, say."

"The medicine cabinet."

"I'm asking because it was practically empty when I looked Saturday afternoon. Just Advil and a razor. I thought that was a little light for a group of — well." She smiled. "Men our age." She was fifty-two and she kept four prescriptions in her own bathroom cabinet, and she didn't feel any shame about it, but she understood that some men did, especially men who existed in groups where vulnerability got expressed through beer choices and fantasy football trash talk rather than actual words.

Paul shook his head. "I didn't — I don't think I noticed. We've got our own stuff in our bags, mostly. The cabinet's more for communal stuff. Tylenol and such."

"Communal stuff," she repeated. "Sure." She waited a beat. "And you wouldn't know if someone took their prescriptions out of there? After Friday night?"

"No. I didn't go in the upstairs bathroom that night. I was in the master bedroom with Martin and then the hallway and then — no." He paused. "Why? Is there something about the bathroom?"

"Oh, probably nothing. I'm just checking boxes. You know how it is — the paperwork side of things. My office wants everything accounted for." She waved a hand in the direction of bureaucracy generally.

Paul was quiet for a moment. She let the quiet work. The loons had moved farther across the lake — their calls were fainter now, more distant. Inside the house, someone ran water briefly and stopped.

"Can I ask you something?" Paul said.

"Of course."

"The safe. In the study. Did someone open it?"

She looked at him. This was a gift — she hadn't asked about the safe, and he'd brought it up himself. Paul Decker's assessment engine was running on its own power, and it had snagged on something he wanted to know.

"What makes you ask about the safe?"

"Martin always left the dial one click off. It's just — he did it every year. He'd close it and then not quite spin the dial all the way. We all knew. It was one of his things." He took a breath. "If someone was going through the house that night, they would've found it. I should've mentioned it before. I didn't think of it until today."

"Mmm." She let the sound carry its own weight. "And you didn't go into the study that night."

"No."

"But you knew about the safe. About the dial."

"Everyone knew about the safe. Martin showed it to us years ago. He was proud of it — the combination, the mounting, the whole thing. He'd show people and say 'Don't tell anyone,' and then he'd show the next person. And we'd all pretend we hadn't seen it before." The faintest smile. "That was Martin."

"That's nice," she said. She meant it. It was a detail about a dead man that made him more real — a man who wanted to share his secrets and call them secrets anyway. She filed it under the part of her job that wasn't about solving anything, the part that was about remembering the person the investigation was built on top of.

"So you didn't go in the study. You didn't notice the medicine cabinet. And you weren't near Martin's phone," she said. She said it the way she might list things she'd picked up at the grocery store — bread, milk, eggs — just ticking items off, conversational, done.

Paul looked at her. He was holding his beer but his hand had stopped moving. The condensation was running down the bottle and over his knuckles and he didn't seem to notice.

There it was. The moment she'd been building toward, and she watched him arrive at it. She'd asked him about three pieces of evidence. He'd been genuinely ignorant of all three. But he was not a stupid man, and the pattern of her questions was drawing its own picture. She was asking about the phone, the medications, the safe — specific tamperings, specific locations — and he knew nothing about any of them. Which meant she already knew he'd done something else. And which meant she knew the something-elses were done by different people.

"Detective," he said.

"Hmm?"

"You're asking about different parts of the house."

"I am."

"Because you don't think it was one person."

She let the loon answer. A single call, no response. The other one had gone quiet.

"Mr. Decker, I think you moved Martin's body and wiped the surfaces you'd touched during CPR. I think you did that because you loved your friend and you panicked and your training in claims adjustment told you that your DNA at a death scene was an exposure you couldn't account for. I think that's all you did. And I think that while you were doing that, other people in this house were doing their own things, for their own reasons, and none of you spoke to each other about it, and by morning the whole place looked like a crime scene designed by a committee."

"A committee," Paul said. His voice was flat.

"A committee that never met." She looked at him. The reading glasses caught the light from the kitchen window behind them. "I think everyone in this house was scared of something Saturday morning. I just don't think any of you were scared of the same thing."

Paul stared at the lake. His jaw worked once — the small, tight movement of a man processing something he couldn't argue with. The beer was still in his hand. He still hadn't drunk from it.

"You know I can't—" he started.

"I'm not asking you to tell me what anyone else did. I already know more than you'd expect, and honestly, Mr. Decker, I think most of what happened in this house that night was sad and dumb and very human."

He let out a breath. It hung in the cool air for a moment and dissolved. "It was a natural death. The ME said so."

"The ME said so."

"Then why are you still—"

"Because five people tampered with a natural death, and I'd like to understand why, and I'd like to be sure that all five reasons are as sad and as simple as I think they are." She paused. "That's all. That's the whole thing."

Paul sat with that for a while. The refrigerator cycled on inside the kitchen, faintly audible through the screen door. Somewhere in the house, a floorboard creaked — someone moving through a room, someone getting water, or someone just shifting their weight in a chair.

"The casserole," Mallick said. "Did you try it?"

"What?"

"My casserole. The green bean. It's in the fridge."

"No." He blinked. "No, I didn't."

"It's better the second day," she said. She stood from the Adirondack chair, which required a small graceless push off both armrests that she'd stopped being self-conscious about years ago. "You should try it. There'll be a third day too, the way things are going."

She went back inside. The screen door banged behind her. Paul sat alone on the deck with his warming beer and the lake and the stars she'd pointed out and the understanding that the detective knew exactly what he'd done, and that what he'd done was the least of what had happened in this house.

In the kitchen, Mallick sat back down at her table and opened her notebook. She wrote: "PD confirmed — body and surfaces only. Ignorant of phone, meds, safe. Minimum two independent tamperers. Likely five."

She underlined "five" and looked at the page.

Five panic attacks. Not one murder. Five sad, frightened, thoroughly human decisions made by men in the dark who should have called the police and gone to bed.

She should close the case.

She didn't close the case.

The house settled around her, creaking the way old lake houses creak when the temperature drops and the wood contracts. Through the kitchen window, she could see the stone path that led through the pines and birches to the dock — eighty yards of pale stone in the dark, ending at the wooden dock that extended thirty feet into the lake. The dock was a dark line against the darker water.

She thought about the five men in this house, each holding his own piece of the night like a stolen object he couldn't put down. They were all wrong — that was the comic part. The Viagra guilt, the voicemail panic, the medication shame, the financial snooping, the CPR paranoia — all of it was the wreckage of a death that nobody caused and nobody could accept as uncaused.

But twenty years had taught her that "probably" was where the real work lived. Most cases were exactly what they looked like, and this one looked like five idiots in a lake house. The cases that weren't what they looked like were the ones where "probably" had a splinter in it — a single detail doing more work than it should, a piece of evidence that was clean when everything around it was dirty, or dirty when everything around it was clean.

She couldn't name the splinter. She couldn't even point to which piece of evidence contained it. But the shape of the case — five panics arranged around one natural death — had a lopsidedness she couldn't account for. Five explanations, each one sufficient, each one human, and still the picture tilted.

Maybe the tilt was nothing. Maybe this was just a dead man and five frightened friends and a casserole nobody ate and a county detective who'd been at this long enough to see ghosts in clean rooms.

She closed the notebook. She'd sleep on it. In the morning the shape would either settle flat or it wouldn't.

The refrigerator cycled off. The glasses in the cabinet above the sink stopped their rattling. Mallick rinsed her coffee mug, dried it, and set it on the counter next to the coffeemaker because she'd want it first thing and it was already clean and why open the cabinet twice. She put the foil back on the casserole dish, though she'd already put the foil back, so she was just pressing it down — a thing to do with her hands while her mind ran the grid one more time.

She turned off the kitchen light and went upstairs to the guest bedroom the deputies had cleared for her. The third step from the top creaked under her weight, and she wondered how many of the five had remembered to skip it in the dark.


# Chapter 11

Paul woke at five-forty on Monday morning with the specific, organized thought that someone needed to go through Martin's things.

Not the bedroom — not again. He'd been in the bedroom once already, Saturday morning, the CPR, his palms flat against Martin's sternum pushing rhythmically into a body that gave back nothing. His hands had been sore for two days afterward, a deep bruise across the heels of both palms that he kept pressing absently, testing. The bedroom was police evidence now, or it had been, though the deputies had cleared out Sunday afternoon and Mallick hadn't explicitly sealed the rooms. She'd said to keep things as they were, a suggestion that was either casual or tactical and that Paul, after two days of watching her, could not determine.

What he meant was the practical work. The paperwork. Martin was divorced from Linda, no kids, self-employed. No HR department filing bereavement forms. No corporate life insurance policy with a named beneficiary triggering the right cascade of phone calls. Someone needed to find Martin's lawyer's contact information. Someone needed to locate the mortgage documents, the financial accounts, the insurance policies. Someone needed to figure out what happened next, procedurally, to a lake house on Elbow Lake when its owner died during a fantasy football draft and five of his oldest friends independently decided to redecorate the scene.

Paul was the right person for this. He'd been an insurance claims adjuster for twenty-two years. He knew the paperwork of death the way Martin had known the paperwork of money — intimately, professionally, without sentiment. He'd walked through forty or fifty houses where someone had died and assessed the property value of what remained. It was not a romantic skill. It was not something he mentioned at parties. But it was his, and this morning it was what he could offer.

He got out of the pullout couch in the basement — he and Joel had taken the basement beds after the deputies cleared the upstairs, neither of them wanting to sleep in the rooms adjacent to Martin's. Joel was still asleep, or pretending to be: on his back, one arm over his eyes, breathing with the measured deliberateness of a man who'd been lying awake for hours. Paul dressed quietly. He took the stairs. The third step creaked the way it had creaked for twenty-five years, and this morning he didn't bother skipping it. It didn't matter who heard him.

The kitchen smelled like old coffee and the faintly botanical sourness of the green bean casserole that Detective Mallick had brought on Saturday and that nobody had touched. Paul could see the edge of the casserole dish through the glass refrigerator door, sitting behind two six-packs and the container of Anne's potato salad. Martin had complimented the potato salad at Thursday's dinner — not generically, but with the specific, excessive praise that was Martin's way. He'd told Paul the ratio of mustard to mayonnaise was ideal, that Anne clearly understood the fundamentals, and that Paul should let her know Martin said so. Paul had relayed the message. Anne, on the phone, had gotten embarrassed and pleased, and Paul had felt the familiar, low-wattage irritation of a man whose wife was charmed by his best friend's effortless authority over small kindnesses.

He poured coffee from the pot Nate had set to auto-brew at five. Nate was up somewhere — Paul could hear the low mumble of his voice from the direction of the deck. Narrating, probably. Processing the weekend through the only mechanism Nate had: talking into a microphone, organizing experience into segments, finding the rhythm of it. Paul didn't blame him. Everyone had their mechanism. Paul's was paperwork.

He drank the coffee standing at the kitchen window. The fog was low and gray over the lake, the dock dissolving into it thirty yards from shore. A loon called from somewhere in the mist, and the sound carried the way lake sounds always carried — flattened and amplified at the same time, closer than the bird actually was. He put the mug in the sink and went upstairs to Martin's room.

The door was open. The bed had been stripped by the county deputies — just the bare mattress now, white with a yellowish stain near the center that Paul's brain cataloged automatically as old, pre-existing, unrelated to the death. Sweat, probably. Martin ran hot. He slept without a top sheet half the year and had never bothered with a mattress protector, which Paul had mentioned exactly once, in the context of resale value, and which Martin had received with the look he reserved for Paul's most insurance-brained observations.

Martin's suitcase sat on the floor by the dresser, unzipped, clothes erupting from it in the way that meant Martin had been digging for something specific and had not bothered to repack. Three polo shirts — the expensive kind that Martin bought to look casual, as if a sixty-dollar polo was casual — two pairs of khakis, boxers, a balled pair of running socks that Martin almost certainly hadn't run in. The dopp kit was on the dresser with the zipper half open. Paul looked through it: deodorant, a disposable razor, a bottle of ibuprofen, dental floss still in its original sealed packaging.

The floss made him stop. Not because it was significant but because it was Martin. Twenty-five years of the same running joke: Martin announcing at every draft that he'd finally committed to flossing regularly, Paul asking to see the receipt, Martin calling Paul the most tedious man in North America, and someone — Nate, usually — pointing out that this was, objectively, the least interesting recurring conversation in their twenty-five-year friendship. Martin's dentist knew he didn't floss. Paul knew. Martin knew Paul knew. The sealed package was the punchline to a joke that had been running since they were twenty-two years old and that would now, Paul understood, never have a new installment.

He set the floss down on the dresser. He didn't pocket it. He wanted to, and the wanting embarrassed him, because it was a three-dollar package of waxed floss and Martin was dead and Paul's grief was apparently finding its expression in the desire to steal dental supplies.

Martin's reading glasses were on the nightstand — the cheap drugstore magnifiers he'd been wearing for five years, the ones Paul had been telling him to replace with a real prescription. The thriller novel beside them had a folded page at about the two-thirds mark: Martin had been in the middle of something when he died. Paul picked up the book, looked at the cover, set it back. He didn't fold the page back. He left it where Martin had left it, at the last page Martin would ever read, marking a place no one would return to.

The room smelled like Martin's deodorant and something else — the Lagavulin, maybe, a whisper of peat smoke embedded in the curtains, the pillow, the years. Paul stood in the middle of the room and felt what it felt like to be in his dead best friend's space, going through his things, handling objects that were, technically, no longer Martin's but were not yet anyone else's either. It felt like adjusting a claim on a property where the owner had died. The comps changed. The history changed. You walked through the same rooms and they were not the same rooms, because the dying had restructured the value of everything inside them.

He left the bedroom and went down the hall to the study.

The door to the study was open. It had been open since the deputies went through on Saturday, and Paul had glanced in once or twice since then without going in, the way you pass an office that belongs to someone who isn't there yet. Martin's desk was the same organized chaos it had always been — papers in stacks that looked random but that Martin could navigate with a precision that made Paul's filing system look crude. The bookshelves held financial manuals, thriller novels with creased spines, and a set of lake maps dating back to when Martin's parents had bought the house in 1982. A coffee mug with a dried brown ring sat on a stack of papers. The room smelled like dust and paper and the faint turpentine whiff of old wood polish.

The painting was crooked.

It was a landscape of the lake — an oil painting Martin's mother had commissioned from a local artist two decades ago, competent but uninspired, the kind of art that existed to fill a wall rather than to be looked at. Martin had hung it behind the desk, and for twenty-five years it had hung straight, because Martin noticed when things were off-kilter and corrected them. Now it was tilted, maybe two inches to the left. And behind it, Paul could see the dark edge of the wall safe.

The safe was open. Not all the way — the door was pulled out maybe four inches, the combination dial set to what looked like its usual position, one tick from locked. Martin's habit. Martin had told Paul the combination once, years ago, during one of those conversations that Martin began with "if anything ever happens to me" and that Paul had received the way he received all such conversations: gravely, precisely, filing it away in the mental cabinet where he stored things that were probably unnecessary but whose absence would be catastrophic. The combination was Linda's birthday. June ninth, zero-six-zero-nine. Paul remembered it the same way he remembered policy numbers: automatically, permanently, without emotion.

Someone had been in the safe. The deputies, maybe. Or someone else. Paul pulled the painting aside — it swung easily on its hook, the wall anchor slightly loose — and opened the safe door the rest of the way.

Inside, a manila folder of financial documents: tax returns, quarterly statements, a copy of the house deed, correspondence from Martin's accountant. A smaller envelope with cash — Paul counted it automatically, the way he counted anything that could be itemized. Eight hundred and sixty dollars in twenties and fifties. A sealed manila envelope, letter-sized, with the letters "RT" written on it in Martin's handwriting. And beneath everything, in a clear plastic sleeve, a document.

Paul took the document out first.

It was Martin's will. *Last Will and Testament of Martin David Kessler*, dated March 14, 2023. Eighteen months ago. Six pages, typed, notarized, with Martin's signature at the bottom of each page — the firm, slightly oversized signature that Martin used for everything from contracts to birthday cards. Martin had used a real lawyer, a Minneapolis estate planning firm whose name Paul recognized from bus bench advertisements, the same benches where Paul's insurance office bought ad space.

He read it standing up, his back against the desk, the safe open behind him.

The first page was boilerplate — appointment of the personal representative, revocation of prior wills. The second page specified financial accounts and personal property: the investment portfolio went to Martin's sister Carol in Duluth, the car to a cousin Paul had met once at a barbecue, a donation to the University of Minnesota Athletic Department. Standard distribution. Generous but not surprising. Martin was organized even in death, which was exactly what Paul would have expected and exactly what Paul had always quietly admired — the precision of a man who ran his life the way Paul ran his claims: everything accounted for, every contingency addressed, every beneficiary named.

The third page was the lake house.

Paul read the paragraph once, quickly. He read it again, slower, with the attention he gave to coverage language when he suspected a gap — looking for the qualifier, the exception, the dependent clause that would make the plain text mean something other than what it appeared to mean on first pass. He read it a third time, word by word, the way he'd been trained to read exclusion riders.

There was no qualifier. The language was unambiguous.

Martin had left the lake house on Elbow Lake — all structures, land, and contents therein — to Thomas James Renaldi.

Tommy.

Not Paul. Not his best friend of twenty-five years, his best man, the person who had the combination to the safe and the spare key to the front door and the standing invitation to use the house any weekend between Labor Day and Memorial Day. Not Martin's sister Carol, who had spent childhood summers here. Not the university. Not the group collectively, which would have made a certain sentimental sense — the house as a trust for the annual draft, the tradition preserved.

Tommy Renaldi. High school football coach. Married to Denise. Three kids. A man who, as far as Paul had understood Martin's life, occupied the same tier of friendship as the rest of them — present, valued, one of the seven. Not first among equals. Not the heir to the thing Martin loved most.

Paul sat down in Martin's desk chair. The chair creaked under him, the wood-and-leather protest it had been making for years. The armrests were smooth where Martin's forearms had rested during decades of phone calls and calculations. Paul put his own arms on those smooth places and felt the shape that Martin's weight had worn into the leather.

He looked at the will again. Page three. *Thomas James Renaldi.*

What arrived was not grief. Grief he could have handled. Grief was appropriate, expected, a claims-adjuster response to total loss — you documented it, you processed it, you adjusted. What arrived instead was flatter and more humiliating: he was jealous. His best friend of twenty-five years was dead four days, and Paul's first response to the will was the petty, undeniable indignation of a man who had been assuming, for a quarter century, that he was Martin's person.

He had been Martin's best man. He had driven Martin to the lawyer during the divorce from Linda — five separate trips, because the proceedings dragged and Martin refused to handle it by email. He'd sat with Martin through two Christmases after the divorce when Martin was alone and drinking too much, Lagavulin in quantities that made Paul count ounces and then stop counting because counting wasn't helping and Martin wasn't listening. He'd lent Martin his truck three times. He'd listened to Martin talk about the consulting business for what amounted to probably a thousand cumulative hours over twenty-two years, and he had never, not once, said what he sometimes thought, which was: Martin, I have my own work, and my work is boring to you, and you have never once asked me about a claim the way I ask you about a client.

And Martin had left the house to Tommy.

Paul set the will down on the desk. He looked at the rest of the safe's contents. The manila envelope labeled "RT." He picked it up, felt the weight of it — several pages, the paper thick, something handwritten inside. He turned it over. No other markings. He put it back. Whatever else Martin had been keeping in this safe, whatever RT meant, Paul was not ready for it. He was not, at this particular moment, capable of processing additional revelations about his best friend's private architecture.

He was still, for the moment, adjusting to the fact that he was not the primary beneficiary of a dead man's loyalty.

He left the study and did not close the safe.

---

The chair Paul sat in on the deck was the one he thought of as Martin's, even though Martin had never claimed it. It was the Adirondack nearest the lake, angled to catch the afternoon sun, with a knot in the left armrest that Martin rested his scotch glass against — a natural coaster that Martin had discovered their first summer here and had used every year since, and that Paul knew about because Paul noticed things like that and Martin knew Paul noticed things like that and neither of them had ever mentioned it.

Paul sat in the chair now. Not claiming it. Just sitting in it and looking at what Martin looked at when he sat here.

The fog had lifted. The lake was silver and flat, and across the water a pontoon boat was cutting a slow, purposeless path, trailing a wake that took a long time to reach the near shore. The pines along the bank were dark against the lightening sky. A second loon called — farther away this time, the sound skimming the water's surface.

He thought about the time Martin showed up at his house with a chainsaw.

This was 2017, maybe 2018 — a spring storm had dropped a branch the size of a small tree onto Paul's garage. Not catastrophic, but the branch was too heavy for Paul to move alone and too awkward for a simple saw. Paul had scheduled a tree service for the following week. Martin had arrived Saturday morning without calling, a borrowed chainsaw in the bed of his truck and a look on his face that said he had already decided how this was going to go.

Martin had never used a chainsaw. This became evident quickly. He held it like a man who had watched a YouTube video once and retained the confidence but not the technique.
