He spent four hours cutting the branch into sections while Paul stood in the driveway offering corrections that Martin did not accept, and by the end of the afternoon Martin had gouged the garage siding in two places, nicked the gutter, and cut a shallow, bleeding line across his own forearm that he dismissed as cosmetic. Paul had pointed out, with the measured exasperation of a man who assessed property damage for a living, that the tree service would have cost a hundred and fifty dollars and the siding repair would run four hundred.

Martin had looked at him, sweating through his shirt, a piece of bark in his hair, the cut on his arm beading blood onto his watch, and said: "Yeah, but they'd have taken three weeks to schedule it, and you'd have spent every one of those nights calling me about the estimated damage to your garage. I know you, Deck. I did this for me."

He was right. Paul would have called. Probably not every night, but often enough that Martin's preemptive strike with a borrowed chainsaw was, in its stupid and destructive way, an act of genuine understanding. That was Martin. His generosity was aggressive and sometimes more expensive than the problem it solved, but it came from knowing people — knowing specifically what they needed, what they'd worry about, what would keep them up — and deciding to handle it before they could turn it into a project.

Paul sat in the chair and missed his friend with a force that made the jealousy feel petty and the pettiness feel correct, because Martin would have found it funny. Martin would have loved that Paul was sitting here, four days after Martin's death, furious about a will. He would have said something like: you're adjusting the claim, Deck. You're looking at the disbursement schedule and you don't like the numbers. And Paul would have said: the numbers don't make sense, Martin. They don't add up.

The numbers didn't add up.

Paul went back through the file. Twenty-five years of data points, organized the way he organized claims — chronologically first, then sorted by pattern, looking for the anomaly that would reclassify the whole account.

He started with what he'd never questioned. Martin was protective of Tommy. This was not, on its surface, unusual. Martin was protective of everyone. He'd covered Nate's rent once, disguising the payment as a podcasting fee. He'd called Doug every week after the divorce. He'd lent money to Cliff — though Cliff, as everyone now knew, considered the forty thousand a debt rather than a gift. Martin took care of his people. Martin held the group together. Martin was the gravitational center, and his protectiveness was the force that kept them all in orbit.

But with Tommy, the protectiveness had a different quality. Paul could see it now, the way you see a pattern in a claims history when someone finally gives you the piece that reframes the file.

2019. Tommy tried to skip the draft. He'd called Paul about it — some coaching conflict, a scrimmage he couldn't move. Paul had said he understood. Martin had driven to Tommy's house. Paul learned this months later, through Nate, in the middle of an unrelated conversation. *Martin went and got him. Picked him up like a goddamn extraction.* Paul had laughed. That was Martin. He wanted the full group, didn't tolerate opt-outs.

But Tommy had tried to skip before. 2013, the year Tommy's oldest was starting high school and Denise was working weekends at the clinic. Tommy called Martin directly that time, not the group. Paul didn't know what Martin said. He only knew that Tommy came, and that Martin, who could get loudly furious about things like Joel temporarily joining a different league, had handled Tommy's absence threat with a private conversation that nobody else was privy to. No loud objection. No guilt trip at the dinner table. Just a conversation, and then Tommy was there on Thursday like he'd never wavered.

2008. Tommy's daughter was born — his second kid. Tommy said he couldn't make the draft weekend. Martin rescheduled the entire event. Moved it a week later, ate the cost of a venue change when the original cabin wasn't available, told the group the shift was for "logistical reasons" that he didn't elaborate on. Paul had noticed, in the way Paul noticed everything, that Martin had not rescheduled when Nate's mother was in the hospital in 2011 or when Joel's divorce proceedings conflicted with the 2016 draft. Those years, Martin had expressed sympathy and said the draft would miss them and moved on. For Tommy, Martin moved the draft.

Paul thought about the smaller things. The refills. Martin was generous with drinks — everyone's glass stayed full, that was part of the host performance, the lake house as Martin's stage. But with Tommy, the attention was different. Not more generous, exactly. More watchful. Martin would notice Tommy's beer getting low before Tommy noticed it himself, and he'd get up to replace it without comment, without the usual "who needs another one" announcement that accompanied everyone else's refill. It was the difference between hospitality and monitoring.

And the conversations. Martin talked to everyone — that was his gift, the thing that made him the center. But his conversations with Tommy had a different architecture. They happened on the deck, usually. The two of them in adjacent chairs, voices low, the exchange private in a way that the draft weekend didn't ordinarily allow for. Paul had walked out there a few times over the years, and both times he'd noticed the same thing: a brief, involuntary pause in the conversation, the kind that happens when someone enters a room where a separate discussion is already underway. Martin would smile, invite Paul to sit, change the subject seamlessly. Tommy would lean back and look at the lake. The previous conversation would not resume.

Paul had never pushed. He'd assumed it was Martin being Martin — building individual relationships within the group, doing the one-on-one maintenance that kept the whole machine running. Martin had private conversations with Paul too. They talked about Anne, about Paul's work, about money and kids and the specific frustrations of being forty-seven and noticing that the life you'd built was exactly what you'd planned and somehow less than you'd imagined. Those conversations were real. Paul didn't doubt that.

But Martin hadn't left Paul the house.

Paul thought about the year Tommy's coaching staff got investigated for grade manipulation — 2015 or 2016, Paul couldn't pin it. Tommy had been distressed, louder than usual, pacing the deck. The group had ribbed him about it, the way they ribbed everyone about everything. Nate had done a whole bit about Tommy's team being the only squad in Minnesota history whose offensive line read at a fourth-grade level, and it was funny, and Tommy had laughed. But Martin had shut it down. Not immediately — he'd let the joke run its course, let Tommy take it and give it back. But when Nate circled back to it an hour later, Martin had said, quietly, that they could probably find something else to talk about. And Nate had stopped. They all had. Because when Martin said something quietly, it landed differently than when Martin said something loud, and everyone in the group understood the distinction even if none of them could have articulated it.

Martin protected Tommy from sustained examination. That was the pattern. Not from jokes — Tommy could take jokes, Tommy lived on jokes. But from anything that pressed too hard on Tommy's life, Tommy's choices, Tommy's past. Martin would let the conversation approach a certain depth with Tommy and then redirect it, not obviously, not clumsily, but with the practiced ease of a man who had been managing a particular boundary for a long time.

Twenty-five years. Martin had been managing Tommy for twenty-five years.

Paul considered the possibilities. The obvious one: Martin and Tommy had been involved. It was 2024 — the idea of two men in their late forties carrying on a concealed relationship was not outlandish, and it would explain the protectiveness, the private conversations, the will. But it didn't fit. Paul had been around Martin and women enough to know that Martin's attractions ran in one direction, and the Martin-Karen revelation from Sunday — Martin sleeping with Joel's ex-wife — confirmed it. And Tommy had three kids with Denise and a marriage that, as far as Paul could observe over twenty-five years of weekends, was tired but functional in the way that long marriages among men their age tended to be.

Not romance. Not sex. Something else.

Paul thought about the word obligation. It was an insurance word. A contractual duty. An enforceable commitment arising from an event or agreement that bound one party to another. Obligations had origins — something happened, a loss occurred, a liability was incurred, and the obligation attached. The obligation continued until it was discharged, settled, or extinguished by limitation.

Martin had been obligated to Tommy. The word fit the shape of what Paul was seeing. The protectiveness wasn't friendship operating at a higher frequency. It was management — careful, consistent, long-term management of a person and a situation that required ongoing attention. Martin had been managing Tommy the way Paul managed a long-tail liability: monitoring the exposure, keeping the file current, ensuring that the underlying claim didn't develop into something worse.

What was the underlying claim?

Paul didn't know. The lake house was worth — what, four-fifty? Maybe five hundred, the way lakeshore in Becker County had gone. Martin had left Tommy half a million dollars in property. A high school football coach's salary in suburban Minneapolis was not a lake house salary. Tommy hadn't contributed to the mortgage. He hadn't co-invested. If anything, the financial flow went the other direction — Martin had covered Tommy's draft expenses more than once, and Tommy had never objected in the proud, stiff way he should have if the payments were between equals.

So not a financial debt. Not a business arrangement. Something else, something older, something that had been running for twenty-five years and that Martin had managed until the moment he died, at which point the management transferred to the will — the final instruction from a dead man who had been protecting Tommy Renaldi from something Paul couldn't yet see.

Paul sat in Martin's chair and watched the lake. The knot in the armrest pressed against his forearm where Martin's scotch glass had sat ten thousand times. He could smell pine resin warming in the morning sun, and the mineral-wet scent of the lake, and somewhere inside the house the refrigerator cycled on with the low, grinding hum it had been making since the nineties.

He thought about going back to the study. The envelope labeled "RT." Whatever was in it might answer the question. Or it might open a worse one.

He wasn't ready. Not because he was afraid of what he'd find — Paul's professional life was built on opening files that other people didn't want to look at — but because he understood, with the clarity that came from twenty-two years of adjusting other people's losses, that some information restructured everything that came before it. You couldn't unread a document. You couldn't unfind a fact. Once you knew what was in the file, the file was yours, and the file's consequences were yours, and whatever action the file demanded was on your desk until you either processed it or closed it, and Paul Decker had never in his career closed a file he'd opened.

So he sat. He watched the pontoon boat complete its slow circuit of the far shore. He listened to the loon and the refrigerator and the distant murmur of Nate's voice from around the corner of the house, narrating something to no one. He thought about Martin's gap-toothed smile when he'd shown up with that chainsaw, proud and incompetent and absolutely certain he was helping.

Martin had been his best friend. Paul did not doubt this. Martin's friendship with Paul had been real — the conversations, the Christmases, the chainsaw, the potato salad compliments, the twenty-five years of showing up. All real. All genuine.

It just hadn't been the most important friendship in Martin's life. That distinction belonged to Tommy, and the will was the proof that it wasn't casual.

Paul sat in the dead man's chair and felt the specific, humiliating comedy of a man who had spent twenty-five years as the best friend and had only just learned he was second.


# Chapter 12

Tommy was alone on the deck for the first time since Saturday morning. Monday afternoon, the sun low enough to turn the lake copper, and he had maybe fifteen minutes before someone came out to check on him or ask if he wanted another round of Mallick's questions or share their feelings about Martin or whatever the fuck else these guys couldn't stop doing.

He'd pulled one of the Adirondack chairs to the rail where he could put his feet up and watch the water. His hands were around a can of Busch Light that had gone warm twenty minutes ago. He wasn't drinking it. He was holding it the way he held a football before a snap drill — not for the thing itself but because his hands needed something to do.

It had been four days. Four days since Martin, and the house felt like a locker room after a loss that shouldn't have happened — everybody walking around like they'd forgotten how hallways worked, picking up cups and putting them back down, starting sentences and letting them die. Paul had been in Martin's study all morning doing whatever Paul did when he was processing, which meant reorganizing information into categories nobody asked for. Joel was somewhere being Joel, which meant radiating controlled fury at a man who couldn't receive it anymore. Doug was on the couch reading the same page of a magazine he'd been reading since breakfast. Nate was in the basement doing something with his phone that Tommy hoped wasn't podcast-related but probably was.

And Mallick had gone back to Detroit Lakes. Working the case, supposedly. Tommy didn't believe a goddamn word of it. That woman cataloged information the way Tommy cataloged opponent tendencies — every mistake filed, every tell indexed, waiting for the film review where it all made sense. She'd be back.

The screen door opened and banged shut behind him. Tommy didn't turn around.

"Hey." Cliff's voice. Easy, clean, the voice of a man who always sounded like he was about to offer you a deal on a time-share. "Brought you one."

A cold Busch Light appeared in Tommy's peripheral vision, held out at chest height. Cliff stood there in his pressed chinos and his collared polo — the guy had been at a lake house for four days and he still dressed like he was showing a property. His silver hair was combed. He'd shaved that morning. Tommy looked like a man who'd been sleeping in his clothes because he had been.

"Thanks." Tommy took the beer with his left hand and set the warm one on the deck. He cracked the new one. Cliff settled into the chair beside him, close enough that their armrests were six inches apart, which was closer than Cliff usually sat.

"Hell of a view," Cliff said.

"Yeah."

"Martin always had taste. I'll give him that. The man knew real estate, even if he didn't know—" Cliff paused, adjusted. "Even when it came to personal finance, the eye was there."

Tommy drank. The beer was cold enough to taste like something.

"You doing okay?" Cliff asked.

"I'm fine."

"Because you look like shit, if I'm being honest."

"I look like a guy who's been in the same house for four days with a dead friend and a detective who thinks I rearranged furniture in my sleep. So yeah. Shit."

Cliff laughed. Not his sales laugh — something closer to real. "Fair enough. Fair enough." He drank his own beer, some craft thing in a can Tommy didn't recognize. Of course Cliff brought his own beer to a lake house stocked with Busch Light and Miller High Life. The man couldn't drink a regular beer if you held a gun to his pressed chinos. "Listen, I wanted to talk to you about something."

"Okay."

"Not a big thing. Well — it's a thing. But I think it's a conversation we should have had a while ago, and the circumstances have kind of accelerated the timeline."

Tommy looked at him. Cliff was doing the thing he always did when he was working — leaning forward slightly in his chair, his beer resting on his knee, his posture communicating openness and accessibility. Tommy had watched Cliff do this at a hundred barbecues, a hundred draft nights, a hundred situations where Cliff was turning a person into a transaction. Cliff sold things. That was what he was. He sold real estate and he sold himself and right now he was selling something to Tommy on the deck of a dead man's house and he'd brought beer because Cliff always brought something.

"What conversation?"

"The money conversation. Martin's estate. The forty thousand he owed me."

Tommy shrugged. "Talk to a lawyer. That's not my thing."

"Right, and I will. Eventually. But here's what I'm thinking — and I'm just putting this out there, no pressure." Cliff paused long enough for the sentence to breathe, a technique Tommy recognized as the same one he used on anxious buyers. Let them sit in it. "You and Martin were tight. Tighter than I think some of the guys realize."

"We were friends."

"You were more than friends, Tommy. I mean — not like that." Cliff waved the implication off with his beer hand. "I mean Martin took care of you in ways that went beyond the regular friendship stuff. The way he always made sure you came back for the draft, even the years you tried to skip. Those little conversations you'd have, just the two of you. Out here, usually." He gestured at the deck, the chairs, the railing they were both resting their feet on. "Private conversations nobody else was part of."

"What's your point?"

"My point is you have weight with this group. If you say Martin acknowledged the debt, if you tell Paul and Joel that Martin intended to pay it back, that kind of thing — that puts social pressure on the process without anyone having to get lawyers involved. Martin owed me forty thousand dollars, Tommy. That's not a rounding error. That's real money from a real deal he walked away from."

"So talk to the estate."

"I will. But estates take time. Probate, lawyers, all of it. And I'm in a situation where time isn't something I've got a lot of." Cliff's voice didn't change, but something in his jaw did — a small tightening that Tommy recognized as the same look he saw in kids before they admitted they'd been the one who threw the punch. "My business is in a restructuring phase. And the forty thousand is — it's a real number. It's not theoretical money."

Tommy watched the lake. A breeze had come up from the north, putting small waves on the surface that caught the copper light and broke it into pieces. He could smell the water and the pine and, faintly, something cooking inside the house — probably Paul making pasta because Paul always made pasta when he was stressed, as if feeding people was a form of insurance.

"I can talk to Paul," Tommy said. "That's not hard. If Martin owed you the money, he owed you the money."

"He did."

"Then it's not a hard conversation."

Cliff nodded. He drank his beer. He looked at the lake. The silence held for maybe ten seconds, which was about eight seconds longer than Cliff usually let a silence sit.

"There's one other thing," Cliff said.

Tommy waited.

"I was in Martin's study. Sunday morning — I don't even know what day it is anymore. The safe was open. Martin left it unlocked, basically. The dial was one tick off, you could —" Cliff made a twisting gesture with his fingers. "One tick."

"Okay."

"I was looking for documentation. The loan, the forty thousand. Emails, contracts, whatever Martin had. And I found the financial stuff — that's all fine. But I also found something else."

Tommy's hand had stopped denting the beer can. His hand had stopped doing anything. It was resting on his thigh with the can balanced in his grip the way a quarterback holds a ball at the line of scrimmage — loose, or what looks loose, but the fingers know exactly where the laces are.

"What'd you find?"

"An envelope. Manila. Labeled 'RT.'"

The lake kept doing what the lake did. A loon called from somewhere past the dock, that long warbling sound that always made him think of his kids' first time up here, Denise wrapping blankets around them because she'd packed for July and it was September in Minnesota. Somewhere inside the house, the refrigerator cycled on with that loud mechanical groan that Martin had been saying he'd fix for fifteen years.

"Doesn't ring a bell," Tommy said.

"Yeah, I figured it might not." Cliff's voice had shifted into a register Tommy hadn't heard from him before — still smooth, still controlled, but with something harder underneath. Like the foundation of a building Cliff was showing you, where the finish was nice but you could feel the concrete through the carpet if you stood still long enough. "So I opened it. I figured it was real estate documents — RT, maybe a property code, an address. Martin was in real estate, I was in real estate, it made sense."

"And?"

"It wasn't real estate, Tommy."

The silence this time was different. Tommy could hear the blood in his own ears, which was a thing that happened to him on the sideline when a play went wrong — not a fumble, not a bad call, but the kind of wrong where a kid was lying on the field and not getting up and the whole stadium went quiet and Tommy could hear the hydraulics of his own body doing their work, pumping blood past his eardrums like a count that wouldn't stop.

"I don't know what you're talking about," Tommy said.

"It was a letter. Handwritten. Martin's handwriting — I've seen enough of his notes to recognize it. Dated August 2002." Cliff's voice was patient and level, the way it got when he was walking a buyer through a property's defects — not hiding them, just presenting them as facts that could be managed. "He wrote about a cabin fire. Washburn County, Wisconsin. A caretaker named Ray Toomey." Cliff let the name sit in the air between them. "He wrote about who started it. And he wrote about what he did afterward."

Tommy set the beer on the armrest. His hand was steady. His left forearm, where the scar was, had started to itch — a sensation he hadn't felt in years. He'd burned it on the door frame of the cabin in Washburn County twenty-two years ago and the scar tissue had settled into a smooth white ridge along the outside of his forearm that he wore long sleeves over in summer and that Denise had stopped asking about a decade ago.

"You read that," Tommy said. Not a question.

"I read it."

"And you — what. Took pictures."

Cliff looked at him. The smooth salesman face was still there, but the eyes were doing different work now — measuring, assessing, calculating square footage in a property he'd just realized was worth more than the listing price. "I'm a businessman, Tommy. I document things. It's habit."

"Fuck you it's habit."

"That's — look, let me put it this way." The reframe was instant, professional, almost admirable in its efficiency. "It's relevant. That's what it is. It's relevant to a conversation about Martin's estate, about the forty thousand, about how we all move forward from this weekend."

"You're blackmailing me."

"I'm not. I'm not even close to blackmailing you." Cliff held up a hand, palm out, the gesture of a man calming a situation that he believed he was in control of. "Blackmail would be 'give me money or I go to the police.' That's not what this is. What this is, Tommy, is two guys who both have a position here, and I'm saying let's find an arrangement that addresses both positions." He spread his hands, palms up. "You want that document to stay where it is. I want the forty thousand dollars that Martin owed me. These aren't incompatible interests."

Tommy stared at him. Cliff was sitting there with his craft beer and his pressed chinos and his leather loafers — the man wore leather loafers to a lake house, had been wearing them all weekend, and somehow Tommy had spent twenty-five years never letting this bother him — and his hands were smooth and manicured, the hands of a man who moisturized, who'd never done anything with those hands that didn't involve a pen or a handshake or the steering wheel of whatever German car he was leasing that year. He ran three miles a day on a treadmill and called it training. Tommy outweighed him by forty pounds and every one of those pounds had been put there on purpose, over years, in weight rooms and on practice fields.

The coaching part of Tommy's brain — the part that evaluated situations in terms of field position and time on the clock and what your personnel could handle — was doing something he couldn't quite control. It was calculating. Not the money. Not the negotiation. Something else. Something that had to do with the distance between this chair and the dock and the way sound carried over water at night.

The thought arrived and Tommy didn't push it away. He let it sit there the way a play diagram sits on a whiteboard — not a plan, just a shape, just lines and arrows showing where bodies could go if you told them to.

"Okay," Tommy said.

Cliff blinked. "Okay?"

"I'll talk to Paul. The forty thousand — yeah. I'll help move it along."

"Well, that's — I appreciate that. I do. That's a reasonable response to a reasonable situation."

"It's not about being reasonable. Martin owed you the money. He should've paid it."

"He should have. Two years ago, ideally, but—"

"So I'll help. Whatever you need. Pressure, conversations, whatever. I'll make it happen."

Cliff studied him. Tommy could see Cliff running the numbers behind those measured eyes — was Tommy too easy because he was angry, or too easy because he was planning, or too easy because he'd already decided to cooperate and was just letting Cliff talk himself out? Cliff would pick option three. Option three was the one that confirmed the deal, and Cliff always chose the interpretation that confirmed the deal.

"Good," Cliff said. He didn't stand up yet. He was still assessing, still working the close. "And the document—"

"Stays where it is."

"That's — right. Martin's business. Old history. Nobody needs to see it."

"Nobody needs to see it," Tommy agreed.

---

The memory came the way it always came — not as a story with a beginning but as a collection of sensory facts that his body had stored separately from his brain, as if the brain had refused delivery and the body had signed for it anyway.

The cabin. August 2002. He was twenty-five. Martin was twenty-five. They'd gone up to Washburn County for a weekend — a buddy's family cabin, or a buddy of a buddy, the chain of ownership so long that nobody checked because that was how it worked in your twenties, you knew a guy who knew a guy and the cabin was empty and you brought beer and nobody asked questions.

Friday night. The bonfire. Too much lighter fluid because Tommy had always been the guy who made fires too big, who couldn't just start a fire, he had to build one — the same thing that made him good at football made him bad at measured responses, the same physicality that coaches praised on the field was the thing that broke a screen door when he slammed it, that put a fist-sized hole in drywall when he lost an argument, that made every fire a performance of what fire could do if you let it.

The lighter fluid. The bonfire climbing higher than the tree line. Martin saying something — *that's enough, that's enough* — and Tommy squeezing more onto the base because the more Martin said enough the more Tommy wanted to show him that enough was a concept that didn't apply to Tommy Renaldi. Not at twenty-five. Not with eight beers in him and the Wisconsin summer dark around them and the feeling that the world was a thing you could grab if your hands were big enough.

Then the wind shifted. Or the fire found something it liked in the underbrush between the pit and the cabin's porch. Or the lighter fluid can was closer than Tommy thought. He didn't know. Twenty-two years and he still didn't know the mechanical sequence. He only knew the result: the fire jumped. Not toward the lake, not toward the clearing where Martin had set up the chairs. Toward the cabin.

The porch caught first. Dry cedar, old and desiccated, and it went up with a sound like someone crumpling heavy paper next to a microphone — that amplified crackling that wasn't like any fire Tommy had heard before or since, not the contained fires in a barrel or a pit but something alive, something that moved like it had an appetite and was glad to finally be feeding.

Martin yelled. Tommy ran toward the cabin, not away from it, because his first instinct was always to go to the problem — the coaching instinct, the football instinct, the thing that made him useful on a field and dangerous everywhere else. He grabbed the garden hose from the side of the cabin and it was dry, no pressure, the spigot handle spinning loose in his hand. He dropped it and ran around to the front where the propane tank was, thinking he could — he didn't know what he was thinking. He was twenty-five and the world was on fire and his thinking was an animal thing, all movement and no strategy.

That was when he saw the truck.

A green Ford pickup, dull paint, mud on the wheel wells. Parked in the gravel behind Martin's car. It hadn't been there when they arrived. Or it had been there and they hadn't looked, which was worse, which meant they'd been fifty yards from the bonfire they lit next to a cabin where someone was sleeping and they hadn't checked because checking wasn't something twenty-five-year-olds did.

Ray Toomey. The caretaker. Tommy didn't know the name then — didn't learn it until the newspaper report three days later, the one that said WASHBURN CO. CARETAKER DIES IN ELECTRICAL FIRE, the one Martin showed him in a McDonald's parking lot off Highway 53 with his hands flat on the steering wheel and his voice flat and his eyes doing something Tommy had never seen Martin's eyes do, which was nothing. They were doing nothing at all. Martin had turned off whatever part of himself was supposed to react and what was left was a man reading a newspaper headline to another man in a parking lot and saying, "They're calling it electrical. We don't talk about this again."

But that was three days later. In the moment, in the August dark with the cabin burning and the green truck in the driveway, Tommy ran to the front door and the handle was hot and he pulled it open and the heat came out like a physical force, like the thing heat becomes when it stops being temperature and starts being a wall that shoves you backward. Tommy's left forearm hit the door frame and the skin cooked — a half-second of contact, but the metal frame had already absorbed enough heat to do the job — and he jerked back screaming and the door swung shut and inside the cabin something fell. A beam or a section of ceiling. Something structural. The sound it made was the sound of a room closing itself off from the world, and under that sound, nothing. No voice. No movement. Just fire working.

He didn't go back in. He stood in the driveway with his forearm already blistering, the skin going waxy white where it should have been red, and Martin came around the side of the cabin and grabbed him by both arms and said, "We have to go. Now. Tommy. Now."

"There's someone in there."

"I know. We have to go."

"Martin—"

"There's nothing you can do. There's nothing — look at me. Tommy. Look at me." Martin's hands were on Tommy's shoulders, his grip tighter than Tommy had ever felt from a man who didn't do physical things, who hadn't played a sport since intramural soccer sophomore year. "There is nothing you can do for whoever's in there. We need to go."

And they went. Martin drove. Tommy sat in the passenger seat with his arm throbbing and his mind doing something it would never fully come back from, which was accepting that he had killed a man. Not on purpose. Not with malice or awareness. He hadn't known anyone was in the cabin, hadn't thought to check, had been too drunk and too young and too much himself to do the thing a careful person would have done, which was look at the goddamn driveway before pouring lighter fluid on a bonfire. But the result was the same. A man was dead in a burned cabin and Tommy's arm was burned and Martin was driving thirty miles an hour under the speed limit because Martin was always thinking about what came next while Tommy was still stuck in what just happened.

Martin took him to a clinic in Spooner the next morning. Grease burn, Tommy told the nurse. Deep fryer accident at a friend's place. She believed him or didn't care. The scar healed the way burns heal — slowly, badly, with a texture that announced itself to anyone who touched it. Denise asked about it on their third date. Tommy said, "Cooking accident. I was twenty-five and an idiot." Both parts were true even though neither part was the truth.

Twenty-two years. Every September, Tommy came back to the draft, and every September Martin would find a moment — on the deck, by the fire pit, during a walk to the dock — to check the seal. Not with words, usually. A hand on Tommy's shoulder. A look that lasted a beat longer than friendship required. Occasionally something spoken, always brief: "You good?" or "We're okay" or just Tommy's name in a tone that contained the whole history without breaking it open. And Tommy would nod or grunt or say "yeah" and that would be it for another year. The September ritual inside the September ritual. Martin was the valve that kept the pressure from blowing the pipe. Martin had written it all down and put it in a sealed envelope and put the envelope in a safe and left the safe one tick from locked, and Tommy had never asked why Martin wrote it down because asking Martin why he did things was like asking weather why it rained — the answer was beyond Tommy's operating level and always had been.

Now Martin was dead. The valve was gone. And Cliff Haywood, who sold houses for a living and brought craft beer to a lake house, was sitting on this deck with his moisturized hands and his leather loafers treating twenty-two years of managed disaster like a real estate opportunity with an attractive cap rate.

---

The screen door banged. Cliff came back with two more beers, even though Tommy hadn't finished the one he had. He set one on Tommy's armrest like a man topping off a client's water glass.

"So," Cliff said, settling back into his chair with the ease of a man who believed the hard part was over. "Logistics."

"Logistics."

"Paul's the one to start with — he's the mediator type, right? Insurance background, knows how estates work, at least roughly. If you tell him that Martin acknowledged the debt in conversation, that you heard him say he intended to pay it back — that creates social momentum. Gets the process moving without anyone pulling in attorneys."

"Mm-hmm."

"And there's probably some flexibility around assets. I'm not saying anything drastic. I'm saying there are mechanisms for settling debts before probate closes. Liens, purchase agreements on specific assets, that kind of thing. It can be clean."

"Mechanisms," Tommy repeated.

"Structures." Cliff glanced at him. "You're quiet."

"I'm listening."

"That's — okay. Good." But something in Cliff's face said it wasn't good, said that Tommy being quiet wasn't what he'd expected. Tommy could see Cliff running the numbers — was Tommy too quiet because he was angry, or too quiet because he was planning, or too quiet because he'd already agreed and was just letting Cliff finish? Cliff would pick the third option. The third option confirmed the deal, and Cliff always, always chose the interpretation that confirmed the deal.

"The thing is, Tommy, this doesn't have to be adversarial. I know how it sounded out there. Guy comes to you with information, asks for a favor — it sounds like a movie. But it's not that. Martin owed me the money. You know that's real. I've got emails from 2022 where he says, quote, 'I'll make it right by the end of the year.' End quote. He didn't. He died instead, which — I'm not blaming him for dying. But the debt didn't die with him."

"I get it."

"And the other thing — the document." Cliff's voice went careful here, calibrated, the tone of a man naming a defect while emphasizing the property's upside. "I've got no interest in that. It's old history. Your history and Martin's. I didn't know this Toomey—"

"Don't say his name."

The sentence came out flat and final. Not loud. Tommy hadn't raised his voice. But the temperature on the deck shifted by several degrees, and Cliff's mouth closed and stayed closed for three full seconds, which was the longest Tommy had ever seen Cliff Haywood go without following up on a silence.

"Fair enough," Cliff said quietly. "My point is, I've got no stake in whatever happened back then. What I've got is a financial position that needs resolution, and you're the person who can help resolve it. That's the full scope of what I'm asking."

Tommy looked at the beer on his armrest. Cliff had set it there the way he set things down in other people's houses — with the casual territorial confidence of a man who believed every surface was available to him if he was polite about using it.

"The photos are on your phone," Tommy said.

"Phone and cloud."

"So even if the letter disappeared—"

"It wouldn't change anything. That's correct." Cliff said this without triumph. He said it the way he'd tell a buyer that yes, the property had a lien, and yes, it would need to be cleared, but here's how they could work around it. "But I'm not holding it over you, Tommy. I want to be clear about that. It's just how I manage sensitive information. It's good practice."

Good practice. The phrase landed on Tommy like something physical — the weight of a man who thought documenting a twenty-two-year-old death was the same thing as saving a contract to the cloud. Cliff had filed Ray Toomey alongside his invoices and his property listings and his tax returns, and he didn't see anything wrong with that because Cliff didn't see categories — he saw positions. Leverage was leverage the same way a dollar was a dollar, regardless of what the dollar was made of.

"Okay," Tommy said.

"Okay."

"I'll talk to Paul this week. The forty thousand — you'll get it. I'll make sure."

"I appreciate that. I really do."

"And we don't talk about this again. You and me. This conversation didn't happen."

"Clean break," Cliff said. "Professional resolution. I couldn't agree more." He stood up, stretched, cracked his neck with a sound that made Tommy's jaw tighten. Then the smile — not the full-wattage Cliff smile, the one he used on clients and women and anyone whose agreement he needed. A smaller one. More private. The smile of a man who believed he'd just closed the best deal of a very bad year. "I think this is the right move. For both of us."

"Sure."

"I'm going to see what Paul's doing about dinner. You coming in?"

"In a minute."

"Don't sit out here too long. Gets cold when the sun drops."

"I know how September works, Cliff."

"Right. Yeah. Sure." Cliff went inside. The screen door banged shut. The sound of his loafers on the kitchen floor — two distinct steps, the leather soles on the old hardwood — and then the house swallowed him.

Tommy sat in the Adirondack chair. It was the same one he'd sat in every September for twenty-five years, the one closest to the rail, the one with the armrests worn smooth by two decades of men resting beers on them. Martin had bought these chairs the year he bought the house, which was the year after the fire, and Tommy had never let himself think about whether there was a connection between those two purchases because thinking about connections was thinking about the architecture of the thing between them, and the architecture was Martin's department. Martin built the structure. Tommy lived in it. That was the arrangement.

The scar on his forearm had stopped itching. His hands were flat on the armrests, palms down, fingers spread. He looked at them. Not the way he usually looked at his hands — with the casual ownership of a man who used them for everything, who coached with them and cooked with them and fixed things around the house with them. He looked at them the way he sometimes looked at his players during film review. Not evaluating their skill. Evaluating their capacity. What they could do if the play called for it.

Cliff's phone was in Cliff's pocket. The cloud backup was wherever cloud backups lived — some server, some building, some place Tommy couldn't reach. But Cliff wasn't a server. Cliff was a hundred and seventy-five pounds in pressed chinos who ran three miles a day on a treadmill and thought that was exercise.

Tommy thought about the dock. He'd walked it a hundred times. The wooden planks, the slight give under his weight, the swim platform at the end where the kids used to jump off before they got old enough to think jumping was uncool. The water beside the dock was three feet deep. Maybe less where the silt built up near the pilings. Enough to swim in if you were a kid, enough to wade in if you were an adult.

Not enough to drown in if you were conscious.

The thought sat on the whiteboard of his mind. Not a play yet. Just a shape. Lines and arrows showing where a body could go if someone told it to. He didn't push it away. He didn't draw it up. He let it sit there the way you let a bad idea sit on the board during a late practice, the kind of idea you're supposed to erase before the players see it, except Tommy didn't erase it because it wasn't a bad idea. It was a simple idea. It was the kind of idea Tommy was built for — physical, direct, one step, done.

The loon called again from somewhere out past the dock, and the lake had gone from copper to the flat gray it turned when the sun dropped behind the far tree line — the color change so gradual you couldn't point to the moment it happened, but the result was definitive. The golden afternoon was gone. September nights in Minnesota started warm and ended cold. You could go from shirtsleeves to shivering in an hour. The deck planks got slick when the temperature dropped and the dew settled. A man in leather loafers on wet wood in the dark could lose his footing. Might not even make a sound that carried eighty yards through pine and birch to the house, especially with the loons calling and the refrigerator running and nobody listening for anything because why would anyone be listening.

Tommy picked up the beer Cliff had brought him. The can was still full. He held it for a moment, then tipped it over the rail and watched the liquid catch the last gray light for a second before it hit the ground and disappeared into the pine needles below the deck.

He set the empty can on the armrest where Cliff had put it. He wiped his hands on his shorts.

Then he went inside.


# Chapter 13

The draft board was still on the wall.

Nate had been looking at it all day, the way you look at something you should probably take down but haven't because taking it down would make it real. Martin's picks were circled in red Sharpie — Martin's red Sharpie, Martin's steady block capitals, each player name written with the same deliberate pressure Martin used for everything from grocery lists to lease agreements. The whiteboard was the erasable kind, and someone would eventually wipe it clean, and twenty-five years of data would disappear in one pass of a damp rag.

But not yet. Not today. Today the board stayed up and Martin's picks stayed circled and the great room still smelled faintly of the Lagavulin that Martin had been drinking Thursday night, and if Nate squinted at the room from the right angle it almost looked like any other year.

It was Joel who brought it up, because Joel was constitutionally incapable of letting a procedural question sit unresolved. They were in the great room — six men who should have been five and used to be seven — and Joel said, "So are we going to talk about the roster?"

Nobody said anything for a second.

"Martin's roster," Joel clarified, as if they might have thought he was talking about someone else's. "The season starts Thursday. His team's sitting there. Someone needs to decide what happens."

Tommy, from the far end of the couch, said, "His team plays."

"OK, but—"

"His team plays. That's his roster. Those are his guys. You don't mess with a man's roster."

"I'm not saying mess with it. I'm saying someone has to set lineups. Bye weeks, injuries, the waiver wire. You can't just let the app—"

"Auto-manage works fine."

"Auto-manage is dogshit," Nate said, because it was, and because saying things about fantasy football was the only behavior that still felt normal. "Auto-manage started Dalton over Herbert in week four last year. I did a whole segment on it. Auto-manage will bench your stud receiver on a Thursday game because the algorithm doesn't understand revenge narratives. Auto-manage is for people who don't care, and Martin cared more than anybody in this room."

That last part came out heavier than Nate had intended, and the room absorbed it.

"So someone manages it," Paul said. He was sitting in Martin's chair — Martin's leather recliner by the fireplace, the one Martin had claimed in 2006 and defended with the quiet immovability of a man who owned the house. Paul either hadn't noticed or had noticed and was doing it on purpose. "Someone takes over. Makes the waiver moves, sets the lineups. As a — I don't know."

"Tribute," Joel offered.

"That's a little Hunger Games."

"It's what you mean, though."

"It's what I mean, yeah. Someone runs his team the way he would've run it. Keeps it competitive. Honors the — I don't know. The work he put in."

"And who does that?" Joel leaned back. "Who among us thinks like Martin thought about fantasy football?"

"I do," Paul said.

Tommy made a sound from the couch. Not quite a laugh.

"I'm serious. I was his best friend for twenty-five years. I know how he thought about matchups, I know his waiver tendencies, I know—"

"Paul." Tommy sat forward. "You don't know shit about football."

"I know plenty about football."

"You took a tight end in the second round last year. The wrong tight end."

"Kelce went second round in every serious league that—"

"You took Kittle."

"Kittle had a great week one."

"Kittle had a great week one and then he had Kittle's season, which is what Kittle always has, which is why you don't take him in the second round, which is what Martin would've told you if you'd asked him, which you didn't because you thought you knew better."

Nate watched this play out with the part of his brain that was always watching, always noting, always filing things away in the folder marked *potential content*. Tommy's voice had the old rhythm — the coaching cadence, the halftime certainty, the way he built an argument by repetition until the other person either capitulated or got tired. It was the most normal Tommy had sounded in two days.

"OK," Nate said, "so Paul wants to manage it, Tommy thinks auto-manage, Joel's asking the procedural question nobody wants to answer. What about the rest of us? Cliff? Doug?"

Doug, who was sitting on the floor by the fireplace with a glass of water that Nate kept glancing at because it should have been a beer, shrugged one shoulder. "I think Paul should do it."

"Why Paul?"

"He'd actually do it. He'd pay attention." Doug took a sip of water and set the glass down with the careful precision of a man whose hands weren't entirely steady. "The rest of us would forget by week six."

"I wouldn't forget," Nate said.

"You'd turn it into a podcast arc."

Nate opened his mouth to argue and then closed it because Doug was right and the fact that Doug could see through him in four words while sitting on the floor nursing water was both impressive and slightly embarrassing. Doug saw things. That was Doug's whole deal — he was so quiet that people forgot to perform around him, and then he said something that made you realize he'd been watching the whole time.

"Fair," Nate said. "Cliff?"

Cliff was in the armchair by the window, legs crossed, holding a beer he hadn't drunk much of. He looked comfortable in the way Cliff always looked comfortable — settled, composed, the posture of a man who'd learned to take up space like he deserved it. "I think Tommy's right," he said. "Let the team play as-is. Auto-manage. Don't overthink it."

That was the second time in ten minutes that Cliff had agreed with Tommy without arguing about it first.

Nate clocked this. He clocked it the way a play-by-play guy clocks a coverage change — not with alarm, not with certainty, just a mental note that something in the defensive alignment had shifted and he should keep watching. Tommy and Cliff had fought about everything for twenty-five years. They'd fought about kicker scoring rules for forty-five minutes in 2019. They'd nearly thrown hands over a trade veto in 2016 — Nate still referenced it in his top-ten all-time episodes. Cliff and Tommy's default setting was opposition. It was how they related. It was their version of friendship, the friction that proved the connection.

And now Cliff was agreeing with Tommy quickly, smoothly, the way he agreed with a client. And Tommy was accepting it without the pushback that should have followed, the "damn right I'm right" or "since when do you agree with me, Haywood" that had been part of their rhythm since college. Tommy just nodded. Not at Cliff — at the space between them.

They were sitting on opposite ends of the room. They'd been on opposite ends of every room since yesterday.

"OK, new question," Nate said, because redirect and narrate and fill space was what he did, it was the skill that paid his rent and the skill that would probably get him killed someday, but at least he'd die talking. "What about trades? Can you trade with Martin's team?"

Joel's eyebrows went up. "You want to trade with a dead man."

"I don't want to. I'm asking if it's possible. If his team is auto-managed and someone offers a trade, and the algorithm accepts—"

"The algorithm doesn't accept trades. It rejects everything."

"Some platforms auto-accept if the trade improves the team's projected score."

"So you're asking whether a bot can consent to a roster transaction on behalf of a deceased owner." Joel looked at Nate with the expression he used in depositions, the one that said *I want you to hear what you just said.* "Is that the question?"

"When you put it like that—"

"That's how I'm putting it."

"It sounds weird when you put it like that."

"It is weird, Nate. The whole thing is weird. We're sitting in a dead man's house deciding what happens to his fantasy football team while a detective who may or may not be an actual detective is probably somewhere writing up a report about how five of us fucked with the crime scene. The whole thing is weird."

The room got quiet. Joel had that ability — the lawyer's talent for reducing a situation to its elements, stripping the cushion off the thing until you were sitting on the frame. Nate respected it and hated it in equal measure, because Joel's precision had a way of collapsing the bit, and the bit was the only thing between Nate and the actual shape of what was happening.

"No trades," Tommy said, quietly. "Nobody trades with his team. That's—no. You don't do that."

"Agreed," Paul said.

"Agreed," Cliff said, half a beat too fast.

The silence after that had a texture to it. Nate could feel the gears in the room — the things people wanted to say and the things people were choosing not to say and the gap between the two, which was wider than Nate had ever felt it in twenty-five years of weekends in this house. The refrigerator in the kitchen cycled on, its compressor kicking with the familiar clunk that had been the baseline hum of every late night they'd ever spent here, Martin standing at the counter at 2 AM making a sandwich, the fridge humming, the lake outside in the dark.

"We need a new commissioner," Nate said.

"You," Tommy said. "You've got the podcast. You're already tracking everything."

"I don't want to be commissioner."

"Nobody wants to be commissioner."

"Martin wanted to be commissioner," Paul said.

"Martin tolerated being commissioner because nobody else would do it right."

"For Martin, that's the same thing."

That landed and Nate let it sit, because Paul was right and because the truth of it — that Martin's love for them had always been expressed as control, as management, as the willingness to maintain the scoring system and enforce the trade deadlines and make sure everyone showed up every September even in the years when showing up was hard — was as close to a eulogy as any of them were going to manage on a Monday afternoon in the great room.

"Fine," Nate said. "I'll be interim commissioner. Of a league with no bylaws, no constitutional authority, a dead owner, and a scoring system from 2004 that still counts punt return yards because Martin thought punt return yards mattered and none of us ever had the balls to tell him they didn't."

"Don't touch the punt return yards," Tommy said, and his coaching finger came up, pointed at Nate across the room. "Martin put those in. You don't touch them."

"I'm not touching anything."

"You touch the scoring settings, I'm done."

"You're not done. Nobody's done. That's the whole—" Nate stopped. The bit was right there — *nobody's done, because the last person who was in is dead, and quitting would mean admitting the thing is over, and nobody in this room is ready to admit the thing is over* — and he could feel the shape of it, the rhythm, the way it would sound on the podcast, the thirty-second clip that would get shared. And then the bit collapsed, because he couldn't find the angle that made it funny without making it cruel, and the space where the bit should have been was just six guys in a dead man's living room who didn't know how to say they missed him without a scoring format.

"Nobody's done," Nate said, quieter. "We're playing the season. Martin's team plays. Auto-manage. Paul checks in on it. I'm commissioner. Punt return yards stay. Anything else?"

"Waiver priority," Joel said. "Martin had third priority. It redistributes — that's the only equitable ruling."

"Redistributes," Nate said. "Sure. Yeah. Done."

He looked at the draft board. Martin's picks in red. The names of players who would play the season for a man who wouldn't watch them.

Tommy got up and went to the kitchen. Nate heard him open the fridge, heard a bottle cap hit the counter, heard Tommy stand there for a second too long before coming back. When he came back he had two beers. He handed one to Cliff.

"Thanks," Cliff said.

"Yeah," Tommy said.

They didn't look at each other. The exchange took less than three seconds and told Nate nothing he could articulate and everything he could feel: something had happened between these two men, something that had replaced the friction with courtesy, and courtesy between Tommy Renaldi and Cliff Haywood was as wrong as silence in a room full of people who'd been talking over each other for a quarter century.

Nate filed it. He didn't know what to do with it yet, but he filed it.

---

They grilled steaks because that was what you did on the last night. Tommy manned the grill because Tommy always manned the grill — feet planted, tongs in his right hand, beer in his left, the posture of a man whose confidence began in his body and extended outward from there. He worked the propane burners with the easy precision of someone who'd spent his life managing heat and timing: the grill, the practice field, the weight room, the thing inside himself that Nate had always read as intensity and was starting to wonder if he'd been reading wrong.

The steaks were Martin's. Two-inch ribeyes from the chest freezer in the basement, purchased and portioned before the weekend, each one labeled in Martin's handwriting with a date and a cut. They were eating a dead man's groceries. Nobody mentioned it. The not-mentioning-it sat on the deck like a seventh person at a table set for eight.

The deck table had eight chairs. They took their usual seats, which left Martin's empty at the head and turned the arrangement into a thing with a gap in it. Nate sat facing the lake, next to Paul. Doug was across from him. Joel sat next to Doug. Tommy took the end nearest the grill. Cliff sat at the opposite end.

Three empty chairs between Tommy and Cliff. Nate counted them.

Three chairs between two men who, forty-eight hours ago, would have been shoulder to shoulder at the grill end, arguing about whether Tommy was ruining the sear by opening the lid every thirty seconds, the annual steak fight that had been as much a part of these weekends as the draft itself. Cliff would have said *you're letting the heat out, Coach*, and Tommy would have said *I've been grilling longer than you've been pretending to know what medium rare looks like*, and someone — Martin, usually — would have told them both to shut up and drink their beer.

Now Cliff sat three chairs away and cut his meat in small, careful movements, and said nothing about the sear.

The sunset was doing the thing it did in September on Elbow Lake — the light going from gold to amber to something Nate didn't have a word for, the color of the end of a weekend, the color of wanting to stay. The birches along the shoreline were turning at the tops, a yellow that hadn't decided whether it was autumn yet, and the lake was flat enough to hold the sky in it.

"Beautiful," Cliff said. To nobody. To the lake.

"Yeah," Doug said.

Cliff's fork paused. "Martin always said September was the only month that mattered."

"He said that about every month," Paul said.

"He said it most about September."

The conversation died. They ate. Tommy's steaks were good — medium rare, heavy pepper, the coarse salt from the grinder Martin had bought from some kitchen store in 2018 that everyone had mocked and then used every year since because it was Martin's grinder on Martin's deck for Martin's steaks.

Nate ate and watched.

Joel was doing the thing. The Joel thing. He'd look at Paul, hold the look for half a beat, then look away. Then again. Look, hold, away. It had the rhythm of a signal — not casual glancing but the deliberate eye contact of two people who had information to exchange and couldn't do it here. Nate had watched Joel do this in the courtroom once, the year he'd gone to see Joel argue a motion, the same silent choreography with co-counsel before a sidebar. Joel and Paul were building something. Nate didn't know what, but the body language was a conversation the rest of the table was excluded from.

Doug pushed his steak around his plate. He'd taken three bites that Nate had counted. His pint glass was water, still water, and he held it the same way everyone else held their beers but the camouflage wasn't working because the glass wasn't sweating the right way and there was no label and water catches light differently than pale ale. Doug had lost weight since last year — not good weight, not I-joined-a-gym weight, the kind that came off when food stopped being interesting and getting out of bed became the hardest thing you did each day. His hoodie was a size too big now. His face was thinner and the circles under his eyes had the permanence of something that wasn't going away with a good night's sleep.

He was forty-seven and he looked fifty-five.

"You doing OK?" Nate asked, quiet enough that it was just the two of them.

Doug looked at his plate. "Yeah. Fine."

"Yeah?"

"I'm good, Nate." He picked up the water and took a sip and set it back down. "Just tired."

"Sure."

"It's been a long weekend."

"It has."

Doug nodded and went back to pushing his steak and Nate let it go because Doug's voice had the quality of a lid being held down on something that was pressing upward, and Nate knew enough about men who were holding things down to know that pressing didn't help. You let them hold the lid. You just made sure they knew you could see the lid.

"Hey," Joel said, louder, to the table. "Anybody remember when Martin fell off the dock?"

"Oh, shit," Nate said, and the memory arrived fully formed. "2012."

"Was it twelve?"

"It was twelve because we were arguing about RG3," Paul said. "Whether you could trust him as a keeper with the knee."

"He just walked off the end," Joel said. "Not tripped, not slipped. Just walked. Like the dock was three feet longer than it actually was."

"He was making a point," Nate said. "Fumble return touchdowns. He wanted to add fumble return touchdowns to the scoring and he was up out of his chair waving his arms around and he walked backward right off the end of the dock."

"Was he drunk?"

"He'd had four scotches, but that wasn't — Martin with four scotches was baseline Martin. He wasn't drunk, he was making a point. He was making a point so hard that the dock ran out."

"And he went in," Paul said. He was smiling — the real smile, the one that came before grief interrupted it. "He went in and he came up coughing and the first thing out of his mouth—"

"Oh God—"

"The first thing out of his mouth, he's standing in three feet of water with his shirt soaked and his drink gone and his phone ruined, and he raises his hand"— Paul raised his own hand, the gesture involuntary, mimicking a dead friend from twelve years ago — "and he says—"

"'Motion still carries,'" Nate and Paul said at the same time.

"'I'm making a formal motion and it still carries,'" Joel added. "He said it like a goddamn parliamentarian. He's standing in the lake and he's citing Robert's Rules."

"And Doug went and got him a towel," Paul said.

"I got him a towel," Doug said, and for a second he was back, he was present, his face had the animation of a man remembering a thing he'd been part of. "He wouldn't take it. He said he wasn't leaving the water until someone seconded the motion."

"Did someone second it?"

"Tommy seconded it," Nate said. "Just to get him out of the water."

"I seconded it because he was right," Tommy said from the railing. He'd turned around to listen, his beer resting on the deck rail, and his face had something on it that wasn't quite a smile and wasn't quite grief but was the expression of a man looking at a place he couldn't go back to. "Fumble return touchdowns are a legitimate scoring category and Martin was right and the only reason we didn't add them is because Cliff voted it down."

"I voted it down because it would've blown up the historical comparisons," Cliff said, and for a moment — for one moment — the table sounded like itself. Like twenty-five years of arguments about things that didn't matter and meant everything. Like Thursday nights and Saturday mornings and the sound of six voices and a seventh that would never be part of it again.

They laughed. All six of them, together, and the sound traveled out over the flat water the way sounds do on Elbow Lake — further than it should, longer than it should, reaching distances where it had no business arriving.

Then the laughter trailed off. The silence after it was different from the silence before. Warmer and sadder.

Paul was looking at his plate. Joel was looking at Paul. Tommy was looking at the lake. Cliff was looking at Tommy. Doug was looking at his water. And Nate was looking at all of them, because that was his role and his curse and the thing that kept him in this group and the thing that kept him at a distance from it — the watcher, the narrator, the man who experienced his own life at a slight remove because somewhere in his brain a producer was always saying *good stuff, can we get that again from a different angle.*

He couldn't get this again. He knew that without knowing why he knew it. This particular configuration — six men on a deck, September light, the smell of grilled steak and pine and lake water, the sound of a shared laugh still ringing in the space above the table — was not going to happen again. The next time they sat on this deck, if they ever sat on this deck, something would have changed. Something was already changing. He could feel it the way you feel a weather system before it arrives, the barometric drop, the wrongness in the air that your body reads before your brain catches up.

Tommy turned back to the lake. The sunset had burned down to a thin line of orange along the tree line and the sky above it was going purple. The loons started — that sound, that specific sound that existed halfway between music and mourning, the sound of a lake in September when the summer people were gone and the water was getting cold.

"Gonna clean up," Paul said, and started stacking plates.

Joel helped. Doug brought the glasses inside. Cliff gathered the bottles — six empties and one glass of water — and carried them to the recycling bin by the kitchen door. Tommy stayed on the deck, resting his forearms on the railing, looking out at the dock at the far end of the stone path. The dock was a dark shape against darker water, the swim platform at the end just visible, the small bench where Doug had been sitting earlier in the weekend, alone, looking at the lake like a man who needed the lake to look back.

They filtered inside one by one. Doug first, with a quiet "night" that barely registered. Joel, with a nod to Paul that contained more information than any nod should be able to carry. Paul, who stopped at the sliding door and looked back at Nate on the deck and then at Tommy on the railing and seemed to decide something and went inside without saying it. Cliff said "Good night" to the deck generally — "Night, everyone" — and went to his room.

That left Nate and Tommy.

"Hey, Coach," Nate said.

Tommy didn't turn around. "Yeah."

"We're going to be all right. You know that, right?"

Tommy's left hand tightened on the railing. Nate could see the tendons in his forearm, the scar that ran along the outside of it — pale, old, the kind of scar you stopped asking about because the answer was always the same deflection. *Got it in high school. Doesn't matter.* Tommy's hands were the biggest hands in the group, built for gripping things, and right now they were gripping the railing like the railing was the only solid thing left.

"Yeah," Tommy said. "We're fine."

"Good."

"Night, Griggs."

"Night."

Tommy went inside. Nate heard his footsteps on the stairs, heard him skip the third step — everyone in this house skipped the third step, it had been creaking since 2003 and Martin had never fixed it because Martin said the creak was a feature, not a bug, it told you who was moving around at night — heard the upstairs hallway and then a bedroom door.

Nate sat on the deck alone.

The lake was black. The loons had stopped. Across the water, a single light — dock light or porch light, somebody else's house, somebody else's last night of something. The kitchen behind him threw his shadow long across the deck boards, out toward the stone path, out toward the dark pines and the dock beyond them.

He thought about the podcast. He thought about what he'd say, if he ever said anything, if this was his to say. The opening came to him the way openings always did — fully formed, the rhythm already there: *So here's the thing about a keeper league. It's not about the players you keep. It's about the people who keep showing up.*

He said it out loud. Quietly, testing. It was good. It was the kind of line that would land in the first thirty seconds and get clipped and shared and liked by people who'd never met Martin Kessler and never would.

He hated it. He hated that it was good and he hated that he'd thought of it and he hated that the part of his brain that made content never shut off, not for death, not for grief, not for the specific unbearable fact of an extra steak wrapped in foil in the fridge of a man who wouldn't eat it.

"Shit, Martin," he said, to the dark lake.

He went inside. He locked the sliding door. He went down to the basement where his pull-out sofa waited with his duffel bag and his podcast mic and his phone with Mia's last text — *Miss you. Come home safe* — still glowing on the screen. He brushed his teeth with the bathroom door open because the basement bathroom was small and the ventilation was bad and Martin had always said he'd fix the fan and never had. He set his alarm. He lay in the dark and listened to the old refrigerator kick on, its compressor clunking into its forty-minute rhythm, and the house above him settling with the sounds old houses make — pipes, wood, wind, or the weight of six men trying to sleep in a house that still belonged to the seventh.

He thought about Tommy's hands on the railing and Cliff three chairs away and Doug's water and Joel looking at Paul looking at Joel, and he thought: *something is wrong and I don't know what it is and I'm the guy who's supposed to know what it is.*

He filed the thought. He'd come back to it. He'd come back to all of it — the punt return yards and the draft board and Martin's steak and the loons and the lake and the sound of six men laughing at the same time for the last time.

He didn't know it was the last time. But his body knew. His body had been keeping score all weekend, and the score was bad, and the clock was running, and nobody had called the timeout.

He slept. Eventually, they all did. It was the last night all of them would sleep in this house, but nobody knew that yet, and the house held them the way it always had — old wood, lake damp, the pine smell and the dark water and the dock reaching out into the lake like a question that hadn't been answered.


# Chapter 14

Doug folded the same flannel shirt for the third time. The sleeves wouldn't square right, the cuffs refusing to line up, and he gave up and stuffed it into his duffel. His other shirts were already in there, along with the prescription bottles he'd moved from the upstairs bathroom Saturday morning — the Lexapro and the Ativan, buried between a pair of jeans and his shaving kit, where they'd been since this whole thing started. He'd taken his evening dose an hour ago in the basement bathroom with the door locked, the way he'd taken every dose this weekend: quickly, quietly, the bottle opened and closed in a single motion so that the rattle of pills didn't carry through the thin walls. Twenty-five years of friendship and he still couldn't swallow an antidepressant without making sure the coast was clear.

The great room had the feel of a waiting room where the appointment had been cancelled. Paul sat at the kitchen table with his reading glasses and his phone, texting Anne about departure times, his thumbs moving with the deliberate patience of a man who answered every text in complete sentences. Joel had his laptop on the couch, answering the work emails he'd been ignoring since Thursday, the blue light catching the sharp angles of his face. The draft board hung on the wall above the fireplace. Martin's picks were still circled in red marker, and nobody had suggested taking it down. Nobody had suggested much of anything today. The day had passed in the half-speed of an airport delay — too awake to sleep, too tired to do anything useful, suspended in the lag between catastrophe and departure.

Mallick hadn't been back since Sunday evening. No follow-up calls, no uniformed deputies in the driveway, no casserole. The implication had settled over the house like weather: the investigation into Martin's death was winding down. Natural causes, suspicious tampering, no murder charge. Doug didn't know the legal specifics — he didn't have Joel's vocabulary for it — but he understood the shape of it. Martin died of a bad heart, five of his closest friends panicked in the dark, and the panicking had made a natural death look sinister, and now they were all going to go home and spend the rest of their lives knowing they'd made themselves look guilty of something that never happened. It was, if you tilted your head at it, almost funny. Doug didn't tilt his head.

He zipped the duffel. The sound was loud in the room and Paul glanced up.

"You're set?"

"Yeah. Early flight." This was a lie. Doug's flight wasn't until two-thirty tomorrow afternoon, but the lie gave him a reason to end the conversation, and ending conversations was what Doug did best. Twenty-five years in this group and his primary contribution had been his willingness to go get more ice.

Nate came in from the deck, sliding the glass door shut behind him. He'd been on the phone — Mia, probably, or his producer, or whichever version of his future he was negotiating with at any given moment. He went to the kitchen and poured himself a glass of water, then stood at the counter drinking it with the distracted air of a man whose body was in one room and whose brain was somewhere else entirely.

"Mia says hi," Nate said, to no one in particular.

"Hi back," Paul said, without looking up.

"She wants to know if we're all okay." Nate said it flatly, the performance drained out of it, which meant he'd been asked the question genuinely by someone who loved him and didn't know what to do with a genuine question. He drank the rest of the water and put the glass in the sink. "I told her we're fine."

"Are we fine?" Joel asked, from the couch, with enough edge that it could have been a real question or a lawyer's question, and Nate didn't answer either version.

"Where's Tommy?" Paul asked, and Doug heard the question carry more weight than Paul meant it to. Paul was asking about logistics — did Tommy need a ride to the airport? — but the name landed differently now. Everything landed differently now.

"Deck, I think," Joel said. "Or the firepit. I saw him walk that way twenty minutes ago."

"And Cliff?"

"Took his phone into the study." Joel closed his laptop and set it on the cushion beside him. "Calling someone. His office. His lawyer. Does Cliff have a lawyer? Everyone has a lawyer now, I guess."

That was a Joel observation — dry, self-aware, the sarcasm pointed at himself as much as anyone because Joel was a lawyer and the week had made lawyers of them all. Nobody picked it up. The room let the observation die of natural causes and moved on.

Doug sat on the arm of the stairs that led down to the basement and watched the room. He'd been watching rooms his whole life. Forty-seven years of reading rooms from the edges — the doctors' offices during his pharmaceutical sales calls, where you learned in the first three seconds whether the receptionist would let you wait or send you home. The living room of the apartment in Roseville where Steph had told him she wanted a divorce and he'd stared at the coffee table and cataloged every ring and scratch on its surface because cataloging surfaces was easier than hearing the words. The shared bathroom upstairs in this house, where he'd taken his prescription bottles out at four in the morning because the shame of other men seeing his medication was worse than any shame the investigation could produce.

The great room was lying to him. Everything in it said the weekend was over — the packed bags by the front door, Paul's careful logistics, Joel's email, Nate's glass of water where a beer would have been on Thursday. The draft board said it was over. Martin's empty chair at the kitchen table, still pulled back slightly from the table as if he'd just gotten up for a refill, said it was over. But Doug could feel something in the room that hadn't resolved. Not in any one person — it was in the distances between people. Tommy alone outside. Cliff in the study with his phone. The way Paul and Joel were careful with each other in a way that was new since Sunday, a carefulness that Doug couldn't identify as alliance or suspicion or something in between. The group's geometry had shifted, and Doug couldn't see the shape of it from where he sat, which was where he always sat, which was at the edge.

Cliff came back from the study with his phone in his hand and poured himself two fingers of Martin's Lagavulin. The bottle had been on the counter since Thursday night. Nobody had put it away. The scotch smell mixed with the general smell of the house — pine paneling, lake damp, the ghost of Martin's presence that lived in the walls the way old smoke does. Cliff drank standing up, the way he did everything — leaning forward slightly, one hand in his pocket, the posture of a man who was always about to leave for somewhere more important. He was wearing his pressed chinos and the navy polo and the leather loafers that Cliff wore indoors because he was the one person in the group who gave a shit about shoes.

"Paul." Cliff raised the glass an inch, a small toast or a small acknowledgment. "Joel. Doug."

"Hey," Doug said.

"We should talk about the house at some point," Cliff said. "Not tonight. But someone's got to deal with Martin's things. The estate, the property taxes, the—" He waved the glass in a small circle, the gesture doing the work the words wouldn't. "There's a process. I've gone through it with clients. I'm happy to help coordinate, if that's useful."

"Let's get through the drive home first," Paul said. The qualifier he'd swallowed — the _in my experience_ he'd left out, the _to be fair_ he hadn't added — made the sentence sound like a door being held politely shut.

Cliff nodded. He drank. He looked at the draft board and said, "He'd have won the league this year. Those picks were solid." Then he took his scotch and went out to the deck, and Doug watched him go — the chinos, the polo, the loafers making a soft sound on the hardwood as he crossed the room. The sliding door opened and closed. Through the glass, Doug could see him settle against the railing with his drink and look out at the lake where the last light of the day had gone and the moon was putting a silver path across the water.

Joel looked at Paul. Paul looked at his phone. Neither of them said anything about what Cliff had said or the way he'd said it, which told Doug that whatever was wrong in the room, they'd already discussed it between themselves and decided not to include him. This was fine. This was what always happened. Doug was the one you told things to when you needed someone to nod, not when you needed someone to act.

People drifted. Nate went down to the basement, said something about packing. Paul made a last round of the kitchen — washing the dinner dishes that nobody had finished, wiping down the counter, the caretaking that was Paul's way of grieving. Joel closed his laptop into his bag and said he was going to read upstairs. Tommy was still outside somewhere, a large, silent shape that Doug had lost track of.

The house settled into the sounds of its own machinery: the refrigerator cycling on with its forty-minute hum, the wind through the pine trees pressing against the walls, the third stair step creaking under Joel's weight as he went up. Doug sat on the arm of the basement stairs and felt the quiet accumulate. It was a different quiet than the dock quiet. The house quiet was full of people trying to sleep and failing, full of the sounds they made when they thought no one was listening — the floorboard, the sink, the muffled voice. The dock quiet was empty and that was what made it clean.

He went downstairs. Nate was on one of the pull-out sofas, phone in hand, thumb scrolling without purpose. He didn't look up when Doug came down.
